THE RED PAGE.
TO PHYLLIDA, PUTTING ON HER HAT.
V \LUE ice, blue fire, faces of blue baboons,
Blue milk, the melancholy blue of moons,
A, J Blue-apron’d butchers menacing blue files,
Blue water in blue distance of blue skies,
Blue-stockings dancing reckless rigadoons
To chase blue devils in the dismal noons,
Blue-wreathing smoke, the blue of bloomy prunes,
Blue crabs and blue turquoises, but Her eyes
Blue.
Her bluest veins loiter in kissing runes,
Lovers’ blue study since those Royal Spoons
Stirred the blue dusk of Egypt with their sighs...
And our Gythera down the harbour lies: *
“ Embarkl” She signals, for Her Hat’s (eftsoons !)
Blue .
AMERICAN CRITICS
WITHOUT approving, one may ap-
plaud Jas. Huneker’s craving to
see everything and know every-
thing, but never his ardour to tell
everything. Everything indeed is per-
mitted to be told, yet not “all at once and
all o’er.” There are formal reticences
evitable. Whitman’s offence is just th
opposite of that commonly alleged : it is no;
a sin of nudity, but a crime of clothes. I;
was not immoral to strip Adam of his fig
leaf : to dress him in that ill-cut su t
of words was inartistic. Huneker :s
one kind of American, and (with all th
differences) he repeats the kind of America
that was Whitman. His book of Iconoclas
(N.Y., Scribners; f1.50c.) collects chief!
from N.Y. Sun a dramatic critic’s observa
tions and commentaries upon modern plays
plays by Ibsen, Strindberg, Becque, Haupt
mann, Hervieu, Shaw, Gorky, D’Annunzio
Yilliers, Sudermann, and Maeterlinck
nearly all the current keynotes or topnotes
The work is seductive journalism. Huneke
has bright eyes, good tastes, and occasiona
flashes of phrase : contact with a mint
that has traversed so much is titi -
lating. If he would shape and clarif
the inchoate, instead of throwing article
holus-bolus into covers ! Ibsen, for example
is lost in descriptions of his plays, overlaic
with criticism which is not Huneker’s. Th
much better notice of Maeterlinck is stil
less lucid than it might be. But, all faults
reckoned, the book is readable and valuable
by Australians who may never see the plays
which are its subject: it gives glimpses or
what the real world is thinking and creating.
Huneker is no dull peddler of formulas : his
pages are sown with suggestion—not neces-
sarily to be agreed with, yet suggestion, good
to be made or re-stated. Even his crudity
or uncouthness :
We are all snobs of the Infinite, parvenus of
the Eternal:
might not jar if he led up to it as Emerson
could lead. The devotion of second-rate
writers to “ the simple style ” would make
literature an intolerable desert; and even
outrage is better than monotony. Some
fragments :
Shaw is like a' pianoforte virtuoso whose fingers
work faster than his feelings.
Gorky, with all his moral anarchy, is as super-
stitious as a moujik. He shakes his fist at the
eternal stars and then makes the sign of the cross.
D’Annunzio is satisfied with sheer shapes of
beauty, [yet] in each of his plays we assist at the
toilette of a woman’s soul.
Wagner, whose marvellous soul changed colour
every moment, like one of those exquisite flying
fishes which paint the air and waters of the
tropics....
Huysmans, with his omnivorous eye
To every man his illusion.
Maeterlinck’s is the dramaturgy of the subcon-
scious.
Why should admiration of the atars ex-
clude enjoyment of such fireworks ? Seen a
little closer, the stars would be fireworks too.
■J*
The Eng. edition of Iconoclasts , published byT.
W. Laurie, Lond.. at an equivalent price (6s. net),
points a current copyright argument. Laurie’s
book, printed from Scribners' plates, is in all
respects of facture inferior to Scribners’: the
paper is not so good ; the binding is shabby by
comparison ; the gold titles upon the copv sup-
plied are stamped unskilfully and askew ; and an
end-paper has been pasted down on the author’s
acknowledgment (430)—defacing the book. Yet
Laurie’s bookselling agents might clamour for an
import duty levelled against the American edition
—to the manifest loss of Australian buyers.
Dramatists of To-Day, by E. E. Hale jr
(N.Y., Holt & Co., 81.50 c.) comes con-
veniently for comparison. Hale avows
paternity from The Dial, most sober of
Amer. critical reviews; and his difference
from Huneker might be the difference of dial
from sun. Huneker is in the dramatic
orb and whirling with it, warm with
its heat and confused . with its light :
Hale, set rather coldly aloof, reflects
and registers dutifully. His subjects are
Rostand, Hauptmann, Sudermann, Pinero,
Shaw, S. Phillips, and Maeterlinck ; and
bis method is so formally informal that it
seems stilted. One does not get inspiration,
but there are pondered poses to consider,
llie writer is in sadder hours a lecturer, and
this book is designed as a series of deliberate
gambols off the academic track. Unluckily
the track has a way of following the gam-
boiler and disarranging his heels. There are
a certain number of facts—incomplete,
a certain number of impressions—unexhila-
rating ; and the rest is a useful appendix,
recording details of performance or publica-
tion of plays by the authors named.
SAID THE CHEMIST
YOU know the chemist was a wizard in
the old days ? Made spooks with a
bit of phosphorus, and so on. It’s
something like that still; only he puts
the sacred mystery in a bottle and sells
it for whatever he thinks you ought to
pay. And it heals ’em, cures ’em : bottled
mystery will cure anything, with the
aid of a trifle o faith. When it misses
blame the doctor ! Lucky it’s only the
doctor that thinks of blaming the chemist.
But a doctor can be a powerful bad adver-
tiser when he likes. One doctor, attending
gently to business, can easily keep 1000
people in a year out of one chemist’s shop :
if he has anything like a practice. Why
should he ? Because he does n’t get the
drugs in his prescription. Say he strikes a
patch of fever, and gives a dozen patients
the same mixture to bring down the tem-
prature. Ten temp’ratures go down ; two
don’t. Doctor inquires : ten prescriptions
made up at various chemists’ : the two dead-
’uns make-up at Boggs’s. “Ohho ! ” says
the doc. ; “and how much did you pay?”
he asks the Boggsers. “ Eighteenpence. ”
And the others paid half-a-crown ; but
they got the drugs.” After that his red lamp
flares one message: “Danger! beware of
Boggs ! Gro to Moggs, or Loggs, or Coggs ;
but as you value your mortal soul-case, don’t
go to Boggs ! ”
Result ? Boggs’s dispensing drops dead ;
and lie thanks his stars for his proprietaries.
“Proprietaries”—that’s proprietary medi-
cines, see ? the things we shine in. Penn’orth
o linseed ; throe-hap'orth o’ glycerine,
ha porth o’ aniseed, dash of spirits, and
water to fill up a big bottle—always give ’em
a big bottle. People like a lot for their
money, especially the women : quantity
beats quality any day for selling to women.
There you are !
Dr. Theophilus McGinty’s
••' • CELEBRATED COUGH MIXTURE !! 1
An Infallible Remedy.
Obtainable only prom Ooggs & Co., Chemists,
Proprietors and Patentees.
mmS wonderful and never-failing cure for coughs,
eolds, bronchitis, asthma, and all pulmonary troubles
is the result of Seventeen Years’ Special Research by the
Eminent American Physician, Dr. McGinty, and is pre-
pared according to his Secret Formula by Coggs & Co..
Chemists, Only. One dose gives relief.
Price—Eighteenpenoe.
—Three-and-six, if you think you can get
it: depends on your advertising. Cost to
us—drugs, perhaps 3d. ; bottle, label, and
packing, 2|d. ; total, s|d. Profit, 200 oer
cent; ; anything less is robbery—of the
ehemist.
And when you ’ve got it, you sell it—or
another “proprietary” on the same lines :
liver tonic in a rich suburb; rheumatism-
cure in a poor one ; “female pills ” every-
where. People come to you for something
they’ve seen advertised, and you sell ’em
your own mixture—always better; you
guarantee that personally, or your boy does.
I expect a boy to sell at least 41. -worth of
proprietaries with every 41. -worth of other
goods. That’s a fair day’s work for a
chemist’s boy in most shops. Profit on the
other goods, perhaps 10s. ; profit on “pro-
prietaries,” 21. 15s. ; gross profit, 31. 15s.—
less boy s wages for a day, reckoned at 11. a
week. That’s how we earn a crust: without
the proprietaries we’d starve. There’s profit
on prescriptions, but not more than a fair
thing if you put honest drugs in ; and there’s
some drugs you can’t dodge without the
doctors spotting you.
Prescriptions used to pay well—not now.
In the old days we used to get a shilling for
the sealing-wax ; now some people expect
the lot for a shilling. One-and-nine and
two-and-nine are popular prices ; sometimes
three-and-nine. Anything over that makes
customers grumble. Though, mind you,
you can easily put seven-and-sixponce worth
of stuff into an eight-ounce bottle. What
chemists sigh for is something that will un-
methylate methylated spirits—take the
flavour out, you know; and save the duty.
If we could only hit on a device for selling
10s.-spirits at 305.-a-gallon, we’d commence
to grow rich. Some chemists try to grow
ricu without the device ; but doctors object.
The necessity of circumventing doctors is
very wearing on a chemist ;so it’s lucky a
good many of them are disposed to stand in
with us as far as they can safely—and as far
as we make it worth their while.
That big retail business was founded by
Scotchmen; if one of them bad been a
Jew they might have monopolised the city.
As it is, the suburban chemists hold their own.
None of them was a chemist; but they
knew how to employ chemists, and they stuck
to their business. They pushed it so well that
the business got ahead of them : then things
were exciting. They had more shops than
capital; the suburban men were fighting for
life ; and they had to find money every week.
Fifty pounds wanted—and one man threw
his buggy in ; five hundred wanted, and
another threw his house in ;—it reminds you
of the yarn in the school-books about the
artist who hadn’t enough wood for his
furnace, and burnt all his wife’s tables and
chairs to keep the pot boiling. Then they
floated a company, bought out the big
opposition, and excuse me for a moment
.. . Good-night !
NEW BOOKS
In Voices of the Desert (Lond., Eliot Stock;
3s. Qd.) Ernest Favenc. collects his bushman’s
verses from The Bulletin and other papers. He
writes roughly, sincerely, often forcibly. In
several pieces he tries to give expression to the
feelings roused in solitary wanderers by the desert
tracts of Australia. “ With its great loneliness,
its dearth of life, and its enshrouding atmosphere
of awe and mystery,” the desert, he says, “has a
voice of its own, distinctly different from that of
the ordinary Australian bush.”
To pass a night alone in the desert spinifex country is
to feel as much cut off from the ordinary life of the
world as one could feel if transplanted to another sphere.
The motionless air, so dry and devoid of moisture that a
polished gun-barrel can lie exposed to it and remain un-
touched by a speck of rust, seems to bring the stars nearer
to the earth and enhance the beauty of their rays. The
ordinary light of the moon is coarse and garish compared
to the pure effulgence of the larger planets. On a moon-
less night the coming of the morning star is heralded by
a breaking light that might well be mistaken for the
dawn. Like a cold, white flame the planet rises, and to
watch its ascent from the horizon gives a man the feeling
that he is watching the birth of a new world.
And it is added :
That the themes inspired by the desert and its sur-
roundings are more mournful than joyous, and the pessi-
mistic view of life more in evidence than the sanguine, is
but natural, for, such as these verses are, thev are desert-
born ...
In an effective piece, originally printed in The
Bulletin , the writer pictures a vigil kept by the
wraiths of
Thi3 is a good example of Favenc’s style : his
manner is vivid; his description true. Through
all runs the vein of shy sentiment which belongs
to every good bushman and is fostered naturally
by lonely meditation in the vast spaces of Aus-
tralia. In daily life one has usually to seek to
find it, yet it has a strong influence upon the
brave hearts, the kind hearts, that are met so
often on “ the outside track. ”
Seafarers, byC. D. Websdale (Syd.; States Pub-
lishing Co., 3s. 6 d ), is a story misunderstood by
the author and publishers. It is really an excel-
lent boys’ yarn, naively told by a nautical Eider
Haggard, and packed with thrilling incident. A
ship on fire ! a wreck in the ice ! two hoards of
treasure, with rubies and emeralds galore ! a won-
derful cavern ! a lovely aboriginal heroine ! a
mystery ! —the whole catalogue, with every lot
genuine. The author shows that this class of
story can be written in Australia just as well as it
is written beyond : in fact, he has written better
than most books of the kind are written, and his
generous extravagance of material is praiseworthy,
schoolmasters seeking Christmas prizes are recom-
mended to Seafarers , and schoolmistresses too. O,
and we forgot to mention the octopus, and the plan
of Treasure Island, and the really good illustrations
A J. Muir Auld—much better than the English
average. For ourselves, we shall dream of the
aboriginal heroine all next voyage. She is a
sailor’s pleasure, to be overhauled every Sunday.
Her hair was piled up in a great knot of pure shimmer-
ing gold, and her eyes, of the most heavenly blue, were
soft and tender (p. 121).
The lips were a true Cupid’s bow, and their rich carria-
;ion was reflected in rose colour on the pure whiteness of
her cheeks. The outlines of her face came down from her
small pink ears in a perfect oval to her round and dimpled
chin. Her neck, like a column of alabaster, was firmlv set
upon shoulders muscular and exceedingly graceful,' and
her fair, unblemished skin shone like——
But we won’t say what it shone like, for this
may be read by some woman-thirsty Australian
fighting flies and dust in the backblocks. No.
we would not recommend Seafarers to schools li
it were not distinctly moral. At p. 128 the
heroine says “Fish, Will ! Fish ! !”
I turned to look and saw several goodly-sized fish swim-
ming a tew yards away. Turning again, I found her
hastily divesting herself of her only garment of bird skins.
I quickly caught her hand, exclaiming, “No, Len ! No 1 I
No !! ! ”
The heroine is what our ancestors used to call a
coming lass. When she was offered sugar
she tasted the strange brown stuff with hesitation, then
seized the spoon from my hand, crying, “More, Will.
Give me more. Very good.” So I gave her several spoon-
fuls, and she ate voraciously, until I thought she would
finish our store (p. 139).
At p. 258 things are different but similar.
Impulsively I -turned to Len, and, throwing my arms
about her, kissed her full on her sweet lips.
Then I was immediately sobered by the thought of my
own temerity. I shrank away as I saw the look of sur-
prise on her beautiful face, and the burning flush that ran
quickly over her cheeks. I was almost dismayed by her
silence and wonder, but my heart gave a great bound
when in a moment she nestled nearer to my side, and
whispered breathlessly—“ Do it again, Will. Do it again.
I like you to do that! ”
And I did it again, and again and again. I simply
rained kisses on the lovely flushed face upturned to mine.
The result of all this is “three manly boys and
our one beautiful girl, almost as beautiful as her
mother ” (p. 279). But we have given the back-
blocks enough to lie awake on.
“Do it again. Will. Do it again. I like you to do
that! ” And I did it again, and again and again—
—and again and again, and again and again and
again, and again, and again and again, and again
and again and again
That is what you get when you go to sea! This
is the book to encourage an Australian naval
spirit!

“Dum-Dum,” who writes light verse and paro-
dies in Rhymes of the East (Lond., Constable ; 3s.
6d, net) is not in the first flight of butterflies : at
the outset, indeed, he beetles rather. As the
book goes he quickens and brightens occasionally.
One can get a line for h —r I—gs :
Clothed in black stockings, mystio, wonderful:
or a rhyme 5
Solace of mine hows of anguish,
Peace-imparting View, when I,
Sick of Hindo-Sturm-und-Drang, wish
I could lay me down and die :
or even a set of verses agreeably couched: “A
Happy New Year ” —and the invocation of Sairey
Gamp in a notorious manner :
By the whites of thine eyes that were yellow,
By the folds of thy duplicate chin,
By thy voice that was husky but mellow
With gin,, with the r ichness of gin.
By thy scorn of the boy that was Bmgian,
By thy wealth of perambulate swoons,
0 matchless and mystical Mdgia-n,
Beguile us with boons.
For Anglo-Indians one would grant the author
greater vogue—or even for the English. Judged
by the kind and credit of his comic papers, the
Englishman’s humour must lie nearer the mental
surface than ours : he is damper and more affect-
able, we drier and tougher, asking an edge to the
jest.
AU who had suffered fell Nature’s wrath
In the van of a nation’s onward path,
Had died in their harness, unknown, unwept.
They had dreamt, as their eyes closed dull and dead,
They had opened a track for a nation’s tread ;
They had thought, as the failing brain grew weak,
That smiling plenty ivould line each creek.
That the toiling poor would enjoy the lands
They had fought for and held with their dying hands ;
That farm and vineyard ivould grow apace,
Where they looked their last on a lifeless space.
But the western downs, where the red sun sank,
Are held in the name of a tottering bank ;
And the boundless pastures , the verdant sivard,
Are owned by a co. ivith a “ guinea-pig " Lord ;
The cities, where vice and crime cry loud,
Are rearing a stunted and pallid crowd;
And the taciturn bushman of st urdy stamp
Gives place to the loafing, foul-mouthed tramp.
And they moaned—uplifting their hollow eyes—
“ We were offered, a useless sacrifice;
For greed and plunder our blood was spent —
For laics—that are worse than the droughts God-sent.’’.
In the west a burnt-out moon hung low;
The scrub-wood ashes were white as snow,
The owl still blinked, with a drowsy stare,
And the morhiny star was <t glory there, . ,
MEMORANDA
In despair of getting “ her novel ” published, a
Lancashire schoolmistress recently committed suicide.
Unluckily, she didn’t imitate the M.L. patriot and pro-
tesbant against coloured labour, and make a magnificent
gesture for twenty centuries to look up to. If she had
accosted J. Murray (say) with “ Moritura te saluto!"
and done it on his doorstep, “the House” would have
had a legend to keep step with the yarn of Byron’s burnt
autobiography.
All publishing houses should have legends for
clients to goggle at. Some of these days a local firm will
recount with pride how a favourite author—having been
used, as he conceived, despitefully— sought out his pet
enemy at the business, and hit him in the full glare of
publicity and the eye. The turmoil was monstrous ; but
the author held his ground till a policeman came. Then,
as he walked away with the policeman—or as the police-
man walked away with him—the author grew oratorical
upon his youth, his exploits, and his misfortunes ; where-
upon the bowels or other appurtenances of the police-
man’s compassion were so moved that he told the author
to go in peace and sin no more. And the author, his
countenance shining with glorious resolve, walked care-
fully back to the place where he had left his enemy,
greeted him kindly (him expectant of apologies and
buried hatchets !) and—hit him in the eye. Our faith in
the great, the indubitable Future of Australian Litera-
ture dates from that day.
The grammatical blunders of official N. S.
Wales reading-books have been the subject of a question
in N.S.W. Assembly. The curious thing is that the
editor of the series, E. Irvine, is N.S.W. civil
service examiner: with the .regular duty of de-
tecting in examination papers errors such as
he has passed in the reading-hooks. W. Brooks &
Co., the publishers, make the weak defence that
they have faithfully followed blundering texts—it
was their duty, and the}' did ! It would have been better
to say, what is quite true, that the slips are few and
easily to be corrected, and that merits outweigh faults.
The books can be improved ; but compared with those
previously in use, or with any current English series, they
are in substance superior for Australia. The illustrations
are not always good, and the type and printing are usually
bad or badly adapted to children’s needs ; yet neverthe-
less they mark an advance.
When Education-Minister O’Conor, in his
gradual and deliberate wa.v, has established the National
Art School which he promises, he might ascertain whether
his Dept, has not brains enough to issue its own school- 1
books, and devote the profit to raising under-paid
teachers’ salaries. If the “School Paper” is to
be continued, the Dept, should issue that too.
At present, the contents are ridiculous, and the
priceisam imposition on parents, uiven the monopoly
that Education Minister O’Conor has, he can supply his
children with an Australian copy of HurpeY's young
I‘eoplc for Id. —ah up-to-date children’s magazine in lien of
an inferior repetition of a lesson-book. MiViister O'Conor
has a quarter-of-a-million Children who need educational
goods, and must buy the goods he orders. He is con-
stantly in need of money to improve the quality of his
education. Yet lie will not supply his own goods and
make his own profit for departmental uses !

HANS PETER RASMUSSEN,
A CASE AT MARRICKVILLE.
A VERY CONVINCING NARRATIVE.
The Case of
Mrs. C. COOPER.
(By a Special Keporter.)
The physical condition of a large
proportion of tho public makes con-
tinual appeal to the sympathies of the
thoughtful and kind, who are ever on
the alert to render aid where it is re-
quired. In an intervievv with our re-
presentative Mrs. Caroline Cooper, of
510. 3 h’itzroy-stroet, Marrickville
gives some information to the afflicted
which s'aould help them considerably.
Her statement runs as .follows
“My health is altogether different
now to what it was some years ago,
as there was a time when L was no-
thing but a total wreck, and now peo-
ple aro always telling me how well
and strong l look. Yes, l am not
like the same person at all. for now
1 am always contented and happy in-
stead of being intensely miserable like
1 used to be. But Heaven knows 1
had enough to make me so, for the
pains in my head were so great that
many a day l hardly knew what t was
doing, in each temple there was a
nasty beating sensation, which caused
me so much agony that often i
thought 1 would surely go mad. '
“You would welcome the arrival ol
bed-time, so that you could sleep and
forget your afflictions for a while V
suggested the writer.
“Une would naturally think so, but
I didn’t, for the simple reason that 1
could seldom get any sleep, and oh !
how those hours of wakefulness l used
to wear me out 1 Truly, when I got
tip of a morning I felt worse than if
I had not been in bed at all, and 1
always had a bitter tasto to make me
feel sack for the rest of the day. Some-
times I got so thoroughly exhausted
that it would have been impossible to
walk had I tried to, but knowing how
weak I was 1 used to stay indoors to
bo on the safe side, for when one is
like that it is such an easy thing to
meet with a serious accident. And
another thing, i was accustomed to
taking tits of giddiness, and often J
wont right off and did not know any-
thing that was going on, so you see
i had to be careful. And wasn't 1
nervous ? Goodness ! I only had to
hear a little shout or a whistle and
i d actually jump again, and it would
be a long time afterwards before my
nerves would steady down a bit. And
i used to have the same shakiness of
my nerves when i woke up from what-
ever little sleep I chanced to get,
which, by-the-way, was always punc-
tuated with the most awful dreams
that made me shudder to think of af-
terwards, and i d find myself coverjd
with cold perspiration, too. Oh : those
were nights oi horror if you like, and
i do pray that none of mine will ever
suffer in the same way. Why, my
mind was so unhinged through exces-
sive nervousness that i was atraid to
be ielt alone, and would not have
stayed in the dark for a fortune ; be-
sides, if 1 closed my eyes lor a tew
seconds all sorts of ugly faces could
be seen, and those were the kind of
hideous sights i usually witnessed in
my dreams."
“Have you any idea what caused
your head pains and other ailments V ’
"1 think it was all through receiving
a sunstroke when a girl at my home
in Goulburn, for the doctor said 1
would always suffer with my head, end
the older i got the worse it would be.
Other doctors treated me afterwards,
and they all said what a dreadful
state my nerves were in, and also
that my digestiou was as bad as it
could be; and the unfortunate part of
the whole-affair was that their phy-
sics could not help me any. -Powders,
medicines, pills galore, and no bene-
ficial result whatever—not, I can say
with truth, up to the period when i
purchased some Clements Tonic
through” a friend’s advice, and then,
for the first time during my long ill-
ness, I learned what it was to get
some substantial ease from my tor-
ments. At first l thought the relief
would be only short-lived. It seemed
too much to expect anything else af-
ter the disappointments of the past ;
but Clements Tonic soon put an end
to that pessimistic idea by making me
feel better and stronger every day, and
when l realised that my improvement
was making such regular strides, my
heart was overflowing with pre. titude.
It is only right you should know that
besides the medicines my uoctore had
ordered i had taken a large variety
of other mixtures bought from chem-
ists, and when Clements Tonic was
the only one amongst the lot that
could do mo good it is but just and
proper that the truth should be ad-
mitted. Nothing 1 had ever tried be-
fore could make me cat like Clements
Tonic did, and I assure you my appe-
tite had been very poor for months,
and the case that came to my head
after a few bottles’ use of that re-
medy was something to make me
thankful for the re*t of my days. The
pains in that quarter eventually sub-
sided altogether, and the difference in
my life then compared to what
it had been was just the differ-
ence existing between night and
dav. Continuing with Clements To-
nic T lost the burdensome feel-
ings that affected me after taking
food, and soon the windy spasms that
had often doubled me up were quite
cured. It had been my unhappy lot to
have a feeling in the chest as if i had
swallowed something hard and im-
movable —something that kept, the
breath back and almost smothered me;
but Clements Tonic did away with
that trouble, too, and also gave me
permanent relief from the pains that
used to locate themselves between my
shoulders and about the small of my
back. My eyes had always felt dull
and swollen, and 1 used to be irritable
and anxious from morning till night,
fearing that something was going to
happen ; but, strange as it may seem,
after using a few bottles of Clements
Tonic my mind became as free from
anxious thoughts as a babe’s, it gave
me a sense of lightheartedness that had
been lost to me for years, but such
could only be expected to follow after
■o much good had been realised. 1
am glad to say that Clemontsi Tonic
left nothing undone, for it gave me a
digestion that has remainecT sound for
many years past, and in regard to
nervousness —well, i hardly know how
to express myself. At all events, 1
can say that my nervous weaknesses
were soon overcome, and if anybody
suffers from sleeplessness there’s noth-
ing in this world they need take hut
Clements Tonic to make their nights
peaceful and refreshing. That remedy
succeeded in giving me perfect health,
and 1 would rather have a bottle of
Clements Tonic in the house than any-
thing else ; so would my husband.’*
“He has used it too, then V"
“My word, yes ! and thinks the
world of it. He has used Clements
Tonic and Dr. Tletcher’s Pills, and
swears by them. Well, 1 hope others
in need will read my story, so you
may publish it as you feel disposed.’*

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Exile.
I see the soft blue beauty of the long low range,
That lies in hazy distance, far away from me,
And nearer, where swift shadows lift and pass and
change,
The bronze ridge, thickly garmented with fern
and tree;
But dearer, dearer far, the enshrouding mist that
fills
My heart with thoughts of England, when the
rain is on the hills.
I see the royal loveliness of these bright Southern
skies,
The lakes of blue, where white clouds float and
drift and rest,
The gum-trees’ lace, against the sunset light that
dies
In all the golden radiance of the far red west.
But give to me the sombre cloud, and driving
sleet that chills,
And sends me back to England, when the rain is
on the hills.
I know the soft enchantment of the warm Aus-
tralian night,
The low croak in the creek, the sudden tender
hush,
The brilliant stars, too far remote in space to
light
The dark mysterious places of the lone grey bush.
But the slow drip on the iron roof my chafing
spirit stills,
And takes me back to England, when the rain is
on the hills.
0 mystic, lonely bush-land, sound now your low-
voiced call;
Spread far your wide horizons and your wide
brown plains.
Your charm of haunting sadness dries no tears
that fall,
Nor dulls the homesick longing that with me
remains.
But now and then the grey days bring the spell
that joy instills,
For I am back in England, and the rain is on the
hills.
vio.
L.
Heaven and Hell.
Vic.
Do you know what Heaven was
In a day beyond recall ?
Just a valley deep with grass,
Where white dews of evening fall,
While the sunset glories pass,
And the deep sea murmurs by.
Do you know what Heaven was ?
An evening hour—a pale-gold sky.
Do you know what Hell now is ?
Just a valley deep and green,
Memories of passioned bliss ;
All the joy of what-has-been,
Restless demons they, I wis,
Those dim ghosts of hours gone by.
Do you know what Hell now is ?
A valley green—a pale-gold sky.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
After.
After the hapless struggle and the fret of life,
After desire and hope and yearning, grief and
strife,
Earth gives sound sleep 1
There will be no more dreaming and no more
despair;
I shall not even wonder, and there will be there
No need to weep !
And Time his quiet way above me still will take,
And on me all the moving days will ripple and
break
Like gentle waves,
The glory of the summer will above me glow,
And all the traffic of the years sweep on, as though
There were no graves !
The green will surge above me in a tide of Spring,
The blossoms open to the wet sky wondering.
Silent and slow;
The winds will wander free above me endlessly,
And the familiar rain will drip and drip on me—
And I not know !
MAOBILAND.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
The Magic Tree.
In dim lands far away,
’Tween sunset and sunrise,
On the verge of Night and Day,
Where softly to and fro
The shadows come and go,
The deep Lake of Sorrow lies.
Never wind its surface stirs,
On its shores no ripples break,
And the sombre silent firs
That ring its waters round,
Stand grim and make no sound,
By that dark and silent lake.
In that lake there is a fair
Little isle that shines afar,
And the grass that groweth there
Is luminously green,
As grass in graveyards seen,
Under the morning star.
In that isle there grows a tree
Magical, whose blossoms bright
Gleam and glow mysteriously;
The splendor of its smile
Transforms that little isle
To a little Day in Night.
And the Man who tastes its fruit,
He shall know no more of grief;
He shall fear not T ate’s pursuit;
For Death he shall not care...
Alas, when I was there
The tree was but in leaf.
N.S.W.
Y. J. DALEY.

THE AUSTRALIAN, AS GEORGE REID WOULD HAVE HIM.
George Reid recently told an interviewer that Australia, in his opinion, “should frankly look to the British naval forces for its naval protection.
GEORGE REID’S AUSTRALIA N: “You know, ma, I’m. going to frankly leave you to do the fighting if there is any. Frankly, I’m just going to
lean up here and look on. In fact,. I’m frankly going to keep out of it. But I hope you’ll wade in for all you re worth."

II oii> to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead, of
officials. The person may not- be there; the official
alivays is.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 2, 1905/
CARRUTHERS, THE ANTI-IMMI-
GRATIOHIST.
Something akin to a panic struck N.S.W, and its
Government last week when they learned that
Agent-General Coghlan was taking active meas-
ures to promote immigration. Coghlan is the
first live Agent-General that the State has
possessed. His predecessors were battered and
worn-out politicians, in search of a haven of rest
where they could go to bed early, and where the
job wasn’t dependent on the precarious course of
general elections. He, on the contrary, is a man
in the prime of life, an inveterate toiler, full of
enthusiasm, and with a boundles- confidence in
N.S.W. if only its rulers weren’t quite so deceased.
He understood that he was sent to England to
ad "rtise his State, and he did it. The informa-
tion iie published broadcast was true to the letter
so far as the possibilities of the country, its
climate, its coastal rainfall, its cheap railway car-
riage, and its vast unoccupied spaces were con-
cerned ; and if he spoke rather wildly about the
land that was available he probably took it for
granted that the Carruthers Government was
going to make land available, else why send a live
Agent-General to England when a dead one would
do as well ?
* * * *
But the Sydney Evening News and the S.M.
Herald and the Telegraph took alarm at the
prospect of a rush of deluded but desirable
British citizens, for whom N.S.W., with its great
empty spaces, had neither land nor hope to offer;
and Parliament took alarm also, and the hair of
Premier Carruthers stood on end, and the Agent-
General was communicated with in haste, and
'told to diminish his irrational hustling after de-
sirable men and women. With his usual sidelong
and crab-like habit of mind, Carruthers dis-
guised his retreat with a snarl: —
He might say, however, that so long as the Common-
wealth laws remained as they were there would be no
danger of all Europe flocking here.
This, like most things that Carruthers says, was
beautifully and wonderfully irrelevant. The
immigrants whom Agent-General Coghlan was
trying to collect were of a class which is affected
by no Commonwealth law whatsoever. They
were desirable agricultural immigrants with a
little money—not under any contract, not colored,
not alien, not the kind to whom any education
test is applied. The only reason why it was
necessary to order the Agent-General to cease
offering them any large or reckless encouragement
was that, in the State which is officially muddled
by Carruthers —almost the richest State in Aus-
tralia, and with less than five people to the square
mile —there is hardly any room for new settlers by
reason of the big land-grabber. And Carruthers
apparently has not the courage, and perhaps not
the desire, to make room ; but with his usual
narrow provincial spite, and his usual dingo yell,
he pretends that he is discouraging immigration
because, owing to Federal legislation, it wouldn’t
come in any case.
* * * *
There is no question about the abundance of
almost unused land in the comparatively well-
watered regions of N.S.W., and if the State had a
Government which would force land into use, then
all Agent-General Coghlan's florid perorations
would be more than justified. Here are some
facts from the latest issue of N.S.W. Statistical
Register (1904)
North Coast District : 309,342 acres freehold, held in
areas of over 1000 and under 10,000 acres. Average area,
2100 acres. Also, 208,945 acres are held in areas of over
10,000 acres. Average size of these estates, 26,200 acres.
Out of this 578,287 acres of large estates, only 5528 acres
are cultivated, and only 170 of these cultivated acres are
to be found among the really large estates —trie ones that
average 26,200 acres each.
Lower Hunter : 818,477 acres are held in estates
averaging 2660 acres ; and a further 478,872 acres in estates
averaging 23,950 acres. Out of these 1,297,349 acres only
6394 are cultivated.
County ok Cumberland : Has one estate of 11,023 acres.
No cultivation whatever.
South Coast District : Has 213 freeholds averaging 2172
acres, and six more of a larger class averaging 14,000 acres
each. Out of the 546,574 acres included in these two lots,
7098 acres are under cultivation.
Northern Tableland : Has 1,348,477 acres divided into
freeholds of between 1000 and 10,000 acres, average being
about 2600 acres. Has also 2,373,599 acres divided into
84 areas of 10,000 acres and upwards, average of these
being over 28,000 acres apiece. Out of the 3,722,076 acres
above-mentioned 76,149 are under cultivation.
Central Tableland: Has 1,552,779 acres in freeholds
of between 1000 and 10,000 acres ; average about 2600
acres. Also 1,443,115 acres in freeholds of over 10,000
acres each ; average rather over 24,000 acres. Out of
the 2,995,894 acres which the large estates absorb of the
Middle Tableland 73,097 acres are under some form of
cultivation.
Southern Tableland: Has 2,391,044 acres in freeholds
of over 1000 and under 10,000 acres each, the average
being 2840 acres. Also has 1,785,357 acres in freeholds of
over 10,000 acres each ; their average area being 19,620
acres. Out of the 4,176,4Q1 acres which comprise the big
estates of the Southern Tableland, 81,090 acres are under
cultivation.
Riverina : Has 1,295,266 acres in freeholds of from 1000
to 10,000 acres each, the average being- about 2338 acres.
Also 7,454,054 acres in freeholds exceeding 10,000 acres
each, the average holding being close on 55,000 acres.
* * *
These districts include the only parts of N.S.W.
which are worth much for agricultural settlement,
and on them, while the present system lasts, there,
is very little room for agricultural settlement.
The big land-grabber has also got the bulk of the
western slopes, though he hasn’t troubled the dry
far-west plains to any great extent; The number
of freehold estates exceeding 10,000 acres each in
area was 36 more in 1904 than in 1900. The space
which they occupied was 782,414 acres more than
it was in 1900. The fragment of cultivation on
these big estates was 41,455 acres less than it was
in 1900. And it was largely by reason of
the blockade which the big estates set up against
settlement that the population of forty-seven
counties showed a decrease between the census of
1891 and that of 1901—not merely far western
districts, where the drought drove population
back towards the coast, but also such counties as
Buller and Gresham, on the northern coastal
slope, Hunter and Roxburgh, between Bathurst
and the sea, Murray, on the Monaro tableland,
and Kennedy, Gregory, Jamieson and Napier, jill
lying no further out than Nyngan. Then there
are cases of other counties not far from the sea
where the increase of population varied in ten
years from one human being to about 100. It is
impossible to find much space for even the most
desirable farming immigrants under conditions
like these. The Carruthers Government must
either shift the big landlords or discourage the im-
migrants, and it prefers to discourage the immi-
grants. Butit is characteristic of Carruthers that,
while the practical stoppage of immigration dates
back 13 years, and the gradual depopulation of
various counties where the big land-grabber rages
exceedingly, can be traced back at least 14 years,
he should excuse himself by throwing an ill-
mannered gibe at a Federal Act which is only
three years old, and which has never interfered
with a single agricultural immigrant in any way.
The great landholders of N.S.W. not only retard
the growth of population, block agricultural
settlement, repress manufactures, banking and
commerce by preventing the arrival of new cus-
tomers, keep the railways unprofitable by pre-
venting the growth of population along them,
reduce the revenue by keeping away new tax-
payers, enhance the burden of the public
debt by preventing its distribution over a
larger population, and endanger the country by
preventing the increase of its defenders. In addi-
tion to all these sins they do not cultivate the land
themselves, neither do they rear the sheep and
the ox and the cow with half the energy that a
more closely-settled population is capable of.
Even England and Scotland can show almost as
many sheep as N.S.W., and far more cattle and
horses. There is no intense cultivation in any
sense about the big landholders. And instead
of doing anything serious to shift them, the
State policy has consistently aimed at making
any process of removal by repurchase as ruinous
as possible. Country lands have been exempted
from local rates. Their owners pay at a generally
lower rate to the support of the State in land-tax
than the town dweller does in income-tax.
have had their public works provided for them
out of loans, and their produce carried for them
below cost on the railways. All these bonuses,
privileges and exemptions have helped to boom
their lands to fictitious values, and to make
any process of voluntary purchase for closei
settlement ruinous, and compulsory purchase
under present conditions just a little removed
from ruinous. The result is that, while
N.S.W. has been talking for years about
promoting agricultural immigration, as soon as
its deluded Agent-General takes it at its word
and starts to really promote a rush of popu-
lation it is driven to give him peremptory
orders to reduce his exertions, and ease off in
his patriotism, and get back into the old rut
of stagnation. And certainly there is no room for
anything save a patient dwelling in the venerable
rut till the great estates in the eastern district are
broken up. Just now there is some kind of a
farce in progress which consists in buying up a
property here and one there at an exorbitant
price, while other great estates are growing up all
the time to undo the little good that is done.
That process can’t be taken seriously. The need
is for a graduated land-tax so heavy as to set all
the bloated properties disintegrating simultane-
ously, and to stop for ever the process of land
accumulation. If that were supplied, and the
money raised by the special tax were devoted to
the compulsory purchase of properties to hasten
the process of settlement, New South Wales could
more than live up to Coghlan’s enthusiastic
descriptions.
NATIONAL DEFENCE.
So far, the reception given by the Australian daily
press to the proposals to re-organise the Common-
wealth defence system on a democratic militia
basis has been encouraging. The Age warmly
supports the scheme ; and the Age has
always been the most national of Australian
dailies, and lately has been the most demo-
cratic. The Daily Telegraph sourly con-
demns it, and criticises, with a tough disregard
for the truth, its main features. Between these
two attitudes the other papers are grouped, mostly
leaning to the favorable. All unite in attaching
to this citizens’ movement for adequate defence
the importance which it merits. And the move-
ment, inaugurated in Sydney with the help of
such widely diverging men as J. C. Watson,
Julian Salomons, T. T. Ewing and W. Mac-
millan, has spread to Melbourne, where a branch
has been formed with the warm benediction of the
Prime Minister. Characteristically, G. H. Reid
for a while stood aloof, wishing to make quite sure
that the idea was popular before he said “ yes ” or
“no,” or Yes-No, and then, when he saw most
political capital on the “no” side, he said “no”; but
otherwise the leading public men of the Common-
wealth seem to be with the National Defence
League, which has good reason for feeling satisfied
with the progress already made with an agitation
as yet barely a month old, so far as organised
effort is concerned.
* * * *
The Bulletin, has no difficulty in falling into
line with the National Defence League, for what
that body proposes this paper has advocated for
many years. The Swiss military system com-
mends itself in all essentials as the model form of
military organisation for a nation which requires
a defence force at once effective, economical,
national in spirit, and peace-securing in tendency.
In details, it will probably be found susceptible to
a good deal of modification where practically
applied in Australia, for the Australian population
is generally of a quicker intelligence than the
Swiss, and is also, from its love of the horse and
of the gun, possessed already of some of the chief
qualifications of the soldier. But the main prin-
: ciples can be unhesitatingly accepted—the pre-
liminary training of the school children in physical
drill and simple military exercises ; the subsequent
compulsory and universal training of all the able-
bodied manhood in such a way as to make the
citizen a fairly effective soldier without taking
him away for any lengthy time from his occupa-
tion or his family; the dispensing with all gold-
lace and flummery, and with all caste distinctions
as regards the choice of officers; the permanent
maintenance of an adequate instructional body of
such scientific experts as may be necessary.
* * » *
Such a system would be effective, since it would
train all the manhood of the country to the use of
arms, and an invading force can meet nothing so
formidable as a nation in arms. The last ounce of
military efficiency is taken out of a people when
its every citizen is a soldier. The system would
be economical, since there would be no expensive
maintenance of an idle standing army—the per-
manent soldiers would be confined strictly to
members of instructional staffs—and no withdrawal
for any length of time of citizens from industry.
The system ’would be national, since it would
—with an Australian navy—provide a self-respect-
ing and self-supported insurance against attack,
doing away with the present humiliating and
dangerous dependence on Great Britain. The
system would be peace-se-curing, for it would offer
to an enemy contemplating invasion the most ter-
rible of menaces and would be the best of securi-
ties against Australia entering into outside wars
of aggression since, with every citizen a soldier
and equally liable for service, there would be a
marked disinclination to vote for wars not abso-
lutely essential. It is sometimes, in a democracy,
found easy to get people to scream for a war in
which they know they will not have to do any
fighting. Under a universal militia system the
man who votes for the war also fights the. battles,
and he wflll only vote for conflict when it is sternly
necessary. With a caste of professional soldiers,
segregated from the rest of the community, taking
no part in its home life or its industries, there is
always an agitation to enter into war for any
reason or no reason at all. War alone can give
them relief from ennui, and hope of advancement.
War therefore is their constant hope and to win
war they riVe sometimes prepared to intrigue
against a restraining Parliament. A citizen
army has none of those disadvantages. At once
effective, economical, national, and peaceable, the
Swiss militia system commends itself on all points
to Australian needs and sentiments.
* * * *
This Commonwealth wants no pomp and
heraldry of w'ar, no bedizened and gaudy relics of
barbarism to lead it to the fight if ever the time
comes for the country to stand on its defence.
Australia wants no perpetuation of caste distinc-
tions requiring that the officer shall be a “ gentle-
man ” (i.e., a loafer) and the ranker a ‘'common
person” (i.e., a worker). Nor does this debt-
cursed continent want an expensive military sys-
tem based on that of England, which purchases
the least amount of efficiency for the greatest
amount of money. But we do want an adequate
organisation to train the population, and local
factories to arm the men so trained —a plain
brown army which has robbed no fowl of its tail
and no curtain of its tassels ; which can march or
ride, and use a gun, and pour forth “as the sea to
the whistle of death pours its sweeping and unript
waves ” when an enemy touches our home soil.
An army to tight, not to flirt ; to be formidable,
not fashionable. That is the need of Australia ;
and that, we take it, is the aim of the National
Defence League.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Swinburne on Denmark.
Minister Swinburne, of Victoria, lately sym-
pathised at some length with the butter people of
his State, and told them, among other things,
that while it cost 295. per ton to carry butter 102
miles, from Bendigo to Melbourne, the State rail-
ways of Denmark conveyed the pale cow-sub-
stance the same distance for 9s. 4d. a ton. And
then, just when he might have explained how
Denmark managed to do it, he changed the sub-
ject. Here are the points which Swinburne
missed :
(1) Denmaik owes only £5 3s per inhabitant—not £42,
like Victoria.
(2) It has been steadily paying- off its debt for years.
(3) It has no dead debt. Revenue-producing- public
works represent all its debt and more. Therefore its
railways have very little interest to earn.
(4) Denmark in every way discourages large estates.
It is all cut up into small farms, and is closely peopled
and closely cultivated. Its trains, in getting from one
place of importance to another, don’t have to run unpro-
fitable' over huge wastes belonging to persons like Ohirn-
sidb, where there is no business to be done. Therefore
there is no great mass of unprofitable lines spreading-
through a landlord-made and landlord-cursed wilderness,
and nobody’s butter has to be specially taxed to pay in-
terest on the cost of such dead lines.
(5) In fact, the whole story of Danish agricultural
prosperity is told thus in Mulham/S “ Industries and
Wealth of Nations,” pag e 233 : “ The reform of land tenure
has been attended with the most astonishing transforma-
tion. In the 18th century Denmark consisted of 614
estates, belonging to the same number of noblemen,
ranging from 10,000 acres upwards. On each estate there
were 200 or 300 tenants, called bondsmen, whom an
English traveller described as “ dirty, devoid of energy,
and not so well-fed as Jamaica negroes.” ... In 1808 a
reform was introduced by Frkderic VI., which compelled
the nobles to sell farms to their tenants at £6 an acre, and
an official return in 1840 showed that in 30 years the
peasantry had bought up half the kingdom. Those who
had not bought farms were termed huusmen, or tenants
whom the law protected by enactment that the landlord
could neither raise the rent, nor evict them so long as they
paid it. In 1861, Bishop Mourad’s law gave still further
facilities to the peasantry for the purchase of land.
For 56 years Denmark has made it illegal for any
man to enlarge his estate by buying up his neigh-
bor’s property, and for 97 years it has had com-
pulsory land purchase and compulsory subdivision
of large estates. It is especially solid on the right
of the tenant to buy out the landlord. It is this
system which has made Denmark the very richest
among all the countries in the world which depend
mainly on agriculture. It is a pity Minister
Swinburne didn’t finish his story ; he was just
coming to the interesting part of it when he broke
off. And meanwhile Swinburne’s chief, Premier
Bent, has a scheme for lending money to a few
unemployed to enable them to settle on small
patches of dubious soil, while he leaves the big
land-grabber absolutely alone. And he calls that
adopting the Danish system of land settlement.

Trafalgar Day.
Trafalgar Day is dead and gone now. The flags
have been taken down ; the bottle-oh has come
round to the back door for the dead marine ; the
cigar ash has been swept away ; the white-washed
wooden image of Nelson, with an upward look in
its eye and a white-washed telescope in its hand
and a petticoat of flags round its waist, that stood
in front of Sydney Exchange, has been removed ;
and the “ patriot ” who sprained his throat yelling
over Trafalgar has bought himself a j ujube. There
was much bellowing and more than enough drink-
ing, but amid all the celebration only one man—
Captain Cresswell, naval commandant — came
conspicuously to the front with the remark that
imitation is the sincerest form of flattery, and
that the best way to honor the great naval
commander is to get ready, so far as our means
will admit, to do likewise when the case calls for
it. Certainly Cresswell had logic on his side.
A community which maintains not one serviceable
war vessel of its own should be ashamed to
shed so much fuss and feathers about dead
naval heroes and dead naval glories—it should
rather conceal itself in the cellar or the hen-house
on Trafalgar Day, and avoid observation. Also a
Jingo party which shouts so much about “the
empire” as the Australian Jingoes do, and which
doesn’t call on its .country to build so much as
half-a-dozen cruisers, so that it can lend the
empire, or its part of the empire, a hand when
necessary, ought to flee into the scrub when a
naval anniversary comes round. And a White
Australia party, which isn’t shamed by the know-
ledge that, in the absence of any naval defence of
our own, Britain may, in some future war, dele-
gate our protection to its Asiatic ally, should be
so stung by the knowledge of its position that it
would cheerfully raise £20,000,000 by taxation to
remove the stigma. No amount of cash contribu-
tion to the British navy can supply any guarantee
that this country won’t see its ocean defence some
day handed over to the Jap, who will assuredly,
no matter how much we pay to the British navy,
demand free admission to Australia for his
brown multitudes as a reward for his assistance.
This country’s easy-going legislators generally
shirk the naval defence problem by saying that
Australia is too small a community to support a
navy that will be of any defensive value against a
first-rate naval Power. So it is—at present. But
that is no reason why it shouldn’t support a force
strong enough to meet the fleet of a second or
third-rate naval Power, or to meet any fragmen-
tary force which a first rate Power, when engaged
in war with Britain, might spare to raid Australia.
Neither Maoriland, South Africa, Canada, Ire-
land, Scotland, Wales, Lancashire, nor Kent
could individually support a fleet strong enough
to fight a first-class naval Power any more than
Australia can do so, but that is no reason why
they should all sit down and let things slide. At
all events, if Australia is resolved to do nothing
in the matter of navil defence, it should celebrate
no more British naval anniversaries, unless it be
with a crape weeper to signify its sorrow that it
is so unlike Nelson. The contrast is a painful
thing, and ought to be buried.

Prime Minister Deakin is Waiting.
Prime Minister Deakin has stated that, just as
soon as the States amend their land laws so as to
provide land for desirable immigrants, he will find
the desirable immigrants. Which is so like Deakin
-—the affable procrastinator, the man who is always
lingering in three columns of polished eloquence for
the course of events, the person who is going to do
this after that happens, the refined loafer and
gentlemanly waiter upon Providence. This country
doesn’t hire Deakin to wait on events, but to
make events. His Government can start break-


ing up the big estates and providing land for
settlement in a wholesale fashion by imposing a
heavy federal graduated land-tax and thus making
great land monopolies impossible. It can hurry
the process by applying the proceeds of the gradu-
ated land-tax in each State to the purchase of
estates, and can itself cut them up for settlement.
Then when it gets ahead of the local demand for land
it can get the High Commissioner to send out desir-
able immigrants. Alfred Deakin is appointed
and paid to be the guardian and* upholder of the
Federal power. If he set to work to do the things
that the miserable State Governments leave un-
done, and to make the Federal power stand for
progress and energy where the State authority
stands for slackness and stagnation, he would
make the Commonwealth a strong power and
generally respected—a power with infinite capa-
city for good, and one that would overshadow the
petty, snarling State authorities for all time. As
the individual who is always waiting on the States
he is making the Commonwealth for which he
once worked so hard a mere shadow—something
like the ancient, homeless German emperor who
was arrested for non-payment of his meat
bill. If Alfred Deaktn were an absolute
and premeditated traitor to the Federal principle
he couldn’t play the part better than by this con-
stant waiting for the pleasure of the States, this
making of his policy subservient to Carruthers,
this hanging round till Bent is ready, and this
polite subservience to old sea-captain Evans.
The world respects strength and the success that
it brings. The reason why the Commonwealth
has so far commanded only a limited amount
of respect is that it has hid for Premiers a tired
man, a dreadfully polite mild man, a painfully
moderate man, and a greasy wriggler.

Smashing Our Industries.
The Yankee Harvester Trust has ample con-
fidence in the shortness of the public memory. A
few weeks ago it was swearing volubly that to
accede to the demand of the Commonwealth agri-
cultural machinery manufacturers and raise the
ridiculously small duty against the pirated imita-
tions of the Australian harvester, would be to
penalise the poor farmer. The Yankee Trust
. would have to raise the price of its piratical pro-
duct ; the Australian manufacturer would surely
follow suit ; and the poor farmer would groan
under a burden of extra taxation. After
this had been solemnly declared a few
dozen times, and the Freetrade newspapers had
pointed out its beautiful and irrefragable logic—
the law of gravity, we were assured, wasn’t a bit
more sure—the duty was raised. The Yankee
importers were forced to pay on an honest invoice,
and the old duty of £5 4s. 7d. per machine climbed
to £8 2s. 6d.—an increase of over 50 per cent.
That had been done barely a month when the
Yankee Trust came out with an advertisement
(which has appeared in the columns of The
Bulletin) announcing a general cut of £l2 10s. in
the price of its harvesters. So the effect of am
increase of 50 per cent, in the duty has been a cut
of £l2los. off the price, and following the principles
■of the beautiful irrefragable logic of the Free-
trade newspapers, the increase of the duty by
200 per cent, on the old rate—to a specific sum
of £25 per machine—would bring the price
■down by £5O, or to £2O per machine. There is the
strongest of temptations to the Australian Parlia-
ment to try that method of reducing prices by in-
creasing tariff charges. It has acted very promptly
and efficaciously once; and, when on a good
wicket, score quickly ! There is another aspect of
this price-cut that shows the Yankee Trust’s
cynical faith in the public’s short memory. Last
month this offshoot of the great Rockefeller octo-
pus took Australia into its confidence with a
published balance-sheet showing the gross and
the net profit on the Harvester machines it sold.
This document—which professed to be exactly
true gave the profit on each machine as
£3 3s. Bd. at the rate of duty calculated on the old
faked invoice, and at ss. 9d. on the new invoice.
Now the Trust makes a cut of £l2los. in its prices.
Clearly either it lied when it represented its profit
at ss. ()d., or it is now selling at a loss of £l2 4s. 3d.
per machine. If it was lying about the ss. 9d.
profit, the Trust puts itself out of court for ever as
a business enterprise with any claim to being
treated as an honest trader. is likely—the
present quoted price (showing on the Trust’s
figures a loss of £l2 14s. 3d. per machine) is a
“dump” price, at which the Trust is willing
to sell only until it is able to knock out
Australian competition and add to the 90
per cent, of the world’s agricultural machinery
now in its tentacles the remaining 10 per cent.,
the fact should prompt the Australian Parliament
to immediate action to save the Australian indus-
try from extinction and the Australian farmer
from falling into the grasp of this monopoly. The
methods of American Trusts —Oil Trusts, Beef
Trusts, Steel Trusts, Harvester Trusts—are
familiar enough. This Trust will spend scores of
thousands of pounds, if necessary, to sweep
the Australian manufacturers out of its path.
Having done that, it will bleed the Aus-
tralian farmer mercilessly. Where it has spent
thousands it will suck back tens of thou-
sands, and Australia will pay for its remissness
with not only the destruction of its local indus-
try, but also the grinding of its farmers in
the mill of monopoly. It is generally recog-
nised that during the present session the
Australian Parliament will not be able to
generally revise the tariff—there is no one with
the courage and originality to suggest that it
should put in the Christmas season bringing peace
and goodwill to- the Australian artisan —but it
should at least decide before proroguing to put up
the bars against this American Trust, which has
so clearly shown that its idea is not competition
with, but destruction of, a valuable Australian
industry, and the consequent removal of prac-
tically the only barrier the world has against the
great agricultural machinery monopoly.

The Cable Combine.
Senator Pearce’s sub-clause in the Copyright
Bill, by which the press cable combine is compelled
to admit any new journal to the same privileges,
or lose its copyright in the cables, stirred up the
“great dailies” considerably. The Argus, with
a great show of frankness, admitted the existence
of the combine, tried to justify it, and published
what looked like a fair report of the Senate dis-
cussion on the question. Hansard, however,
shows that this apparently fair.report was a shuffle
of the first order. The clause was originally car-
ried on the voices, after a discussion in which
the argument was almost wholly on one side.
Senator Pearce read a letter from Mahon (of the
House of Reps.), in which Mahon gave his own
experience of the combine. He was running a
paper at Menzies (W. A.), and was getting a cable
summary from a Perth daily, in return for a sum-
mary of district news, no money passing either
way. Then the combine stepped in, and Mahon
was compelled to sign
a three years’ contract with the syndicate, at £l5O per
annum. This contract bound me to use their cables
•exclusively. . . . But something even more intolerable
was to follow. The syndicate insisted I must take the
full service, which involved the payment of one and six-
pence per hundred words on reams of messages which
were absolutely useless, and for which the paper had no
space.
Mahon wired to the Argus, and then interviewed
Syme personally, but to no effect, and ultimately
he had to close down at a heavy loss. Senator
Story followed with an interesting account of the
experiences of a proposed new Adelaide daily,
last year. The secretary wrote to the A Rous re
cables. It referred him to the Register and
Advertiser. These were both written to, and at
first replied individually—though to the same
effect —that they would consider the matter. At
last, after pressure, a reply signed by the two
papers arrived to the effect that
the joint inter-State agreement regulating the service
contains a clause which says “that no message shall be
supplied to the proprietors of any newspaper in Sydney,
Melbourne, Adelaide, or Brisbane, other than the news-
papers mentioned in this agreement, without the unani-
mous consent of the parties.” . . . In confornfity with this
obligation, we have communicated with our partners in
the other States, and we find that it is impossible to
obtain the “ unanimous consent ” provided for.
After Story had read this correspondence, it was,
of course, no use denying the existence of an ex-
clusive combine, and the sub-clause was agreed
to without a division. The letters quoted formed
the gist of the discussion, but the Argus\ report
makes no mention, of them. At the instance of
the Telegraph and the Argus, a desperate at-
tempt was made to recommit the Bill for the
purpose of eliminating the sub-clause, but the
motion was defeated, though only by one vote.
The Labor vote was solid for the clause, while, as
usual, the Tory crowd voted with the combine.
Whether the clause will pass the House of Reps,
is doubtful. Deakin is still a great deal too
affable.

Parting of the "Ways.
[For Th b Bdliktis.]
Faded now the glamor of our “splendid isola-
tion ” ;
Gone the old convictions that have ruled our race
so long—
Shibboleths and standards of a stouter generation;
End traditional England, self-sufficient, grand
and strong.
Ends traditional England that is England now no
longer.
Propped and held in station by her Asiatic shore ;
Songs of valediction swell out strong, and ever
stronger :
“We, her bartered children, hail her Motherland
no more.”
Dream of “ Greater Britain ! ” Let us end the
specious story !
Here we face you, England, at the parting of our
ways—
We, who are your children, and who gloried in
your glory,
We, who aped your customs, and who hoped to
wear your bays.
Here we quit you, England, for you may no far-
ther guide us
Through our misty mirage to a goal none under-
stands.
Share your tainted triumphs with your Monkey,
and divide us :
“ Buddh for Jappy England ! ” but “ St. George
for Austral lands ! ”
Tender your asylum to all seedy, swarming
strangers—
Red, black, brown, and yellow, and the non-
descript they breed.
Hug your “ Colored Problems,” and degenerative
dangers ;
Futile would be protest, for we know you would
not heed.
Cut the Christ-God’s crosses from your Judas-
branded banners;
Graft Gautama’s imag'e in the corner-space thus
won ;
Make your mottled millions mostly Mongoloid in
manners.
Red o’er your grey waters portents Nippon’s
Risen Sun.
Futile would be protest ; but your lead we shall
not follow.
Not for us an Empire through the path you seek
to beat.
We, whose ears are open, hear it ringing, oh, so
hollow !
We, who know its pitfalls, dread its end for heed-
less feet.
Looms for us a future with a prospect saner,
brighter
Than you peer at, England, mammon-bossed and
fetish-fed !
Now we found a nation to be cleaner and much
whiter.
Here we hold a refuge for the straightly-thorough-
bred.
Walling in a temple for the cult we preach and
cherish :
“Men are kindred only whom the claims of blood
unite.
Only pure blood prospers, and the mongrel man
must perish ;
Since but caste is lasting, let the caste that lasts
be white.”

Our Enemy the Jap—Again.
Once more is the air more or less filled with the
cry of Bruce Smith and the yelp of Pulsford—
the two renegades who are always against Aus-
tralia and always for the nigger,' the Chinaman,
and the colored man generally—demanding the
free admission of the Jap to this country. These
persons have very much to say about our restric-
tions, but nota word of condemnation for therestrie-
tions by which the Jap elbows white men—includ-
ing the subjects of his British ally— out of Japan.
The Mikado’s country, with no Immigration Act
and without a language test, yet in a left handed
fashion absolutely forbids the immigration of
white people. The land laws of the Monkey’s
country are the most remarkable in the world for
the rigidity with which they refuse to allow a
white man to obtain a title to even one small acre.
The rule is that no one but a Japanese can own
land in Japan. There is in all Japan one white
man w r ho owns land, and he is the exception that
proves the rule. He went from Richmond (Vic.)
30 years ago, married a Japanese wife and had a
large family. He had influence with certain
Cabinet Ministers, and got a little island down
the coast. But no other white man had his luck,
i his law against ownership of land by white men
affects the business relations of the whites and the
yellows. If a Jap owes a white man money, the
white can sue him in a Jap court, and he may get a
verdict, but that’s all he ever does get. The
Jap’s house isn’t worth seizing, and his land the
white man cannot touch. There is mighty little
to be got by an alien who can’t own land, and he
can take no permanent root in the country. This
land law and the restrictions put on aliens as
traders constitute an exclusion policy which, if
judged by results, is brilliantly successful. At
latest advices (beginning of 1904) there were only
13,709 foreigners in Japan, of whom 7423 were
Chinese, 2113 British (largely tourists), and the
rest various. Australia contains more Japanese
than Japan contains of British subjects of all
sorts, and the “proud and sensitive” Jap nation
isn’t at all ashamed of its exclusion policy—not
even of its anti-English exclusion policy. Japan
treats Englishmen a score-of times worse than the
Transvaal ever did, yet Britain quarrelled with
the Transvaal over the latter State’s anti-foreign
policy—tiie alleged anti-foreign policy of a coun-
try which allowed the foreigner to own its mines,
its best city, and much of its land, and only drew
the line at letting him run its Parliament. When
Britain fell on the Transvaal, Pulsford and Bruce
Smith said it was well. And now, for the
moment, the hysterical Bull is as enthusiastic for
the Jap who kicks him as he used to be against
the Boer who treated him better than any other
foreign nation ever did; and Pulsford and B.
Smith, being each of them only a copy and a mind-
less echo, would fain have us give Australia to the
Jap in return for his elaborate process of insult.
Meanwhile, as part of the great conspiracy
wherein Bruce Smith is a humble blue-bottle
buzzing on the cart-wheel, and Pulsford the dog
which runs behind in a cloud of dust, a certain
“Viscount” Hayashi has issued his assurance
that there is no risk’or intention or possibility of.
any large Jap movement towards this country.
The demand for entrance, it seems, arises merely
from the lofty spirit of a “proud and sensitive
nation.” When the Jap is allowed to come here,
he won’t come. It is a good lie—but other coun-
tries have listened to the glozing tale of the
Hayashi gort of person, and in every case they
have been sold. The Jap practically owns
Hawaii now. There were 24,000 of him in’97;
there are 70,000 now. The Hawaiians have
diminished nearly one-half in a mere handful of
years—eaten up by Jap competition. The Jap
outnumbers the natives, the Chinese and the
white Americans, and is driving them all out.
The Jap is starting to eat up New Caledonia so
fast that an Exclusion Law is contemplated. He
is pouring into Western Canada, and is even
beginning to invade Mexico and Central America,
and everywhere he goes he is the locust—the same
old locust. The assurance of Viscount Hayashi
or the Duke of Haystack or any other polished
Jap statesman may be a very good assurance, but
facts are against it. Australia was just beginning
to realise the huge possibilities of a Jap influx
when it made its feeble effort at closing the door.
It had noted the proud and sensitive Jap in all the
brothels from Cairns and Cooktown round the
Gulf to Kalgoorlie, and it knows things that B.
Smith, poring over his occasional brief in his
office, or Pulsford, mooning over his statistics, is
incapable of realising.

The Great Subdivision Sale.
Sydney papers are again daubed over with the
poster-advertisements of the subdivision sale, and
the community is besought about 10 times every
Saturday to come and buy the desirable suburban
block whose beautiful views never can be built
out. And the man at the street corner takes up
the song of the daily paper, and says it is all a
glad sign of returning prosperity and restored
confidence, etc. The Bulletin, however, regards
this resurrection of the subdivision salesman with
mixed feelings. The boom means that there is
money about, and that’s a good thing. There is
money about. Pretty well every quarterly
banking average and every bank’s balance sheet
proves it. In this respect Melbourne is the
same as Sydney. The report of the Royal Bank
of Australia, issued last week, showed that its
liquid assets —the coin and the public stocks that
it has lying in its own vaults or which, unable to
find other use for, it has lent to other banks,
mostly in Europe—have increased in 12 months
from £261,957 to £499,367. In N.S. Wales, while
the advances have in some cases increased, the
deposits have increased much more rapidly, in
spite of the fact that the rate of interest for
deposits has been reduced. One result is a long
jump in the market price of investment stocks.
Haying nothing else of a profitable sort to do with
their money, some people are rushing the things
that look reasonably safe, and the rush has
whirled up market prices. Thus, though the
tendency of banking profits is to sag, because
the deposits which are costing the banks money are
outrunning the advances which are earning money,
some bank shares have sprung as much as 15 per
cent, during the last three or four months ; while
N.S.W. Funded 4 per cents, for which, in March,
the best offer was £9B 10s., now have sales at £lO2
15s. In other directions the movements have
been still greater. The man who bought Broken
Hill silver shares 12 months ago can now, on the
average, sell out at a profit of 50 per cent.—in
some cases over 100 per cent.; while Melbourne
has been excitedly putting its spare cash into tin
ventures. This is just the chance that the bank
or the mortgage co., with the hideously unprofit-
able vacant land on its hands, has been waiting
for; it wants to pass on the unprofitable baby
while the public has money to buy unprofitable
babies with—or, anyhow, enough money to pay a
deposit with, and so reduce the amount at
which the infant stands in the mortgage co.’s
books. What the public intends to do with
the block of land it buys nobody knows; the
public doesn’t know itself. Mostly, perhaps, it
expects a bigger boom to happen by-and-bye, and
then it will pass the child on to someone else to
nurse for another few years. And all this is doing
absolutely nothing to develop “our great natural
resources.” All it is doing is to provide a few
auctioneers’ commissions and the chance for mort-
gagees to creep out of debts that have been in the
Doubtful Debts ledger for years. Meanwhile all
this money that is either accumulating unused in
the banks, or is passing from hand to hand among
speculators, or is being shipped to England
because, while it cannot earn anything at all here,
it can earn per cent, there, is badly needed for
the development of Australia. Last month alone
the one State of N.S. Wales imported from
foreign parts £1,492,324 worth of goods, and for
the first nine months of the year £11,000,104
worth. To the foreigner, its bill for iron and iron
goods has this year already come to £327,970,
wjth another £529,827 for machinery, and another
£792,829 for metal manufactures. Practically
all these things could and should have been
made in Australia, by Australian factories, pro-
vided out of Australia’s surplus capital. But
Australia supports not only the foreign workman
and manufacturer, but the foreign bank as well.
While its own deposits either lie unprofitable or are
shipped away to Europe, the foreign deposit in the
foreign bank earns good interest by helping to do
for us the things which G. H. Reid, and Cook,
and Johnson, and Kelly, and the rest of the
Foreign-trade crowd won’t let us do for ourselves
—and the party that sinks the fiscal issue stands
by and says that the tragedy of workless Austra-
lian workmen is none of its business. To a great
extent, therefore, an extra-good harvest, fol-
lowed by an extra supply of money, means
one of two things. Either the man who
owns the money can take it to an Australian bank
which, unable to find any profitable use for it
here, sends it to Europe, where it finds employ-
ment in cutting Australia’s throat industrially ;
or he gambles at a great suburban subdivision sale
or in stocks and shares, so that his capital doesn’t
add one cent to the wealth of the country. It is
a- stiff price to pay for the fun of regarding George
Reid as a political saviour and Joe Cook as a
deputy saviour.

The Blessings of Yellow Labor.
The Palmerston and Pine Creek railway is a tell-
ing example of the sort of prosperity Australia
would have if it only let in plenty of cheap
labor of assorted tints. It is the only railway
in the Northern Territory, and serves a country
that never has had any scarcity of yellow labor,
and which, at the present moment, has nearly three
Asiatics for every white inhabitant. Moreover,
the N,T. is known to be one of the richest
parts of the continent, and specially suited to
the colored brother. According to all the rules
of the Bruce Smith school, the N.T. should there-
fore be a country of beastly affluence, and its
public works should be showing a return
so large as to prompt every other State
to follow its example and become likewise varie-
gated. But the dreadful fact is that, so far from
anything of this sort resulting from practically
unlimited cheap colored labor, the figures are so
shocking that the S. Australian Railway Depart-
ment never prints them with its own. In that
respect it only adopts the practice of almost
every S. Australian Department; the figures for
the Territory are so bad that they are kept apart
from the rest, for fear of spoiling the average.
As for this Palmerston-Pine Creek line, it
was opened in 1889. Its capital cost was
£1,179,059; in the 16 years of its life it
has cost £270,309 to work, arid the revenue
has amounted to £236,845. Thus to pay mere
w’orking expenses the line has had to be subsi-
dised to the extent of £33,464, and, in addition,
the whole £814,563 of interest has come out
of the pocket of the S. Australian taxpayer. The
Territory, with its practically unlimited yellow
labor —the labor that is supposed to enrich the
land—has had to call on S. Australia to pay
nearly £4 for every £1 it has paid itself ; and yet
S. Australia is a poor country, with not half the
mineral resources of the Territory, and, according
to the yarn of the colored-labor crowd which is
always booming “ tropical agriculture,” with not
half its other resources. As an object lesson in
the salvation of a country by letting in the Asiatic,
the Northern Territory, with its proportion of
nearly three Asiatics to every white person, ought
to be simply invaluable. And as a hard, concrete
example, this case of the Palmerston-Pine Creek
railway ought to be nailed up on Australia’s door-
post.
