Cats.
Bombala (N.S.W.) has secured a Government
proclamation protecting the domestic cat “ which
has become wild,” the expectation being that the
animal will solve the rabbit question. It may be
admitted at once that such cats will kill young
rabbits. Even domestic cats, before becoming
wild, will do that. But to declare that the domestic
cat “become wild” is “a natural enemy of the
rabbit ”is an absurd blunder. The grown rabbit
and the grown cat, tame or wild, fraternise
after a very short acquaintance, and that
acquahntanceisalwayspossible where food abounds.
It is not cats but settlers that the Bombala district
requires to put down its bunny. This writer has
explored part of that district on foot with a knap-
sack, and this is the verdict. Cooma to Berri-
dale (about 20 miles), no population, all great
squattages, grass, rabbits, scenery, but hardly even
any sheep or cattle visible. Berridale to Timbery
Range (about 26 miles), no population, and few
sheep or cattle visible—only grass, scenery, rab-
bits and monopoly. A few small selectors at Tim-
bery Range. From their bit of cultivation to Bom-
bala (about another 26 miles), no population, no
houses except the deserted homes of old-time
dummies, very few cattle or sheep, much
grass and fresh air, Bombala to Delegate (20
miles south), much the same over again. Bom-
bala back to Cooma by main road (about 50
miles north), two hamlets, the rest squatters, land
monopoly, grass, scenery, and very little visible
stock. This writer knows where there is room to
settle 100,000 of an agricultural population on
Monaro, with reasonably good soil and an English
climate, and these people would keep down the
rabbit, and indirectly provide support under a
decent tariff for another 100,000 or 200,000
tradespeople, artisans, and others. But the
Carruthers Government prefers to let the
big land monopolies remain, and to promote
a rush of cats in the great wilderness. Even
when it sees a prospect of the Australian
Government building a city on this grassy deso-
lation and putting the land to some real use, it
prefers cats.

Britain’s blindness to her military weakness is an
old story, but her deafness to the voice of Lord
Roberts— the glorified “ Bobs ”of not so long ago
—is a new and surprising fact. The ex-Com-
mander-in-Chief, who realised the absurdity of
Tommy Atkins in the Boer War, has been vairily
attempting to raise money, for the creation of rifle
clubs. He appealed to the municipalities and
Jingoes in general for a total sum of £lOO,OOO, and
the response was £lOl7. Meanwhile the average
Englishman, ignorant of the use of modern fire-
arms, and proud of his inability to hit a haystack
at thirty paces, continues to believe himself equal
to six Frenchmen when he’s roused. But poor old
Roberts can’t rouse him.

K HE IS V; S ;, W - Government has put a sensible Bill
through the Leg. Council dealing with money-
enders; under it, no money-lending transaction
Mill hold in a court if the interest or charges are
excessive. If Moses Isaacstein (whose name is
or ten It benuzer J ones when he’s a worse Mo. than
usual) has over-reached himself, the court may fix
matters up in what it regards as an equitable way.
ihe b.M. Herald says that the protection is very
badly needed (it had to say that, because this Bill
so tar is a copy of a Bill which the blessed British
House of Parliament has passed), but that, of
course, it will only make things worse for some
borrowers, because the lender, having now to take
oxtra risks, will demand extra flesh. He will, in
short, get his extra bit of flesh when the matter
oes , n . q 6 *' mto court, in order to counter-balance
the bit that the court will take out of the scales at
other times. Having thus landed hard-up
humanity in the mud, the S.M. Herald passes on
r r* e next subject. Probably there’s a good deal
of hard-up humanity that will get in the mud and
stay there, no matter what is done for them ; but,
for the rest, what’s the matter with public pawn-
shops—municipal or State ? The appalling rate
of interest now charged is to a great extent the
result, not of tremendous risks, but of the tremen-
dous number of harpies whom the borrowers have
to support. Cheap loans are impossible, because
each lender has to make one small transaction last
him so long in bread, onions and cigars—and even
bad cigars cost some money. If the S.M. Herald
is certain that the private money-lender will only
be more of a harpy because of any law to protect
his customer, then, as the Herald says the cus-
toiner ought to be protected, obviously the only
way is to abolish the private money-lender. Or,
at any rate, give the customer the option of going
to a public money-lender, who won’t fleece him,
and won’t have to make one small transaction
go so far.
Some extracts from an Adelaide Advertiser
article on the Northern Territory, by James
.Robertson :
It is the business street in Port, Darwin, and contains
some 20 or 30 shops or stores. Only one was inhabited
and kept by a white man ; the others, of all sizes and
descriptions, being owned and run by the übiquitous
Dhow. I subsequently found that there was a small hotel
kept by whites in that street, and two other hotels, a
®® u P* e streets away. These with two, or it may be
three, stores kept by whites, comprise the business estab-
lishments of the city of Palmerston, excepting- the two
solicitors’ offices, two banks, and the office of the
-Northern Territory Times. .... The European stores
were punctually closed on the Saturday afternoon, and
religiously kept closed all day Sunday. John does not,
however, content himself with trading on the Sunday, but
in the most open and glaring manner invites all and sun-
dry to step in and take a hand at dominoes, fantan, or
one of the many other gambling games incessantly kept
going. All this in the main street, with open doors and
no restriction. John also supplies his own countrymen
and blacks with as much grog as he is asked for. . . ,
When once a blaekboy or a gin acquires a taste for opium
(and they do so in an incredibly short time) they are body
and soul under the control of the wretches who supply
their craving for the drug. Every threepence, sixpence,
or shilling, the gin or boy may obtain, is at once traded
with the-Chinaman for opium,. The victims are, mostly
between their spells of semi-unconsciousness or recovery,
at the beck-aryl.call of the Chinaman, who treats the
poor wretches in the vilest and most disgraceful manner.
It is distressing to see the young gins and big-framed
olack boys, so completely and so surely being “wiped out ”
in this way. . . . John is supposed to be debarred from
holding mining leases or mineral rights. He gets over
that difficulty by simply making some derelict white man
a small partner in his show, in consideration of the
said white taking out licenses in his name and giving John
written acknowledgment of the fact. The gold that the
nominal lessee obtains as his share is absolutely the only
portion of that taken out of the ground which lis not at
once sent off to China, there to be hoarded bv the sender's
relations until he goes back to his native country to claim
it. A good deal of misapprehension seems to exist about
the Chinaman as a miner. My experience has gone to
prove that he must be about the worst miner in the
world ; at any rate, he is the worst I have yet come
across. He never prospects. During the whole time I
was in the Northern Territory I never heard of a China-
man going out prospecting. What he does is to root
about old workings at or near the surface. He will not
put in a drive more than a few feet, unless it is absolutely
unavoidable, and then it is with fear and trembling. He
sinks small shafts just about large enough to crawl down,
opens it out a little when he gets down to the wash or
reef, and then starts another hole, perhaps 10 or 12 feet
away. Thus his mining is absolutely useless for further
exploiting, and. generally, the shaft or hole caves in
shortly after he has ceased to use it.
When the No. 3 Supply Bill was before the
Senate, a surprising outburst of Toryistti took
place. Senators Pulsford and Simian Phraser,
as well as Gould and Millen, got off huge masses
of eloquence about various subjects, and most of
them were angry about the White Australia
policy. Simian Phraser poses as an authority on
land ; and, as he owns some big slices of the con-
tinent, he ought to know something about the
matter. In his eagerness to defend the squatter,
he made the following remarkable statement:—
I could name many men who left large estates which
have since been broken up by their families. Take the
case of the Robertsons, of Colac ; the Manifolds, and the
Blacks. Mr. Neil Black died years ago, and his estates
have all been out up.
As a matter of fact, the very reverse is the case,
except as regards the Robertsons. Both the Mani-
folds and the Blacks have actually added to
their holdings since the death of their fathers.
When Phraser uses the terms “ broken up ” and
“cutup,” all he means is, that the owners have
let their land, at high rentals, for dairying pur-
poses, and have thereby doubled and trebled
their incomes. One of the Manifolds has sold a
few of his farms, but the other two are very keen
landlords; and one has become famous—or
notorious—as “Penny Manifold,” on account
of his system of paying his share-dairymer.
only a penny per gallon for their milk. Tin
Black brothers own all that their father left
them, and have since bought two more stations •
This monstrous grabbing policy is what old Simiaj
balls breaking up large estates.
The . Victorian Chamber of Manufacturer; is
striking at what is perhaps the main root of the
prejudice against Australian goods. It borrows
the use of suburban town halls for “matinee”
demonstrations of Vic. manufactures in their
various stages of development, and it dedicate s
these object lessons to ladies. President Atkins
and.sundry sistern of the Advance Victoria party
address the Sex on the local industry subject, and
discuss the exhibits. Those ladies for whom
shows are intended are the women who, as a rule,
have no local tradesmen in the family, and have
never yet looked at a factory with interested eyes—
women who may have employed a parlormaid for
years, without troubling to know that Mary has
half-a-dozen brothers engaged in making goods
which she (the missus) never buys—because they
are “colonial.”
Puck ” on the defence question :
tj ,' 1 o 0 deal has been written for and against General
Hi iton s scheme of defence organisation, but so far its
most ridiculous feature has escaped comment. His aim
was to create a mobile field force, capable of undertaking
military operations in foreign countries, and for that pur-
pose he designed six brigades of infantry, each brigade to
form a military unit complete in itself, with its comple-
ment of artillery, engineers, &c., and able to take the
held independently at the shortest notice. That being
his object, no geographical obstacle was allowed to stand
hHe way. State boundaries are recog'nised in the cases
of \ ictoria and N.S. W., but Queensland, Tasmania,
S.A. and W.A. are expected to mobilise their troops
in joint brigades as soon as war is declared. The
3rd Infantry Brigade, for instance, consists of a regi-
ment from all four States, artillery from Queensland,
i A., and Tasmania; no engineers; and a field hospital
from Queensland and S.A. The W.A. Light Horse belong
to the S.A. brigade ; and the Tasmanian to that of Vic-
toria. General Hutton’s theory was, no doubt, that
transports would collect the scattered fragments of his
brigades from each State, and dump them down for
mobilisation at some foreign port. Whether such a
system would, even in that case, result in efficient brigades
is an open question ; but for the simple defence of Aus-
tralia it is difficult to conceive a more ridiculous arrange-
ment. For example, W.A., on declaration of war,
would be expected to send a regiment of in-
fantry and a battery of field artillery coasting
round all Australia to Brisbane, the brigade head-
quarters ; would see its only regiment of Light Horse
shipped off to Adelaide ; and would be left with an army
of 1155 men for its own defence. This is the scheme that
met with the approval of Big.tohn Forrest, the first
Minister of Defence, and has since been endorsed by each
successive Minister. To be effective, and to provide for
efficient Australian defence, the present brigade organisa-
tion must be entirely swept away, and a new system
adopted which will take the defence requirements of
definite geographical areas into special account in the
arrangements for mobilisation. The subject must in
fact be treated as an Australian one and from a
national standpoint, without any special regard
for the imaginary boundary lines which constitute the
separate States, with their separate populations. It will
be interesting to see whether Senator Playford and the
new Council of Defence will have the courage to formu-
late a sensible scheme with this in view, or whether the
Imperialistic influences of the late G.O.C. are still strong
enough to prevent any radical reform in the military
organisation. ,

The Westralian elections are not completed yet;
but it is already evident that the Labor party has
gone down, and great has been the siiikage
thereof. It has lost eight or nine seats in
all. It has lost its new leader, Johnson,
of Kalgoorlie. The small Independent group,
which gave a general support to the late
Labor Ministry, has lost its leader, Moran,
and has been considerably damaged otherwise.
The one-time Labor leader, ex-Premier Daglish,
has come back again, but as a renegade and an
Independent. He secured a majority of 79 at
Subiaeo, which is at least 49' more than the
majority that Judas Iscariot got when he was
elected in Matthew xxvi., 15, and 81 better
than the luck of Peter when the rooster
beat him by two. All the same, it is worth
remembering that only a year ago, before
he backslid, Daglish’s majority at Subiaeo was
1417. Premier Rason who is practically a relic of
the old Bigjohn school of thought, seems now to
be firmly seated, but apparently Rason is going in
for a further policy of borrow and boom and burst,
and under these circumstances it rests with the
Labor party whether his reign will be long or
short. It is no use looking back on the past
misfortunes, which were numerous, or the past
blunders, which were mostly the work of the ill-
chosen leader; this is now the time for Labor
to glue its eye on the future. It lost its previous
chance for want of a vigorous policy, but if it gets
a vigorous policy now the opportunity will come
again before long. But the party wants one
or two new principles—above all the anti-bor-
rowing principle, and the anti-deficit principle,
and the fixed conviction that it must provide for
public works out of revenue, by hard economy
and by taxing absentees and monopolists. It
wants to beware of the fatal fluency of Tommy
Walker, and to convince the people that the next
Labor Premier won’t “mark time” like the lym-
phatic Dagli.sh, but will go ahead from the moment
he attains to office, and keep going. The late
Labor Ministry was doomed from the moment
when Daglish settled down comfortably, like a
moss-grown precedent sitting on a tomb, to enjoy
the dignity of his billet and do nothing in particu-
lar except look moderately beautiful. The people
of N.S. W. can appreciate the position in which he
placed the Westralian Laborites. They are
accustomed to seeing the local Labor party
doing almost as little as Daglish did.
If your muscles ache it may be your blood
—Wolfe’s Schnapps cures rheumatism. If you
need a bracing tonic for general exhaustion.
Wolfe’s Schnapps is the best in the world.*
Horse racing, as a rule, is in sore need of
purification. Its morals want washing. But those
members of the V.R.O. who visit Daly’s new
Turkish Baths in the Royal Arcade, Melbourne,
have at least a love of external cleanliness which
must assuredly permeate to their inner consciences.
After a healthful two or three hours at Daly’s
Turkish Baths a man feels good. The improve-
ment in his body has brought virtue to his mind.*

The “Merely Mary Ann” stage story,
told for the first time in Australia at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday night, has thus
nothing very strikingly original about it. It dif-
fers from the others in that Zangwill has told it in
bright, sin art dialogue. Sometimes, in fact, the
dialogue'is too smart—Zangwill helps along his
oWn reputation at the expense of his characters.
He makes Mary Ann say maladroit things, for
instance, that even the unsophisticated slavey
wogld baulk at. But these are trifles. Taken as
a whole, and with some reservations concerning
the artificial last act, “Merely Mary Ann” is
well-constructed, with some simple, telling
climaxes, and is bright all through.
* * * *
Miss Titkell Brune finds in it one of her
best parts ; 'in the early comedy section, before she
has any notion of ever being anything but merely
Mary Ann, she is strikingly successful. But most
interest in the production centres in Douglas
Gerrard, the newly-imported Launcelot of the
story. He is excused because on Saturday he had
a swollen arm ; an insect had found out the new
blood. If, however, the insect was wholly
responsible, it was a very powerful beastie.
It made him stiff and stagey on first night,
and never really convincing. Possibly an insect
has bitten Launcelot, too; anyway, that young
man, though nominally the hero—Mary Ann’s
hero— lS often a prig and sometimes a cad. On
the other hand, Albert Gran’s Peter (Peter is
Launcelot’s chum) is at times too boisterous;
Peter is a boisterous person certainly, but Gran
keeps him blowing a steady gale. Apart from
that, it isn’t often that principals are so well
supported. Miss Susie Vaughan’s Mrs. Lead-
batter (Mrs. Leadbatter has in her time kep’ two
husbands eatin’ their heads off, and now keeps a
boarding-house) is a low-comedy performance that
is worth the money in itself ; and Miss Irby Mar-
shall doesthe best work she has yet accomplished as
the simpering Rosy Leadbatter. J. B. Atholwood
makes but a brief appearance as Brownson, a
German-Jew music publisher, and that can be

LITTLE BOY: “ Who killed him?"
IMPORTER CAIN: “Am I my brother's keeper? ”
bracketted with the other very good things.
Herein, by the way, Zangwill gets home on the
Gentile ; for Bruwnson spends most of the little
time he is on the stage imploring the Gentile to
take money, and voluntarily adds a fiver to the
pay for the music of a new “ popular” song.

“Thunderbolt,” Miss Duggan, and the
other philanthropists will give up bushranging at
Sydney Royal on Friday night; on Saturday
‘‘The Mockery of Marriage” will begin to teach
some great moral lesson that isn’t quite apparent
on the surface at present.
* * iff *
The bushranger dominates the jungles ancl
forests of Sydney. It is not safe to go into the-
city these night.; with your gold watch and chain ;
hanging out of your coat-tail pocket. If youi
leave a bag of sovereigns on the footpath outside;
the Criterion theatre whilst you go into the
nearest bar to drink a bottle of cold tea,
it’s a bad banana to a thousand pounds
that the money won’t be there when you come
back. An emissary of Captain Starlight has
taken it, of course. After a good run for its
money, “Robbery Under Arms” goes off the
Criterion stage this week, and will be replaced by
“ His Natural Life ”on Saturday night. Now, if
ever there was an Australian story made up of
loathing and love, and fashioned in tears and
laughter, it is this same tale of the convict days.
And the drama shows the story and so saves you
reading or re-reading it.

The second of the Holman-Mayman re-
citals was, like the first, handicapped by hideous
weather. The Holman-Mayman combination is
remarkable for its good acting, which shows long
and careful preparation, and for its beautiful
dressing. In a Japanese sketch Neville Mayman
rather overdid the part of the honorable Son of the
Bulldog Breed who did not desert the little Miss
Chrysanthemum, and stayed on to rear a piebald
family. The piebald family did not appear at the
recital. Later on, in the screen scene from “ The
School for Scandal,” the quality of the work im-
proved considerably. Mr. W. Holman played the
part of Charles Surface in a manner that will
cause his best friends to have doubts about his ;
real character. Mr. Wilson’s interpretation of
the character of Joseph Surface proves that Joseph,
was certainly no class, while Mr. May man’s.
Sir Peter Teazle was a very respectable and;
natural old baronet. The object of the Holman-
Mayman recitals is to give the gems of English
and Continental drama in easy doses, stripped of
all the padding which the unfortunate authors had
to tack on to them in order that the carpenters
might have a show to do their part of the business
without interrupting the audience. It is the age
of the short story, and the gem-scene dramatic re-
cital is in accordance with the spirit of the age.
In the meantime, Messrs. Holman and Mayman
should arrange for better weather.

Two performances of “ Everyman,” at the-
Town Hall, were not such as to set Melbourne
yearning for any future repetition of that gloom-
some allegory. One is impressed by “ Evervman ”
as by a funeral on a wet day or a visit to the con-
sumptive ward of a hospital. Some holy monks
in the 15th century are said to have built this
‘ ‘ morality ” play for the good of the people, and.
the statement as to authorship is true on the face,
of things. The trouble starts with a crude sugges-
tion of heaven in the background—the kingjitae
aforesaid being a cloudland, where God, in the
original productions, sat enthroned, with a monk
on His right hand and a nun on His left. The
Williamsonian production omits the Almighty
from the picture, but His voice is heard
calling upon Death to summon Every-
man to his doom. Here endeth the im-
proving vision of Heaven. The action of the
morality is mostly carried out in front of a
tapestry screen. Death appears, and serves his
summons upon Everyman, who misrepresents
common humanity to an absurd degree. He is
young and beauteous—a Julius Knight among
men—and he has so much health and wealth that
he can’t face Death with the fortitude of a worn-
out toiler. In his distress he seeks the support of
various abstracts—Fellowship, Kindred, Beauty,
Property, Strength, Discretion, etc. All of them
dislike the idea of going to the dim Hereafter with
their young friend. They either beg to be excused
right away or they jib at the sight of
an alleged grave in the closing scene. Only
Knowledge and Good Deeds can go with him.
Knowledge is a tall commanding lady who stands .
for all he knows about Church dogmas seemingly,
whilst Good Deeds is a young nun. Unfortunately
Good Deeds can’t stir hand or foot until Every-
man has got absolution from a priest, but he meets
this difficulty by confessing his sins, and doing
penance, after which his funeral commences.
Thanks to the Church’s kindness Good Deeds and
Knowledge are able to see the hero through to his
destination, wherever that may be, or rather they
say they will accompany him, but as all the lime-
light is reserved for Julius Everyman, the ladies
leave him to be buried alone. Good Deeds slinks
behind the tapestry and Knowledge remains where
she was. A pretty musical angel and an invisible
chorus provide a suitable climax. The Church has
saved Everyman’s soul. It can’t be that he goes
to the innermost Heaven where only monks and
nuns are admitted, but he joins the angels in the
neighboring atmosphere. The story is better
acted than spoken by the Williamson co., none of
MERELY MARY ANY.
She came from Somersetshire and her name was Mary
Ann—
Merely Mary Ann—
And her farther and her mavvther both were dead ;
Likewise little sister Sally had measured her life’s span,
So Mary Ann was left alone to battle for her bread.
Excepting' brother Thomas, but she “never mentioned
he ”
A wicked fellow Thomas who had fled across the sea,
Leaving Mary Ann a slavey in a hoarding-house in town,
Where Israel Zangwill found her with a cap upon her
crown—
Merely Mary Ann,
Of the boarders there vvas Launcelot, a man of genius,
Who wrote music, classic music, and had a mighty soul;
But in the chase for publishers he always missed the ’bus,
So financially this Launcelot was mostly in a hole.
He’d a baronet for father, and his body oozed with pride ;
So in no one save the slavey would proud Launcelot con-
fide—
>•'" She was merely Mar^'Ann.
He confided in a lordly way, as she polished up his boots,
And they mutually decided music publishers were brutes ;
Till Mary Ann was Smitten in her simple’ childish way ;
And he talked of pigs and elm-trees and the pleasant
month of May,
And a cottage in the country for himself and Mary Ann.
But a wedding didn’t figure in the genius’s plan—
She was merely Mar}’ Ann.
Oily Deering as a vicar next appeared upon the scene—
The vicar of the village where this Mary Ann had been—
With the news that brother Thomas had left this world
of care,
After striking oil in Texas, and had died a millionaire.
He had died a lonesome Thomas, and to Polly left his pot.
But she didn’t, want the lucre, only wanted Launcelot—
Didn’t want to change her duster for an ostrich-feather
fan,
Wanted only to continue his devoted Mary Ann—
His own merely Mary Ann.
But the vicar patted Polly from her long-haired
Launcelot,
Whereupon he gaye up classics, and he wrote much
tuneful rot,
Till he likewise rolled in riches. But he’d lost his
Marion,
And he hadn’t any notion where his old-time mash was
gone ;
Till he found her in a mansion—she was soulful, cultured,
sweet ;
And of course he threw his genius and his fame at
Marion’s feet ;
And the marriage that aforetime couldn’t possibly occur—
Well, the obstacles had vanished with the long-lost
millionaire.
But Marion discarded her silks and feather fan,
And said if he would have her it must be as Mary Ann—
So he married Mary Ann.
The moral of the story : If your name is Mary Ann
You can mount to fame and glory by a very simple plan ;
Give the well-born boots of genius an extra-shiny shine,
And your brother Tom who’s something in the Texan
struck-oil line
Will die and leave his dollars to his sister Mary Ann.
Each Mary Ann’s an heiress,
A prospective millionairess
If she’ll only follow out this very simple pleasant plan.
She’ll be Mary Ann no longer, she’ll be Lady Marion,
With no awful missus watching that she doesn’t “ carry-
on.”

** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s a more than
merely passable performance of “In Town was
achieved by The Players last Saturday, and will
occur again until Friday—six nights in all. Re-
garded as a first attempt by an almost entirely
amateur force, and compared with The Players’
best work in serious comedy, this treatment of
“ In Town ” settles any doubts concerning White
Australian talent for the stage. It is a talent of ani-
mation, alertness, dancing, singing, and audacity.
The Australian girl is not a mistress of quiet effect.
When there is nothing in particular to do she
usually fails to do it very well. Her amateur brother
actor is even more lacking in the arts of mannah
and repose. But when they undertake a frivolous
piece and launch out into variety business they
show the natural national capacity for sport.
The girls dance charmingly, and with remarkable
spirit, in “In Town.” Miss Addie Hine, as Flo
Fanshaw, is a prettier artist than was the im-
ported “ Gaiety ” danseuse of yore in the same
part. Miss Irene Dillon, as the Call Boy, is
equally clever on her young feet, and the
Folly Ballet, by eight willing damsels,* strikes
one as being about the swiftest thing Melbourne
has seen. Miss Malyon is a successful prima
donna in all respects except her make-up, and
among the other long-frocked ladies the only one
who looks very ill at ease is Miss Gray. She
makes some amends by singing creditably enough.
Lawrence Hardinge plays Captain Coddington
with plenty of dash, intelligence and rough-edged
humor. Harry Quealy (long time of Pollard’s
Juvenile Co.) is an extravagantly foolish Rev.
Hopkins. Miss Henningham wears the trousers
of Lord Clanside, and Hal Cohen gets the difficult
part (for a young amateur) of the elderly Duke—
and.doesn’t shape at all badly in it. The orchestra
fully meets the case, likewise the scenery by
George Upward, junr. But the noblest item of
the hotel scenery is citizen John Dane disguised
as the German commissionaire.
On Saturday of this week Nellie Stewart
starts a farewell season at the Princess’s; an
absolutely final last season previous to her sailing
for the land of the Stars and Trusts. “Sweet
Nell” will set the ball of adieux rolling. One or
two previous revivals have been limited to a single
performance, with the result that huge masses of
public were turned away from the doors, so this
next revival is billed for six nights and a matinee.

* * X- *
At Melbourne Royal, ‘ ‘ The Betting Book, ”
undamned by the play-bill’s faint praise, “ power-
ful as a sermon,” drew a vast congregation on
Saturday. There are many Wesleyan blessings
in store for berrother Bland, but, apart from “ The
Book’s ” anti-tote, anti-boolde sentiments, it con-
tains much that is interesting. Australian types
and situations are used exclusively, though most
of the time the treatment is “quite English, you
know.” What price, for instance, the revels of
the station-hands, on the occasion of the chris-
tening of the squatter’s infant son ? Sutton
Vane writes up this scene in the best British
manner, and we have Union shearers ordering
themselves lowly and reverently towards their
betters, and proffering in the innocence of their
good, simple hearts, gifts such as shaving-mugs,
etc., to the monthling babe. On the other hand,
one gets occasionally a whiff of something more
like the true eucalypt, as in the episode of the
fascinating bank clerk who marries the rich squat-
ter’s sister, backs horses, and “does the funny biz.
with the bank’s beans.” The importance of the
bank clerk is a peculiarity of this country. So far as
the characters are concerned, weak points are the
villains, whose sinfulness, aimless but untiring, is
made the most of by Styan and Charlie Brown
respectively. The latter, billed as a Hebrew
bookie, belies the cute bend of his beak by tamely
tagging at Styan’s heels through one unprofitable
felony after another. Melodrama builds too much
on revenge. Off the stage the sinner safeguards
his skin first and smites his enemy when he can
do so in perfect safety. Sutton Vane and co. de-
pict the unprovoked avenger as boring his way to
the scaffold by inches. Foiled in an attempt to
abduct his victim’s wife one day, lie barefacedly
returns next morning to tempt the-victim' himself
to put the penny he stole from his starving child
into a sweep, or to rob and murder the rich kins-
man, who dozes in the intervals of raising the
victim’s clan from poverty to affluence.
* * * *
“ The Betting Book ” is strong in stage
pictures. The Tote Shop scene, said to be the
facsimile of the “Collingwood Curse” culminates
in the thrill of an unsuccessful police raid. A
steeplechase, with live horses in the leading roles,
is another feature. Bland Holt himself is much to
the fore as a footballer, “ pug,” jockey, and general
laugh-fakir. Mrs. Bland plays well up to her
comic colleague and looks her part. Walter Baker
as a rollicking squatter rollicks for all he’s worth,
and Miss Harrie Ireland is effective as the
gambler’s decorative and devoted wife. Max
Maxwell scores as the plunging bank-clerk, while
his stage son and heir is represented at different
stages of growth by three different child-mummers.

The circuses by Princes Bridge are blow-
ing their trumpets as before. Melbourne could
have been happy with either were t’other dear
circus away, but the presence of two at once is
not a matter for complaint. Moreover the rival
advts. are exciting literature. Wirth Bros, lay
special stress on the Demon looper of the Death
Trap and their herd of elephants, whilst the
Eroni brethren call attention to their accumulation
of talent in the arena. Wirths’ show is indirectly
assisted by the appearance of their pet Demon at
Princes Court, where he cycles down a steep
incline and goes bounding over an empty space
before he bumps the second section of the track.
Which suggests that he is practising at the Court
in readiness for a possible hitch in the Death Trap
at the circus.

According to the
woman's page of S.M.
HERALD last week: “ When the
Romans came to Britain they found
all the inhabitants living in one room."
A pack of fashionables spread over Prahran
Town Hall on Friday night, when “Lady Hunt-
worth’s Experiment” was perpetrated by a
set of fashionable amateurs. Mrs. Fred Payne
and the one swell Levey family of this State
(where the children of Israel are not in any social
prominence) ran the show, and sold pasteboards
at a good figure. The mumming was not as bad
as it might have been. Miss Marjorie Levey
(whose debut recently made a flutter in the
Toorak neighborhood) played the pretty in-
genue. Miss Beatrice Norman whipped up
the eggs for the mayonnaise, just as a divorced
aristocrat of blameless reputation would whip
them up. A person of loose character would
have done the thing quite differently. A son
of Surgeon O’Hara, and two Strong brothers (sons
of the Australian Church parson) helped the show
along. A daughter of Bully Barrister Purves
libelled her nice self as a vinegary old maid of
angular lines. Miss Edith Appleton scored better
for a burlesque make-up as the washer-up of the
Vicarage.

A wide change of company is nearly due at
the Opera House. Le Roy, Talma and Bosco, the
lively tricksters, are departing for another place
this week with all their poultry and appurtenances.
Also the Jackson Family, and Spry, and Miss
Challis and other people will retire before an ad-
vancing host of fresh attractions.
** * *
The Stephenson Co. is finishing its season
at Adelaide Tivoli. There are no new pieces.
May Beatty skims through her parts in her usual
absent-minded manner; Edward Lauri makes
love much and often; M‘Naughton and the
brothers Bovis work hard in support. The
laughter is of that easy kind which bubbles up
at a witty joke, but boils over and shakes the
roof when a man is understood to have torn his
trousers. Maggie Stirling has come, appeared
before some deadly poor houses at the Town Hall,
and gone away discouraged. The Sunday show
is a new idea and causes unforeseen developments.
The Sabbath sportsman can do a “grand band
concert ” at the Jubilee oval in the afternoon, and
at night make choice between a “grand vocal and
instrumental concert, with bioscope ” at the
Tivoli, and a “complimentary benefit, with
phonograph,” at the circus ; which is going rather
far for churchy Adelaide.

More Irving memories :
Fred. B. Norton talks reminiscently of the late Irving.
He remembers the long, lean Henry as leading man of
John Knowles’s co., at the Theatre Royal, Manchester, in
1868 or thereabouts, what-time Alfred Dampier, Clifford
Cooper (father of Cooper Cliff, a high-salaried actor of to-
day) and Fred Everill were members of the co. Knowles
was a soulless drama-monger, who conducted his business
on tbe meanest principles, and probably paid Irvine ten
“ bob ” a week more than Dampier got for his labor.
There wasn’t much advantage in being leading- man. A
few years afterwards, when Irving re-visited Manchester,
he was asked to head a subscription in aid of the gifted
Everill, whose chronic thirst had brought him to grief.
The great man staggered the subscription-committee by
declining to put his name down. “No,” said he, “I
cannot give you anything. ... I will allow my
poor friend £2 a week for life ” —which was a kindly
way of dealing with a difficult ease. Everill
lived for not more than two or three years afterwards.
It was at Manchester in his struggling days that Irving,
Fred Maccabe and Phil Day conspired to “burst up ” the
spiritualistic fraud of the Davenport Bros., then gulling
thousands of believers at the Free Trade Hall. The
Davenports were working the cabinet trick for the first
time, and the Spirits were alleged to loosen the brethren
from the cords that bound them. Irving and co. fixed
them so securely that the Spirits couldn’t untie the knots.
Subsequently the triumvirate were induced to appear at
the Gentlemen’s Concert Hall, Manchester, for a charity,
and give an exposure of the knot trick that had put the
Davenports to flight. It wasn't in Manchester, says Fred
Norton, that Charles Dickens was” struck by Irving’s
work in “Dearer Than Life.” The drama - with
Irving in the co.—-was produced at the old Queen’s
Theatre, Long Acre (London), under the management of
Labouchere, a rather enterprising theatrical speculator
before he started Truth. Probably Labby’s obituary
notice of the actor will state what salary he got for
making his first little mark in London at the Queen’s.
And there is the oft-told story of how Irving, soon after
he made his great hit in “ The Bells,” was introduced to
Barry Sullivan by a mutual friend. Barry, at the time,
was storming to neglected benches at the Holborn
Theatre. The new “star” seemed genuinely glad to
know the old-school tragedian. “Proud to meet you,
Mr. Sullivan,” he said. The veteran replied, with lofty
politeness: “Irving, Irving—l have heard the name.
Are you playing in London, young- sir
Ambrose Pratt’s dramatic yarn of “Thunder-
bolt” has been published in book form by the States
Publishing- Co., and is now selling excitedly on the book
stalls. The “ Thunderbolt ” memoirs on newspaper pub-
lication created something- like a sensation, especially in
Victoria, where the Argus had to go back on its tracks
and reprint its earlier numbers containing the beginning
of the serial to meet the demand. The book, which
comes to the public in a very attractive get-up, is likely
to be a popular su -cess.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Died recently in Adelaide Destitute Asylum ex-mummer
Joseph Gardner, whose patchy career was at its brightest
in Australia what time George Ooppin ran shows at the
old Variety Theatre, Light Square, Adelaide. Gardner,
who died at the age of 81, came to Adelaide in 1848,
after having risen to some prominence in England as a
vocalist. Coppin persuaded him to take to the stage,
and he first appeared in “Love Laughs at Locksmiths,”
which ran for three weeks at the above-named house.
Later he went to Victoria, and played small Sh'akspearean
parts at the old Queen’s Theatre, then owned by J. T.
Smith. After a spell at gold-seeking, he formed the
Vaudeville Expedition Co., touring overland from Sydney
to Brisbane. Business was g'ood at first, but four or five
of the company were seldom sober enough to appear, and
the venture ended in failure. Gardner worked back to
Melbourne overland with “The Wizard of the North,”
playing as “ Prof. Gardner, Wizard of the Antipodes,
Comedian and Illusionist.” Afterwards he took a pub. at
Inglewood when the diggings were there, and in 1875
settled in Hindmarsh (S.A.). He turned against the stage
in his old age, and wrote a book on it: “The Down
Grade ; Twenty-five Years on the Stage, or the Life of an
Australian Actor, his Experiences and Vicissitudes.”
“ Ande ” : I found the north bank of our only Yarra
decorated with a string of red, white, and blue lights.
The boat-houses on the south bank were outlined with
red, white, and blue lights, and the house-boats on the
corner, by way of variety, were each a separate blaze of
red, white, and blue. The promoters got the Chinamen
who made the lanterns for boat decoration to paint upon
them Union Jacks and St. George's ensigns. These lan-
terns were carried on a line in the boats from stem to
stern. Originality was shown in each boat being decorated
differently—that is to say, no two boats carried the same
number of red, white, and blue lanterns. The largest
boat carried twelve, and there was a canoe on the river
without a light. About nine o’clock a large number of
rockets —fully ten—showing red, white, and blue lights
were sent up ; and a boat came down the river burning
Roman candles. These same candles are glorified squibs,
each emitting three balls (of fire), respectively red, white,
and blue. A crowd waited about Princes Bridge until
10.30 p.m. for the show to commence, and then went
home, with red language, white faces, and a general
feeling of the blues.
King Edward, by his royal desire to that effect, pre-
vented the production of a Passion play at a London
theatre after the tableaux had been designed and the
dresses ordered. The Church party is mostly in favor of
these Passion mummeries, and the Jews are just as
strongly antagonistic to them, on the g'round that the
New Testament story of the Crucifixion is a monstrous
Christian libel on the Chosen people and priests of the
period. It looks as though King Edward was influenced
by King Cohen.
“E.”: Regarded as a dramatic sermon for up-to-date
people, the Morality play “ Everyman ” is open to the ob-
jection that it insists upon the awfulness of a mortal’s
decease, as though Death were a punishment for the crime
of getting Born. On the other hand, “Everyman” may
be complacently accepted as a relic of the dark ages, for if
it one time expressed a popular dread of the inevitable in
Nature, it now prompts the miserable sinner to thank
God that the times are more comfortable than they were
when religion held the whip over common sense. Fear of
Death is fear of Hell and other after effects which the pro-
fessed experts in Futurity pretend to know' all about.
Disbelieving in the old Hell and blazes, a reasonable
person declines to shudder at the thought of dying.
“Everyman” appeals only to the shudderers.
Florence Challis is a sufferer from gastritis, and has to
endure the starvation cure. She puts much heart and
soul into the “ Old Bull and Bash ” ditty, but it is cruelty
to ask a starving girl to sing about a bull —the animal the
beefsteak comes from.

“N. 8.”: “Everyman,” recently revived by J. C.
Williamson, was turned into English only after the
Buddhist original had passed through several other
languages, including Dutch. English playwrights W'ere
just as fond of “adapting” in the fifteenth century as
in later times. The cleric w’ho compiled the English ver-
sion tried hard to impress audiences with the importance
of the clergy. Everyman is assured by Five Wits :
“ Everyman, God gave priests that dignity,
And setteth them in his stead among us to be ;
Thus be they above angels in degree.”
Evidently a barefaced attempt to crib precedence from
the poor unsuspecting angel, which can’t give evidence on
its own behalf, or even make a statement from the
dock.
“J.A.C.”: I used to know recently-deceased tenor,
William Parkinson, very well. He never w'as, as “Thorold
Waters ”(B. 26/10/’O5) says, “ a big man in his day ” ; he
was a g'ood bit above mediocrity, and that is all. His
success w'as entirely due to tw'o or three ringing high
notes ; he could at no time in his career have been classed
as a thoroughly artistic singer, and he was a very poor
actor. In one respect, how'ever, he was remarkable. For
years he travelled the provinces of the United Kingdom,
singing every night in the week, and sbme w'eeks a dif-
ferent opera each night, and I never knew him to he out
iof form. He was a great stayer. Even when he was in
Melbourne he was very fit for an old ’un. I met him once
in Milan, about 30 years ago; he had come over on a
three weeks Cook’s excursion ticket to study Italian
methods. That amused me. We frequently took our
meals together, and he would confidentially tell me that
he didn’t “ think these Italian chaps knew too much”;
and firm in that conviction he w'ent back much re-
freshed. lie was then lessee of the Preston theatre,
Lancashire.

“ O’E.” : When Brighton’s unparalleled farcical trage-
dian, Tom Bent, climbed on to the stage and made a
speech during the interval at a suburban performance of
“ Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” the other night, he was obviously
worried about something. I believe he had arranged to
play the part of Little Eva, but it was found at the last
moment that the wires on which that sweet child is sus-
pended during her apotheosis would hardly bear the
weight of Little Thomas. Hence the tears of one who
would give at least three silver coins to see Thomas, in a
golden wig and a diaphanous nightdress, standing on
tiptoe on a purple cloud.
“S.T.S.”: Australia seems to be a little mixed over
“Comedy and Tragedy.” Some people write as if it were
new or thereabouts ; a Bulletin writer (19/10/’O5) calls it
“one of Gilbert’s early effects in dramatic work.” It
isn’t either. It was written in 1884 (about the same time
as “Mikado ”) for Mary Anderson, then at the height of
her fame. I saw her in it, and, as she played it, it was
magnificent. All Gilbert’s best non-musical work was
earlier than this. “ Pygmalion and Galatea,” “ Palace of
Truth ” &c. ; in fact he has done nothing since that will
live excepting opera-librettos.
An Australian girl has found a new use for appendicitis.
She had a drab billet, with a commercial firm, and wanted
some excitement for a change and incidentally two
salaries.- So she knocked off work for a couple of days, then
bravely pulled herself together and returned for a day, after
which the boss got a letter to the effect that his faithful
servant had acquired the fashionable complaint and had
been sent to the hospital. A week later the kindly boss
made some inquiries concerning her health, and discovered
that if the young lady had appendicitis she was still bear-
ing up bravely, for she was shaking a vigorous serio-comic
leg with a vaudeville co. up-country. The young lady
knows now that you can’t serve the gods and Mammon,
and she probably shakes her vigorous leg along the streets
in quest of a new job.
R. G. Knowles, of the English halls, had a four-page
advt. in a recent issue of The Music Hall and Theatre
Review, said advt. being mainly devoted to an attack
upon a manager—one Oswald Stoll —who bad quarrelled
with him. When managers and “ stars ” fall out the
public get a notion of how the show business is worked.
In the course of his shriek Knowles says—“ With a sneer
Mr. Stoll suggests that although I am by way of retiring,
I am doubtless prepared to consider fresh contracts with
music-hall managers. In response to this I will say that
Mr. Stoll himself announced me broadcast to make fare-
well appearances when all the time he had contracts in
his safe for future dates.” It seems that the beginning
of strained relations between the parties was a personal
request by Stoll that his dear friend Knowles would omit
the word “damn” from his patter, an astounding
request, all things considered. “ Damn ” has ever been
accepted as the most excruciatingly humorous word in
British music-hall patter, especially when it is emphasised
by a bang on the big drum.
“Den”: Re the “ Australian ga-il”(not “gairl,”as in
B. 12/10/’O5). Can any Bulletin reader explain the
inability of the theatrical person to pronounce the simple
word “girl”? I have not heard that word pronounced
properly on the stage for the past two years. All classes
of mummers seem to be afflicted in the same u'ay, even
the distracted “ mothah ” in the bellowdrama, who
shrieks for her darling little “ gee-ull.” Some pronounce
it “gal” ; and there are many variations, but never by
any chance is the letter “r” given a show. (I am not
under the impression that the word is “ gurl” or “gur-
rul.”)
“J.A.C.”: About six weeks ago died Francesco
Tamagno, possibly, as far as voice goes, the greatest
tenor that ever lived. I knew him some 30 years ago,
before he had completed his studies for the operatic
stage, and I never heard any voice at all approaching his.
It was truly what the Italians call “ a cannon of a voice,”
and of a beautiful, virile quality. He was a big man of
magnificent presence. There was nothing much wrong
about his acting, and, although there havfe been, and are,
more artistic vocalists, yet, taking him altogether, there
was no one to equal him. Verdi, whose dictum was that
there were three things requisite for an opera singer,
“ voice, voice, and voice,” used to swear by him, and
possibly sometimes at him, for the maestro was not
always dainty in his language. Tamagno began his
career as a chorus singer at the Teatro Comunale of
Turin, his native city ; afterwards he rapidly made a large
fortune. He must have been worth a mint of money at
the time of his death, for, as the song says, “ he was a
careful man.” One good point about him was that he
put on no “side.” On his many long journeys, by sea
and land, be travelled in regal state at the expense of
the management, but he was always only too pleased to
escape from his swell surroundings and have a game at
cards with his humbler friends in the chorus.
Ryder Sloan “ off ” is a well-mannered Lancashireman,
with the musical burr noticeable in many of the English
plaintive dialect tones. He entertained his ship-
mates not with comic songs about “Bed,” but his
fine voice thrilled them witi: tales of a devout lover. He
and Boy Glen are pals, the boy’s burr being the result of
Sloan’s treatment. He fathers the lad, chooses his ditties,
cooks his patter, and sews his buttons on, for the sake of
dad Glen, who is Ryder’s oldest friend.

One at Blackburn (Eng.) arrays himself in a shroud and
lies in a coffin, which is drawn through the streets on a
truck. When a crowd has gathered he rises and delivers a
sermon called “ Corpses I Have Met,” or something like
that, amid the jeers of the assembled population. Even
these sensational tactics fail to galvanise the moribund
Salv’army into anything like its former condition.
The names of Goldsmith and Sheridan were blazoned at
the sides of Melbourne Bijou proscenium, in the good old
days of Brough and Bouc.icault, to suggest the standard
of comedy the house intended to live up to. And now,
when the theatre is open at all, the galleryite most times
leans a cheaply-oiled head against the classic names while
surveying the death-scenes of Little Willie and Little Eva,
or the turn of a “ dashing and refined London serio,”
imported direct from Footscray.
Adelaide singer Hilda Felstead, who is studying in Lon-
don, recently’ answered the advertisement of a “ musical
agent ” who wanted vocalists for a forthcoming “ high-
class concert.” The agent proved to be an exceedingly-
oily gentleman with an assumed Italian air, and he threw
fits of amazed rapture when he heard Miss Felstead sing.
Next morning the Australian received a typed note offer-
ing her a night’s engagement at two guineas provided she
spent the amount in half a dozen “ sofa stalls ”at the
“ high class ” suburban concert. There was no ensuement
of business.
The late Henry Irving influenced the English schoolof
acting as no other man could have done, for he was both
the most successful and the most peculiar of actors in his
heyday! A crop of Irving imitators sprang up 30
ago. His mannerisms, in a more or less pronounced form,
were spread throvighout the “ profesh,” the big imitators
having- lesser imitators, some of whom, perhaps, never
saw the old original master of the style. Pinero, what
time he figured in the L.yceum co. under the management
of Bateman and afterwards of Irving, was a notorious.)
copy of the leading man, his long, lean figure, his voibe,
and the top part of his face being- naturally Irvingesque,
apart from his studied mimicry. It happened in more
than one show that Pinero, making an early appearance,
got a loud reception from people who mistook his identity.
As an actor, Pinero was not of great account, but the
co. knew him for a clever fellow, and it was Irving who
staged his first piece—a little comedy-drama—in which
he pla.ved the elderly hero. R. C. Carton, who has
written plays almost equal to the best of Pinero’s, was
another young member of the famous Lyceum Co. for a
while, contemporary with Kyrle Bellew, if the present
scribe remembers rightly. Bellew, more especially at the
time of his first visit to Australia, imitated Irving so
closely that his voice has suffered from the unnatural
strain he put upon it. He talked and posed like the
tall, angular “ Chief,” though, of course, the beauteous
little Kyrle was more spry and graceful in his movements.
Irving had a slight limp, caused by a touch of string-halt
or a defect in the shape of one foot. The walk that his
imitators affected after he became famous was natural to
him from the first, but it ranked among the eccentricities
of genius when he brought it into notoriety. A bend in
his nose would have counted as evidence of intellectual
force. \
Something probably in the wav of another record—cer-
tainly something in the way of another horror. At Ilker-
ston (Q.), lately, there was a waltzing competition ; and
the prize, which was to go to the couple who dropped out
last, had to be cut up amongst three couples. They con
tinued the lunacy for five hours, and then had to be
knocked off because the committee wanted to go home
and milk the cows.
It is solemnly recorded by English papers that violinist
Kubelik bathed recently. Evidently he is coming to
Australia, the clean country.

WHISKY.
The firing of the Stanford-Merthyr coal
mine near Maitland (N.S.W.), last Sunday, is a
tragedy. So far five persons have been killed and
nine injured, and behind that comes the almost
equally pathetic circumstance that 400 men will
be thrown out of work—this at a time when the
coal-miner is none too prosperous, and has com-
paratively few sovereigns stowed away. It is one
of the largest coal mines in Australia which has
met with disaster, and so the distress will be all
the worse. The actual deaths through explosion
were few, yet they leave four widows and 12
orphans. The miner is mostly a married man and
generally a father who does his duty by posterity ;
in fact it is in mining districts that the birth-rate
is highest, while it is in the richest suburbs of
Sydney that it strikes bed-rock. If Vaucluse or
Darling Point blew up in its office the dead
wouldn’t leave many orphans, and the unemployed
wouldn't require to wonder much about the
family crust, there being very little family. But
disaster strikes the coal miner cruelly hard.
» * * *
George Reid’s astute way of putting
things was never more in evidence than in a
recent interview, when he advanced it as his
opinion that Australia should “frankly look to the
British naval forces for its naval protection.” In
other words, we should frankly be cowardly and
dependent and as little like Nelson and the Boys
of the Bulldog Breed as possible, and we should
frankly rely on Ma instead of helping ourselves.
George Beidis capable of putting an adjective like
“frankly” before almost anything. He would
even talk of frankly stealing a copper from a
blind beggar, if it suited him.

A N.S.W. Select Committee has decided
that that Orange firebrand, Parson Woolls But-
ledge, of Sydney, didn’t tell the truth in certain
wild accusations against the Civil Service, and on
the evidence it couldn’t possibly decide anything
else. Any ordinary layman might feel utterly
shamed at being thus publicly branded as a retailer
of falsehood, but Parson Woolls will probably
beam in public places just as usual. The par-
sonical conscience is a curious thing at times.
* * * *
The English bishops chosen to appoint
Bishojb Harmer’s successor in Adelaide must be a
tired lot. They don’t seem to worry themselves
in the least, and the Holy City is apparently
doomed to go bishopless for some considerable
time. Alleged reason is that all the clergy to
whom the appointment has been offered are afraid
of the Labor party, or think they won’t be ad-
mitted into Australia, or some such bosh. Ap-
parently they mistake Australia for the U.S.,
where clergymen arriving under engagement are
shut out, despite the frantic lies of a certain Aus-
tralian politician, who says the States open their
arms to all comers.
The parson whose intelligence has become
bun-besotted and leads him to leave the domain of
the thripenny and talk politics, threatens to grow
to the extent of a nuisance. Bev. Worrall, presi-
dent of the Gippsland Wesleyan circuit, lecturing
t’other night at an annual tart-conflict on “ The
Martyr Heroes of Fiji,” concluded by warning his
hearers of the “sinful, selfish, and hardening influ-
ences” of the White Australia policy. Australians,
then, are to improve this country by welcoming
Tannas who convert their dusky innards into
church-yards ? By importing Priam Singhs, to pay
attentions to unprotected women in lonely farm-
houses? Presumably the Bev. Worrall has never
heard of America, the country which has occa-
sionally to burn some of its nigger in the market
place for the “usual offence”? Or of the dear
little Jap girls of the Westralian goldfields? Or
of the Hindu-Malay-Lascar-aboriginal orgies on
the pearling coast up beyond Broome ? Or of the
sly-grog-selling Chow of Palmerston, or of his
sweet, smellful brother, the opium-smuggling,
girl-buying Celestial of Lilly Buk-stleet ? Aus-
tralia isn’t a goody-goody place, as regards its
white population. It drinks rather too much,
cusses a deal too much, and takes much of the
bloom off its generosity and love of freedom by an
assumption of cynicism and carelessness. But it
may be freely claimed that, minus the horrors which
arise from colored aliens and the presence of our
own blacks, the Australian would be one of the
“best sorts” morally in the world. The parson
who pleads for the admission of the crime-
producing colored alien suggests to The Bulletin
a dim suspicion that the threepenny-man is
seeking to make his occupation of sin-chasing safe.
* * * *
’Lige Dowie’s Australian dupes begin to
roll back. One of three, a woman, who returned by
the Sonoma, writes that “ the falsehoods told to
induce us to come were shameful,” and the pro-
mised plentitude of work, high wages, and free
education were in keeping. Men had to go out-
side for work, and the “ Profit ” collared a portion
of the wages, besides (in one case) sending in a
ten-shilling bill for six weeks’ ‘ ‘ free ” education of
two children. All commodities were double Aus-
tralian prices in Dowie’s monopolistic stores, and
when they ran short (a frequent happening) Zion
had to do without. Also, the most conspicuous
feature of Zion habitations was the swarms of
small, smelly, brown insects which aren’t sup-
posed to haunt a place with a name like Zion.
* * * *
Brisbane Trafalgared greatly —hung out
flags, drank champagne, marched 500 cadets
around, scattered spread-eagle orations, and gene-
rally posed beside its'silted' river as a Son of the
Sea. The Y. M. C. A. , whose chest is like a balloon
since it got a windfall, including a pub., took
charge of the principal arrangements, and wakened
unpleasant memories by sending, on its own
account, a cable to the French President. It ban-
quetted the Naval Brigade and Consuls with tea
and things eatable, and handed round a pro-
gramme-card at the theatre-show, every second or
third line of which card was an ad. for some brand
of whisky, or gin, or schnapps, or beer. The
Boyal Yacht Club at night talked Empire at its
rooms, after drinking ale, cheered Trafalgar pic-
tures on a lantern-screen, and anthemed Edward
VII. Some people also hung out Chinese lanterns
at night. But the demand for an Australian fleet,
so that, at a push, we could go out and try to help
along another Trafalgar, was heard nowhere. The
crowd which talks most loudly about “Sons of
the Sea ” is generally the angriest when anyone
proposes that we also should go in for the Sons of
the Sea industry.

* * * *
The people of Melbourne have lately de-
veloped an eerie habit of leaving their heads
lying loose along railway lines. The first one dis-
covered was standing between the rails at the end
of Princes Bridge station, one Sunday morning.
There was nobody near it, and it was not until a
couple of policemen tramped over a mile along the
line that they came across the rest of the outfit.
An engine had cut the head off and it had bounced
up on to some of the underneath gear and been
carried into the station, to fall off when the brakes
were applied. The next day, the station-master
at North Williamstown walked along the line a
little way to glance at the points, and nearly fell
over another head that was looking at him between
the rails. In this case, the body was only 10ft.
away. The man was identified as a carriage-
builder, who, when leaving home the day before,
had complained of pain in the head. “If thy
head offend thee, cut it off and cast it away,” says
the Scripture—or something like that.
V « » •
The Law in Victoria seems not only to
sympathise with cruelty, but actually to encourage
it. The other day at South Melbourne lovely
woman was fined £5 for unmercifully castigating
a little boy of five years , a ward of State tem-
porarily in her charge. This baby, who had
committed the heinous offence of losing a three-
penny-bit while going a message, was said to be a
mass of bruises from ear down to ankle, having
been stripped naked, his hands tied, and beaten
with a stick. Fined £5 ! The same day, at the
City Court, a woman who, quite unprovoked,
kicked another so that she lay in a hospital bed
for five days, was fined a whole £2 ! But, to
balance this, the culprit in the next case, who had
stolen £6 worth of cutlery from a city hotel, was
given six months’ hard labor without the option.
And Bent still says that no country in the world
looks after its children better than Victoria.

The late chief accountant of Vic. Railways
is being slowly proceeded against on a charge of
falsifying the books, which are undergoing a
special audit meanwhile. From time to time the
accused appears at the City Court, and is re-
manded on bail. The latest remand is for three
weeks, “ so as to clear the Melbourne Cup week.”
During Cup week nothing happens in Melbourne
that can possibly prove injurious to the great
national industry of Ikey Mo.
“Snell”: This
is another fellow’s
story. The name
is Bill, and he
was the male end
of a vagrant court-
ship in one of
the suburbs. The
girl’s parents had
objections, and
the love-making
was done on the
sands, or in parks
and gardens and
waste places. One
night the rain
caught Bill and
Rose. In such
circumstances it
is customary for
the young al
fresco lovers in
our suburb to
seek cover on the
verandahs of
empty houses,
and after a hur-
ried hunt Bill
and Rose took
refuge at a neat
villa and sat on
the gas box.
After a little
while the young
lady became ner-
vous under the
impression that
there was some-
body in the
house. William
also fancied he
heard move-
ments, but was
not disposed to
vacate his com-
fortable pitch,
and succeeded in
persuading Rose
that it was only
rats. In fact
when the noise
subsided into a
sort of clawing
sound on the
other side of the
wall, he was
satisfied himself
that it was rats,
and the love-
making flowed
on smoothly.
Next morning,
going through his Age, William read of a strange
suicide—a man had hanged himself from a
picture screw in the front room of an empty villa,
No. 72, Blank-street. Then Bill remembered that
he and Bose had been roosting on the gas box at
No. 72, Blank-street, at the time given as the
probable hour when the desolate stranger had sus-
pended operations, and he realised that the scrap-
ing sounds he and Bose had heard were theclawings
of the hanging man’s naked feet on the plastered
wall. “Some blokes ain’t got no decency—he
must ’a’ known we was there ! ” said Bill.
* * * •
The “correspondence school” humbug (it
isn’t humbug in so few cases that the exceptions
hardly count) appears to be exploiting medicine
now. The matter was explained the other day
by a litigious cuss, who claimed enormous
damages because, as he alleged, an acci-
dent on the railway had prevented him
making a huge income from his practice as an
optician. He signed himself “Brown, Bef. D.
Phil.,” and he explained that that meant “ Doctor
of Befraction, Philadelphia”; and you became a
“Bef. D. Phil.” by paying fees to a Yankee
“ correspondence school ” for three years and then
writing a thesis. How- many “Bef. D.
Phils.” there are monkeying with this
country’s delicate eyes on the strength of their
having paid a “ correspondence school ” a few
dollars nobody knows ; blit it ought to be some-
body’s business to find out. A country that looks
after the people who look after the other people’s
teeth has plenty of excuse for calling on the people
who meddle with other people’s eyes to justify
their sign-boards and brass-plates.
* « * *
Adelaide police celebrated Trafalgar Day
by raiding one of the most notorious shops in the
Sainted City. Sixty-one prisoners were taken,
and casualties amongst the punters amounted to
one case of severe fright that had to be taken to
Adelaide Hospital to get its breath. Windows
were smashed and furniture broken in the rush of
the gallant but modest defenders to evade pub-
licity, but few, it is alleged, got away from “ the
strong ar-rum.” Three men who hid in a cup-
board were overlooked, and after remaining
folded up for two hours, they managed to crawl
out undetected. A line of cabs was chartered by
the victorious police, and a miscellaneous collec-
tion of racecourse tout, parson’s son, Afghan
camel driver, respectable bank clerk, factory ’and,
gilded Gussie, and plain Bill was loaded into cabs
and carted to the police-station. Several highly
respectable suburban homes have been plunged
into grief as a side issue of the raid ; and there is
much wailing over the unmasking of young hope-
fuls who were supposed to be “ back at the-office ”
or indulging in healthful athletics.

* * * *
The persistent ex-constable Crowe, of Mel-
bourne, has issued another circular re the half-
hearted Commission of Inquiry into Victorian
Police scandals. Crowe makes one very direct
accusation: “I have also sent a charge to the
Commission of my night-watchman and myself
having discovered a gang of policemen and night-
watchmen robbing places I was paid to protect.
Though the Police Department was furnished with
information, and found stolen property in one
policeman’s house, no steps were taken to prose-
cute the constable, though one of their accomplices
received six years for his share of the crimes.”
* * * *
A Portland (Vic.) tradesman, turning out
an old store-room the other day, came upon a box
containing articles of clothing and letters and
documents dated ’5l and ’52. One paper showed
that Mary Campbell owned the box, and that she
was indentured as a domestic servant to a Mr.
Chaffey. None of the oldest inhabitants remember
Mary Campbell, nor can any reason be found why
she went away without her box. Possibly she
left early to avoid the rush of capital being driven
out of the country by Socialistic legislation.

“ Snell ” : Woman has still a lot to learn,
but she is coming on. At Caulfield on Cup day I
was much interested in a tall, handsome girl
charmingly dressed, and looking as sweet and
ingenuous as if just fresh from a nunnery. She
made a rush from the lawn immediately after
Marvel Loch’s number went up. Her face had
suddenly flushed, and there was fine frenzy in her
eye. She actually fought her way through the
c ents flocking about a big-nosed book who was
miserably paying it out, and thrust her ticket in
his hand, gasping: “He’s won! He’s won!”
The book looked at the pasteboard, and said :
“Yith, Mith, he’th won, but thith ithn’t my
ticket.” “Oh,” said the young lady, “does it
matter which?” “Matter vich?” cried Noses,
“Veil, I should thay it did. I’ve got throubles
enough of my own.” The pretty girl then went
to hunt down the right man.
HIS BUST.
The Bulletin
Admiral Togo has been presented by the British and
Foreign Sailors’ Society with a bust of Admiral Nelson,
carved in the Victory’s timbers.
Shadow of Nelson, O forsake
Thy body at St. Paul’s ;
To-day thy kith a mock’ry make
Of England’s wooden walls ;
And, lo ! to magnify the crime
They add thy hero-shape,
To be (base spirit of the time)
The play-thing of an ape.
Let now no Eng-lish grocer boast
That Nelson of the Nile
Doth lend his loyal sailor ghost
To watch o’er Britain’s Isle ;
For all that’s left is —— and enough—
Beneath the chapel stones,
The poor remains of mortal stuff ;
A few forsaken bones.
N.S.W*
HUGH McCRAE.

The summit of absurdity in Trafalgar Day
celebrations was achieved by the naval officahs who
dined aboard Nelson’s flagship and drank in silence
“ To the memory of those who fell in 1805—friend
or foe ” When anybody has been dead 100 years
it is surely time to drop all pretence of solemnity
in referring to the event. Seeing that they
couldn’t possibly have lived until now even if they
hadn’t fallen, all the people who fell in Nelson’s
sea fights are as dead as Julius Caesar. Now, a
silent drink to the memory of Julius Caesar would
be treated as a joke.
IN THE CRICKET SEASON.
FATH ER: “ Tommy, will you have a little more?”
TOMMY: " No, thanks, father. I think I’ll de-
clare my innings closed."
In oratmg over the Trafalgar celebrations
m Melbourne, a suburban municipal councillor
referred to “the splendid victory of Nelson at
vyaterloo.” After that even the memorable occa-
sion when Hannibal pursued Columbus over the
plains of Gibraltar may take a back seat.
“ Melb.”: I came from England to Aus-
tralia .as a child. Not long ago there was a
gathering of people who came by the ship. It
was meant as an entertainment, hut was very
mournful. These colonists seemed to have kept
their eyes in the backs of their heads all the time.
Of course it was different with their children, but
the oldsters sighed as exiles from a place which
kicked them out. You will not get a more exalted
idea of the Pilgrim Fathers and Mothers from
acquaintance with them. .Pear Old England !
Dear old slavery days !
This shows what a grasping person the
working-man is, and how, by comparison, the
Fatman can’t help being benevolent. A while
ago the steamer Milton, bojund from the Cape to
Australia, broke down in mid-ocean, her shaft
snapped. Then—
The engineer, Thomas Halliday, went over the stern in
a sling, and no sooner was he on the boss of the propeller
than he was under water, immediately afterwards to be
sft. clear of it. Two or three times he was washed off,
but he saw his way to partially plugging the inlet, with
the result that one could just work in the inlet without
being drowned.
For 26 days after that the staff struggled away in
the place where they could “just work without
being drowned ”; then the engines were tried,
and the ship moved,' and 12 days after was
able to crawl into Mauritius. The people whose
property had been saved in this way offered the
staff £3OO and a gold watch for the chief engineer
—and they took it, though they had been getting
their wages and dog-biscuit all the time ! The
other case, which shows that humanity isn’t all
bad, however, happened in connection with the
Maorilander Waikato. She broke down, not
under quite such bad conditions; but, instead of
being patched up by the staff, another steamer
hove in sight, picked her up, towed her in—and
that steamer’s Fat owners claim and get £27,700
as salvage. If anybody wants to get a firm grip
of what is meant by “the romance of the sea”
(referred to 716 times in recent Trafalgar articles),
he can’t do better than read the reports of claims
for salvage made by one crowd of rich ship-owners
against another crowd of ship-owners. Dog eats
dog all right when they’re both sea-dogs.

Some folks are like Katisha, and find tame animals very
tame. They do love wild ones. A young girl living not
far out of Sydney has a lion for a pet. She keeps it in a
paddock.
Where is that devouring monster ?
* * * *
Bent says Victoria makes better provision
for child life than any other country known. It
does, it does —and this is how. A young married
wonian camped in a paddock, near Wangaratta
(Vic.), for three weeks without anybody troubling
to inquire the why and wherefore, or”the all-im-
portant how. But one morning a casual passer-by
noticed her lying on the ground in a comatose
condition, while a newly-born infant grazed
placidly some distance away. They were then
taken to the hospital, and, at latest, were not yet
dead ; but that wasn’t the fault of the ruminative
farmers who pervade those parts.

At a Melbourne suburban police court
the other day a large, florid, dishevelled prisoner
was presented on several charges, including
drunkenness, indecorous behavior, and
biting policemen. He had the fragrance
and influence of the beer still upon
him ; there was a dazzled look in
his eye, and he smiled starkly
into the dim distance. When
evidence had been taken, and
just as the chairman was gathering
himself for a moral blast directed
against the sin of beer and the
iniquity of eating
policeman, the pris-
oner suddenly put
out his chest,
piled on all his
dignity, and
addressed the
bench : “Your
conduct has
been shameful
—most shame-
ful. Society
must be pro-
tected against
the likes of
you. Four
months and
two floggings.”
The prisoner
pointed to his
worship and
sternly or-
dered the
police to
“take the
fellow
away,” and
was him-
self walk-
ing magnifi-
cently out of
the court, when
the bench awak-
ened and commanded
the stunned policeman
to drag him back. Possibly
that man was a J.P. who had gone to the
bad, and the good old times came back to him in a
dream when he saw the old familiar court and
the cop’s well-remembered helmet.

Professor Scherer in his. book, “Young
Japan”:—
The two cancers at the core of the Japanese character
are deep-set dishonesty and abandoned impurity ; eitl er
would be sufficient to wreck the life of any nation.
Japan’s is the only civilised Government that deals in
licensed prostitution as a source of revenue, and tolerates
the sale of young gips by their parents under guise of a
regard for “filial piety.” Japan is still a country where
the term “lie ” is not a term of reproach, but rather im-
plies a jocular compliment. Duplicity is masked by the
“Japanese smile”,; nauseating conceit mars popular
manners.
SOW TO REAP.
DOUBTFUL BUYER : “ Yes, but if
this stuff does all you say it does , you could get £1 for it."
BARBER: “ Yes, sir; but we makes our profit cuttin' your hair twice a day after you've used it."
The proposed regulations for the control
of Sydney child street-hawkers were badly needed.
Intention is to issue a license (with a metal badge,
to be worn on the arm) to every juvenile match,
flower and newspaper vendor, street singer or
other alleged musical performer, shoeblack, and
every other youthful wage-earner in public places.
Boys of from 10 to 14 may work from 7 a.m.
till 7 p.m. ; those over 14 may carry on till 10 p.m.,
lint those liable to attend school will be de-
barred from trading at all during school hours. A
card, let into the official badge, carrying the
bearer’s signature, age, number, occupation, and
the date of the license’s expiry, will give even the
casual policeman effective control of the youthful
licensees.
* * * *
The other night, when George Reid was
lecturing to a society of Sydney University
women, this mouse of thought fell dead on the
floor, overpowered by its own brilliancy :
The mind is a power in triplicate—thought, emotion,
will. It was sometimes said there was another power of
the mind, called conscience, but really that was not so.
It was a very amiable view to take, but conscience was
simply the operation of the three powers already referred
to. . . . Few thought of acquiring intellectual powers for
the purpose of developing the immortal principle that
was within them. It would be a grand time for the
world when parents not only put their children to trades,
but opened an avenue for the development of their
immortal intellects.
And many fair listeners were straining their corsets
in the effort to balance on the tight-rope of meta-
physics and guess what George was driving at.
* * « *
Timaru (M.L.) Charitable Aid Board grew
angry the other day. It has been supporting an
old man and his family for quite a long time ; fie
is unable to work and is subject to fits. Yet his wife
goes on adding to the population every year ; the
family is now a large one, and the Board has to
give an extra ration regularly. When the latest
unit came along, the Board made some scathing
remarks, but nothing was done. What could be
done, anyway ? However, it was vaguely under-
stood that one of them was going to tell the old
person privately that he mustn’t do such things
as the community couldn’t afford it, or words to
that effect.
* • * *
Another leper was unearthed, the other
day, in Palmerston (N.T.). It would be interest-
ing to know the number of lepers there are in the
north. Paddy Cahill, the famous buffalo shooter,
recently offered to bring in a mob from the Alligator
River at £1 per head. Two lepers in a month
is a fair average in a small population.
Britain’s idea of suitable emigrants for
Australia : —(1) Convicts of assorted sexes. (2)
Mrs. Pry’s pets, largely female gaol-birds. (3)
“Red Rovers”—females, said to be the street
sweepings of large English cities, many of whom,
however, made excellent wives and mothers, while
some founded “first families.” (4) “Penton-
villians ” —young men and youths from Penton-
ville prison, sent chiefly to Victoria. (5) Booth’s
wasters—origin and condition to be inquired into
before landing.
