“ Hiphen ” writes :
A word on English as she is speaked. Some time ago a
girl said to me : “ I know he’s English. They do speak
so naicelv.” Well, I’ve been there since.
I landed. A man said, “ Kirry yir liggidge, surr ? ”
I touched the fringe of society, and heard : “ Yaas, it
was ah dewy splendid day. Ay thought ay told yah.”
I fled north—to Yorkshire. “ Koom oop, laad, we’m
gooin’ ta show ’ee ta toon ” is what I had to struggle
with.
And so on through Scotland, Ireland, and Wales.
Practically every county in the northern Flyspeek has a
language of its own—it cannot be called a dialect. And
yet they have the impertinence to say Australians speak
badly and with a twang.
If you go from Cape York to the Leeuwin you will hear
one language spoken by white men, but from John o’
Groats to Land’s End you’d need several interpreters.
* * * *
“Elam” : Saw a Melbourne police con-
stable in sad circumstances the other night. He
was removing to the watch-house two soiled
doves, who. after much gin-and-bitters. had wan-
dered into a portion of the city where custom does
not sanction their presence. Crossing Collins-
street. one of them suddenly threw herself on the
tram-line, with loud lamentations. An advancing
gripman rang his bell violently, but had to pull
up. It took gripman, conductor, policeman and
cabby to lift the wailing lady into the four-
wheeler, and meanwhile her comrade clung to the
stanchion of the tram and expounded her estimate
of society to the women on the dummy. A con-
stable who assisted in the suppression of the Fitz-
roy Porties—it was done without the assistance of
the Courts, and by means of batons and short
lengths of stout garden hose—once told me that he
would rather do it all over again than arrest one
woman.
GIVE US A BEST!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After several all-night sittings on the Liquor Bill last
week, N.S.W. Assembly, at 4 a.m. on Thursday morning-,
came to some kind of a decision, which this writer has
been unable to sort out, even after receiving ten con-
flicting exp* illations.
There’s the person of possible piety
Dsing his best;
Plenty of zest 1
There’s the lord of the Liquor Society
Raising his crest;
Swelling his chest!
But as one of the general crowd,
Who are neither important nor proud,
For the sake of a little variety
I would suggest
Having a rest!
Just as one of the general crowd,
I would ask, if I’m kindly allowed,
For the sake of a little variety—
GIVE US A REST !
I’ve a humble regard for propriety
Properly dressed ;
Chain on its vest !
And the greatest respect for sobriety
Filleth my breast !
(’Tisn’t a jest 1)
I have read all the publicans say
(It makes seventeen columns a day)
Till I suffered from weary satiety
Over the quest—
Even the best !
Which is why I am rising to say
In a diffident kind of a way
(I don’t speak from mere contrariety) :
GIVE US A REST 1
Now, a man in a state of dubiety,
Which is the best;
If there’s a test
Very certainly ought to apply it 1 He
Needn’t molest
Those who invest
In a trade that he may not admire
To dispense what he doesn’t desire—
For, unless he can learn to keep quiet he
May be a pest—
Terrible pest!
So, as one of the general crowd,
I would ask, if I’m kindly allowed :
If it’s merely by way of variety,
GIVE US A REST !
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.


The Wild Cat Column.
In Melbourne, the other day, the executive
committee of the Municipal Association wailed piteously
to the members because “ they had in 10 years paid over
£lO,OOO to guarantee companies, and had received back
only £1621.” And even then, when they'saw the profit
there was in the business, the association either hadn’t
the nous or the pluck to suggest undertaking its own
insurance.

The Maoriland Public Trust Office, which
looks after the State’s sinking fund, the Police Provident
Fund, the Coal Miners’ Relief Fund, and other public
trust accounts, and also acts as trustee for private
estates :

1901 .... £22,915
1902 .. .. £10,955 33,870
1903 .. .. 7,363 41,233
1904 .. .. 10,085 51,318
1905 .. .. 14,491 £20,000 45,809
So the State draw's a dividend, and, instead of being
added to sinking fund for the reduction of the public
debt, the amount goes into consolidated revenue ; and
this paper hopes Treasurer Seddon will feel as comfort-
able as the circumstance calls for when he handles the
money. The accounts are this year not accompanied by'
the usual report, and it is impossible to say by how' much
the total value of estates in hand has expanded during
the year. But securities and cash have steadily increased ;
so has the balance to the credit of private estates, put
into the hands of the Trustee under will. These are recent
figures of total trust funds, and as far as possible the
securities in private trust estates (included in the
total): —
; Under Private Total
Trusts. Trust Funds.
1899 .. .. .. £956,153
1900 .. .. .. 1,056,86 S
1901 .. .. £758,323 .. 1,672,304
1902 .. .. 807,624 .. 1,782,951
1903 .. .. 876,049 .. 1,936,073
1904 .. .. 976,768 .. 1,660,716
1905 .. .. 1,098,899 .. 1,896,224
This, of course, refers only to the realised portions of
trust estates ; the values of private estates w'hich have
not been realised and invested are, generally', about 50
per cent, of these amounts. In addition, the office, as a
matter of course, takes charge of intestate estates (value of
Public Trustee’s investments under this head is £100,956),
and of lunatics’ estates (£55,423), and convicts’ estates,
w'hich don’t amount to much, gaols not being in general
for those who have much in the w’ay' of estates. The
number of wills and private trusts in hand increased from
493 in 189 S to 836 in 1904 ; of intestate estates from 797 to
1062. Of the total funds at the end of the past financial
y'ear (£1,896,224), £1,813,709 W'as invested, the balance
w 7 as cash ; and of the investments £1,632,895, or 90 p.c.,
was out on mortgage. Thereportbeing silenton thesubject
of total value of trust estates, the management expenses
p.c. cannot be worked out. For 1904 the expenses ran to
11s. 4d. p.c. of the total value. This, compared with the
rates shown by private trustee co.’s, is high ; but the
public trustee does an immense amount of extremely
difficult work in administering native reserves. The
total income from charges and commissions on wills,
private trusts, and sinking-fund accounts works out at
ss. 11s. per cent., which is well within the rates of
private offices. ,
One item in the accounts of the Maoriland pub-
lic trust office seems liable to be misunderstood unless
some more explanation is offered than the accounts fur-
nish. Of the £32,385 income received last y'ear, £17,797
was “ interest in excess of amounts credited to estates ” ;
from which it might be assumed that, in addition to the
usual commissions and charges, the office invests its
clients’ money at 44 per cent, and hangs on to 4 per cent,
or so for itself. The explanation is that, when the invest-
ment is left to the discretion of the Public Trustee, a
common rate of interest is fixed from time to time by the
Executive, and not only is that rate of interest guaran-
teed, but the estate is guaranteed against loss on the
investment. The “ interest in excess ” is the amount in
excess of the guaranteed rate, and is a very small return
for the State guarantee against loss of principal and
interest. The reserves shown above include £15,414 ap-
propriated to cover possible future losses under this head,
and it doesn’t seem nearly large enough for the work that
it may yet be called upon to do.
-* * * *
After giving the other fire insurance offices
nearly' a year to think Over their refusal to accept re-
insurance from the Maoriland State Office, the latter has
sent its manager to Europe to fix up permanent re-insur-
ance arrangements. When the offices doing business in
M.L. played what they reckoned was their trump card,
and refused anything in the way of a treaty with the new
competitor, and tried to force it to take all its
own risks, the M.L. State Department went to Eng-
lish Lloy'd’s underwriters. In the hope that the other
offices would think better of their boycott, the treaty
was made for only' a year ; but as the other offices
have decided to go on in their foolishness, it has been
decided to- make permanent arrangements abroad.
Whereupon, of course, the baffled insuring crowd shrieks
at the State office, and tells the public that it is mostly' a
mere agency for passing- insurance on to foreign insurers.
Well, whose fault is that? The State office isn’t greedy ;
it is willing to share business with private offices ; and if
private offices won’t, it’s their funeral if the business is
handed over to the outsider. At the same time,
seeing that the State Fire Office has at its back the whole
revenue and assets and taxable power of the State, it is a
pity it didn’t make a perfectly new departure, and keep
ail its risks even if it insured the whole State.
Practically, its capital is about equal to that of all the
fire insurance companies on earth combined, and it
could have taken the chance, and made money by it,
and kept the business and profits in the country.

The writings-off consist of £2996 spent in altering premises
and £1716 devoted to writing down investments. The
reserves consist of formal reserve £77,080, reinsurance
£30,000, equalisation of dividend £lO,OOO, investment
depreciation £8284, and undivided profit £13,593. In
addition there is provision for unadjusted losses £6OOO,
though the directors say' the claims outstanding do not
exceed £l5OO. Compared with some predecessors, the
year’s experience is further shown hereunder :
1895. 1903. 1904. 1905.
Net premiums £58,499 £83,122 £84,468 £88,609

Though fire and marine losses are shown separately', the
same" plan is not adopted with regard to premiums.
Apparently, however, extra losses in the fire department
have been mainly responsible for the jump in the general
loss rate. For several years Australian insurance co. ’s had
a run of remarkably good luck on the whole; but very few
of them show the same for the past 12 months. The expense
rate, though still high, shows a slight reduction, both
absolutely and in proportion to net premiums. Capital
has been increased during the year from £68,226 to
£79,677 ; and, with calls since due, the whole 34,161 issued
shares will be paid to £2 10s. They still carry a £7 10s.
liability. On the mean amount of paid capital during the
y'ear, together with reserves, the profits show 104 per
cent. ; net profits, after the writings-off, are £8 4s. 3d. per
cent.—and there isn’t much to complain of in that. The
co.’s excellent position and its fine progress are mainly
due to the fact that, in the extra-good years, it didn’t pay
meteoric dividends, but added the extra profits to
reserves, and so gave people who want insurance extra
security for their money'. As to investments (apart from
the working balances) the co.’s earnings amount to just
34 per cent.; this is lower than it ought to be, but that is
probably due mostly to a poor return on freeholds
(£56,000), and that again may possibly be due to the co.
crediting its properties with little or nothing for office
rent. But passing over that item as to which there is
some doubt, the investments (60 per cent, of them public
stocks and deposits) are a very solid lot; and their
solidity has been added to during the year by large pur-
chases of N.S.W. and M.L. Government stock, while mort-
gages and miscellaneous debentures have been reduced.

The profits are stated after making an allowance (of un-

and tear of plant. The most solid-looking improvement
in the accounts is the growth of public stock and de-
posits ; the least satisfactory is the rapid growth of
sundry debtors. Whether this amount is running up
because not very much is being written off for bad debts,
or partly on that account, it is impossible to say ; but it
isn’t a good feature anyhow, especially as the turnover
isn’t increasing to the same extent. But, taking the dis-
closed profits as giving a fair idea of the business, Tooth’s,
Ltd., is a highly profitable concern, trading absolutely on
its own capital, and showing on shareholders’ funds a
return of so near to 8 p.c. for the past year that the
difference doesn’t matter.

Somebody will really be suspecting presently that this
co.’s published accounts are not worth much. It writes
off £3OOO as a regular thing, and pays £lO,OOO in divs. as a
regular thing, and has just enough to do it with. From
these figures it might be supposed that Newcastle’s thirst
has a beautifully even surface that you might play bowls
on ; but, after all, it doesn’t appear to be that. A com-
parison of other items (so far as a co. which has the
lumping disease badly itemises anything) may throw

1901. 1904. 1905.
Liabilities to public .. .. £68,307 £61,568 £68,892

In spite of the fact that the lumped expenses and charges
have been much reduced, it looks highly probable that
considerable amounts have been written off for bad debts;
at any rate, the reduction of “sundry debtors” item
is striking. Probably it is through this item that the
remarkable uniformity of the published results is
achieved. Anyhow, note this, which ought to be a good
sig’n if the properties have been reasonably well bought:
while liabilities to the public are practically the same as
they were in 1901, properties, plant, hotels, and goodwill
(the last a shocking- item, at which even a brewery
director ought to blush every time he sees it stuffed in
among the substantial assets) have increased by £25,208,
a little by reducing stocks, but nearly all by reducing
“ sundry debtors,” This money may have been got in
in the ordinary course of business, but it also may
largely have been written off out of current profits. In
any case, the movement is highly satisfactory. An
unfavorable feature, on the other hand, is the reduction
in income. But as to that it has to be remembered that
brewery co.’s have a habit of showing some things net one
year and gross the next, so this comparison isn’t worth
as much as a comparison of balance-sheet figures ought
to be.

And these figures show the book value of properties still

1899 .. £108,576 1903 .. £73,941

1902 .. 78,177
Properties with a book value of £38,492 have apparently
produced £33,387. As these properties have been got rid
of, others have been secured, and the rents from the
entire holding are bunched together ; therefore it is not
possible to form any sort of idea as to whether the most
or the least profitable of the properties have been disposed
of. If it has been a fair average, then the cleaning-up
has been a much less disastrous process than was ex-
pected judging from the size of the contingency account.
This seems satisfactory, anyhow—in 1899 the properties
brought in £3 3s. 6d. percent, on their book value , in
the past year properties (reckoning in the new purchases)
brought in £4 7s. per cent., clear of rates and expense, so
far as can be traced. It isn’t high enough to suggest that
there will be no further losses ; but, as the properties
probably include some unproductive vacant land, it isn t
low enough to suggest that anything serious in the way
of loss or writing-down is ahead.

liabilities have been reduced by only £12,527 ; and at that
rate, the remaining assets would wipe out only £35,000 of
the debt. Still, the experience of the past couple of
years has been rather better, so something a trifle more
cheerful than that may be hoped for. The fading
concern continues to be economically managed.

This society is extending its business steadily ; in fact, it
has increased its advances so much during the past year
that, as it is apparently' not seeking new deposits, it has
had to go to the bank for an overdraft of £5314, whereas
12 months before it had a credit balance of £1604. The
properties in possession are now valued at £42,841 ; 12
months ago the item stood at £45,864 ; and if the profit
of £l5OO has been made on the properties sold (£3021),
then the writing down in 1898 was very severe indeed.
Possibly, however, some new properties have fallen into
possession during the year, though the item doesn’t con-
vey that impression. In any case, the position as the pub-
lished accounts show it is exceptionally good ; for after
paying for repairs, rates, and insurances, the properties
in possession give a return of £6 16s. 6d. per cent. Apart
from properties in possession, the society’s almost only
asset is “ advances, £109,787 ” ; this is £15,136 more than
it was a year ago, and £69,268 more than it was in Sept.,
1899, just after the cleaning up. Meantime net liabilities
to the public (after allowing for current dividends) have
increased onlv from £6622 to £24,680. Thus the new
advances of £69,268 since 1899 have been made up as
follows :—£18,058 from new deposits, overdraft, &c. ;
£29,842 from sale of properties (book value) ; and the
balance (£21,368) from profits. Whether, however, much
of it can really be called profit, seeing that it is merely
the surplus over and above the value to which the assets
were written down when nearly half the capital went
overboard, is another matter. But it was something
emphatic in the way of a cleaning up, anyhow.

During the year this co. has increased its capital from
£170,000 to £250,000 by the issue of 60,000 more cumula-
tive preference shares (making 140,000) and 20,000 more
ordinary shares (making 90,000); in addition there are
20,000 pref. shares, but without the extra blessing of
having a cumulative preference. Besides all this
heavy weight of capital, an undisclosed but tre-
mendous amount of which is represented by nothing more
solid than goodwill, the co. has now a special overdraft
from one bank of £30,000 secured by debentures covering
the co.’s assets, and another ordinary overdraft from the
same bank of £351, and a third overdraft from another
bank of £24,438. In 1903 the total debts to the public
(including overdrafts) were £34,700 ; in 1904, £39,836 ;
now, in spite of issue of another £BO,OOO of capital, the
liabilities stand at £89,772. Something like £16,000 more
has just gone into “goodwill, trade marks, patent rights,”
&c. —paper items which ought to be got rid of as soon as
possible ; and, as they stand at £137,524, they will take a
lot of getting rid of; yet not a penny is written off them this
year. Another £66,967 has gone into stocks on hand (valua-
tion), bringing them to £117,710 ; the balance of the money
which has been raised during the year has gone into
plant or is represented by “ sundry debtors, £37,120.”
The co. has been developing a business in general rubber
goods ; it lately absorbed one of the oldest of Victorian
rubber-goods businesses, the Pioneer, and no doubt this
accounts for the big changes in the figures. As a power-
ful, vigorous concern which is doing much for an Aus-
tralian industry the Dunlop Co. deserves specially well of
Australia; the" misfortune is that it is so top-heavy with
capital, represented mostly by “rights,” which really
amount to so much foreign debt. For the goodwill
alone Richard Garland, the energetic managing director
of the Canadian Co. which secured all “colonial” rights
from the English Dunlop Co., got £125,000 from the Aus-
tralian Co. (£45,000 in cash and the rest, in shares); and
that is a huge bundle for any industry to struggle under.

*■* * *
Shareholders in Wickham and Bullock Island
Coal Co. (N.S.W.) are probably sorry now that they didn’t
save enough out of the profits from the old colliery to
buy and open up a new one. The old colliery is worked
out ; and, as the co. has some pref. capital, if it was
wound up there wouldn’t be enough left from the sale of
assets to give the ordinary shareholders anything. The
latter therefore decided to keep their interest in the co.
by buying a new property. But as the co. has no avail-
able funds, though it paid a 12$ per cent. div. no later
than 1901, and as a new property costs money, and as
calls wouldn’t bring in the necessary money swiftly
enough, the directors have borrowed at 1% per cent,
interest. Of course, there has been a row over it, and one
lot of shareholders want to kick the directors. Nobody
has so far suggested that they should kick themselves for
encouraging per cent. divs. when they knew that the
mine was just about worked out and there wasn’t any
available capital to buy another one with.
* * * *
The basis of the settlement of the trouble
between the Australian Alliance Assurance Co. (Melb.)
and the life-policy-holders is that the co. shall transfer
£50,000. Gent One professes to see great difficulties in
the way of stopping the litigation ; but if both parties
agree to shut-down on costs, Gent One probably won’t see
so many difficulties. The original judgment against the
co. was for £113,082 ; the compromise will reduce capital
and reserves from £224,657 to £174,657, less costs, which
probably run to £lO,OOO at least—£s369 “law expenses”
was included as an asset in suspense as at August 31st,
being apparently the amount paid to that date. There
are 50,000 shares in the co. But in re-estimating the
value of shares, in view of the compromise, it has to be
remembered that, in the bulk, the assets don’t return
quite 3| per cent. ; so the reserves probably ought to be
written down by a considerable amount.

Registered in Melbourne, the Ecclesiastical Fire
Insurance Co., Ltd., to insure Church of England property
in Australia. The Vic. bishops and one or two other

plate and till. * * *
The Canterbury Farmers’ Co-operative Asso-
ciation, Ltd., is another of the big organisations by which
Maoriland producers do, to a great extent, without the
middleman, and keep his profits for themselves. Only two
of its balance-sheets are available—for 1904 and 1905—but
they show a large and apparently increasing business.
After paying dividends (8 per cent.)on capital, 6 per cent,
bonus on shareholders’ purchases, and making a 25 per
cent, rebateon commissionspaid by shareholders on various
sales, the co. in 1904 had reserves of £37,079, including
undivided profit. There were for this year (to
July 31) reserves of £57,551, in addition to
£758 premiums on new share issue ; total £58,309. The
year’s profit was thus £20,472 ; this was after writing off
£2144 for fire losses, besides ordinary depreciation. In
1904 the profit was £18,309, assuming that nothing was
written off the special reserve for doubtful debts—a pre-
vious balance sheet would be necessary in order to see
whether anything was written off or not. The paid capi-
tal is now £59,188, against £52,584 twelve months ago ;
and the same dividends and bonuses are to be paid, ab-
sorbing about £16,000, and leaving the reserves (including
undivided profit) at £42,309. In addition to its capital, the
co. has £94,127 of fixed deposits to work upon, and its
other liabilities to the public run to £89,113 ; total liabili-
ties to the- public, £183,240, or about twice the co.’s own
funds. The assets include freeholds £40,540 ; the balance
is goods on hand (£82,337), and sundry debtors. The
business is, no doubt, highly profitable, but with such a
very large amount of outstanding accounts and stock, the
! co. appears also to carry heavy risks. For a co-operative
co. its reserves are unusually large, but not at all too
large considering that the capital is small and liability to
loss through depreciation and bad debts is very extensive.

Between the beginning of October and the end-
ing of it B.H. Block 10 shares sprinted along from 775. to
90s. and Junction Norths from 13s. to 16s. 3d. This, in
the case of Block 10’s, was on top of a previous big rise.
Sydney rushed in after both these shares, and there has
been a heap of over-selling. Speckers in Sydney
and elsewhere considered that Sydney was coming in at
the usual end of the animal, and that they would be able
to buy in lower in a few days if delivery was demanded.
Sydney all along has been taking a little notice of silver-
lead stocks certainly, but hasn’t been viewing them with
any enthusiasm; and now, when Block 10 has just
doubled its price in 13 months, and British (new) has gone
from 9s. lid. to 315., and South (cont.) from 295. 6d. to
625. 6d., it thinks it is time it spoke. But, having slept
this long time, it had better go on sleeping.
Broken Hill Block 10 shares had a jump of ss.
on the report of “another sale of residues”—6o,ooo tons
at, it is said, about 10s. a ton. “ Residues ” turn out to
be slimes, which Broken Hill hasn’t reokoned in as
residues for a long while past. B.H. Prop, has been kiln-
roasting and smelting this class of stuff for over three
years, and making a profit of £1 per ton out of it; so if
Block 10 hasn’t got more than 10s. per ton, it has done
poorly. And, in any case, the purchase of these slimes
doesn’t indicate any new development in metallurgy.
There would he much more justification for a sharp rise
in price of stock if the mill were doing better work and
getting one ton of silver-lead concentrates from 5£ or 6
tons of ore instead of from seven. The profit from that
wouldn’t arrive in a heap, like the proceeds of these sales
of tailings, etc. ; but it would last much longer, and
wouldn’t give the share-market the jumps so badly, and

A 12 inch Suction Dredging Plant is being built
for the Weldbrook Tin Mines Go. The plant will be
similar in design to those erected at the Pioneer and
Briseis Extended Tin Mines (Tasmania). The engines will
be of the vertical compound condensing type of about
300h.p. fitted with an Edwards type of air pump and
large surface condenser. The boilers will be two in
number, of the marine type, and will work at a pressure
of 1501 b. to the square inch. The nozzle pump will be
centrifugal and directly connected to a vertical compound
steam engine. The barge will be 45ft. square. The
Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South Melbourne, which
is constructing this dredge, has been particularly success-
ful in building these large type of suction dredges and
owing to their reputation have just concluded a contract
for the erection of a plant in the Federated Malay States
which promises to be the fore-runner of many more of the
like.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large »
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*
* * * *
The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Broken Hill speculators are cheerful—such of
them as are not bears—because of ex-ceptionallyfavorable
lead statistics. But most metal statistics, those for lead
especially, are a good thing to fall-in over. It P
out that the September imports to Britain were only
15,917 tons, which was much less than usual , and as 5653
tons were exported, the supply must have been very
short, and prices must move up. No doubt t 0
moment. But look at it this way. For the first seven
months of the year the United States, which does
a huge smelting business in bond for Mexico, held
on to 22,274 tons of imported lead. Someof that may
have gone into consumption; but it isn t likely to
have been much, because it would have had to pay a •klO
per ton duty before it went out of bond ; and that the u.a.
can produce lead at much below the price to which the
duty would bring the imported metal, is shown by the
fact that it actually exported some of its own lead to
England, where there isn’t any duty. It looks, therefore,
as though the 22,274 tons and much more that has accu-
mulated since isn’t in England simply because it’s lying in
store in the U.S. waiting for the price to go up still
further ; and then it will dribble out. Which means that
prices are high now because the whole of current produc-
tion isn’t going into use ; presently they will be pushed
down below normal because more than current produc-
tion will be reaching the market. And prices of silver-
lead shares will probably follow.
In New York Mining Engineering J. W.
Broomhead, who is supposed to know something about
that tricky subject, the supply of metals, says that “ to
Broken Hill the world must look for its future supply of
spelter.. . I can clearly see that in a few years time these
Broken Hill mines will be in a position to dominate the
spelter market.” Broomhead adds: “In the whole
metallurgical world I can see nothing to compare with
future prospects of the Broken Hill group of mines.” All
of which w'ill make pleasant reading to those Broken Hill
co.’s that have jobbed away their whole output of zinc
material, plus one-fourth their lead and about three-fifths
of their silver, at little better than dirt prices. At pre-
sent it looks as though three-fourths of the profits will go
to a foreig n group of financiers : and unless Australia does
something emphatic, it looks also as though it won’t even
have the job of using its own labor and coal and coke to
turn the dirt into metal.
* * * *
The Bewick-Moreing people are taking an
interest in the Dapto (N.S.W.) smelting works—now in a
bailiff’s hfhds, mostly through trying to knock out
stronger people in the risky game of ore-buying. The
Anglo-Australian Smelting Co. is on the stocks in London,
with the new Zinc Corporation behind it ; if it goes (and
with that crowd to back it, it’s a small “if”) the first
business will be to shift the works from Dapto, where they
should never have been put, to Newcastle. The old bait
of big New Caledonian custom is also being held out;
and the product from the new Broken Hill South Blocks
mill, which is also under Bewick-Moreing control, will go
that way. So far the Zinc Corporation hasn’t done any-
thing more definite than get plans under weigh for the
first unit of a concentrating plant to handle its tailings.
Present idea is to send on the silver-lead product to the
new works at Newcastle, and let the foreigner have the
much bigger job of smelting the zinc product.
» * » *
“Watty” : Re first shipment of ore from the
Barrier. According to a statement made to me by late
explorer Ernest Giles, “ Paddy ” Green tried the district
years before the records credit him with having done it.
Giles, in ’94, told me and others that, 20 years previously,
he, under instructions from Green, went out to the
Barrier to find by observation its latitude and longitude,
and ascertain whether it was in N.S.W. or S. Australia.
Green knew of the outcrop of what is now Broken Hill.
On Giles’ return to Bourke, Green sent out a cart from
Bourke, and had a ton of surface stone brought in 300
miles or so. A local blacksmith smelted some of it in his
forge, and as the stuff that resulted looked like ordinary
clinker, Green abandoned his scheme. No crucible was
used, and naturally enough the product would appear as
common clinker. They only thought of g - old —never
thought of silver or lead. For years the boulders lay in
the backyard, and Green, junior, told me he used to roll
them down the banks of the Darling River. Green, senr.,
was within an ace of capturing Broken Hill, and was just
the sort of man to open it up.
* * *
New Hillgrove Prop.’s shaft (N.S.W.) is a
source of anxiety. That no one was killed in the recent
burst is a consolation, but the possibility of more serious
consequences is there all the same. If the shaft became
useless it would force the Prop, to come to some arrange-
ment with,Baker’s Creek Co., whose boundary is only 90ft.
away. ~ If these co.’s threw in their lot together there
would be a better chance of developing the deeper levels,
which can be done more conveniently from the New Hill-
grove end. The scheme to carry the New Hillgrove
Prop, tunnel right through to cut the Eleanora reef is
one of those big ideas that require both courage and
money; and J. Bull, who owns the Hillgrove Prop.,
credits himself with plenty of both. But if the Eleanora
shaft is to be sunk, as is also proposed, it seems like
making two bites at one small fruit.
* * * *
Shareholders in Cobar Gold Mines are a patient,
long-suffering crowd. They didn’t kick when every penny
of profit made out of their oxidised ores was put into the
stocking - —“ to provide plant,” they were told, “ to treat
the sulphides with.” Twelve months ago they were told
that the milling ores were just- about finished. Since
then, however,: things have just dragged on, and latterly
the expensive mill, which will soon have to be scrapped,
has been banging away at odds and ends that have not
anything like paid costs. And still not a word about the
policy for the future. It’s a pretty long stocking the co.
has ; but it will melt without anything being done if
shareholders don’t wake up the board.
Apropos the richness of the ore where struck i n
the C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.). The nature of the C.S.A.
body is worth remembering ; it is a big lode from which,
down to the point where the ore was struck, the metals
had been leached out. G. H. Blakemore and others con-
cluded that there must be something below water level
because, near by, solid blocks of copper, evidently leached
from this lode, and caught in crevices of neighboring
rocks, had formerly been found. It is highly probable
that, where the ore is now found, it contains much of the
metal leached out from the lode above. The same thing
occurred at Broken Hill; the richest ore was found just
below the porous ironstone, and, again, the cap of the
sulphide zone was far richer than the ore that is found
.underneath. The general average of C.S.A. ore can’t
■ therefore be judged with certainty from the value of the
stuff in the present crosscut.
* * * *
R. W. Hardie, of Hardie and Gorman, hit Syd-
ney while it was hot, with a little C.S.A. float. At Cobar
he"got hold of three blocks, south of the one that Frank
■Gardner bought from Andrews Brothers, and, coming
back, asked £2OOO and a sixth interest for them. After
■some negotiations with Gardner, people were found to
.take them up on these terms, and the public has come into
the gamble. There are now 1000 shares of £lO, 800 being
Issued—soo to the public and 300 to the vendors. The
vendors also take £2500, which leaves £2500 to cover the
•cost of fossicking for the"C.S.A. lode—if it is there. If it
.runs far enough in the course it is apparently taking, it
.ought to be there ; but, then, all lodes stop somewhere.
* * * *
Castlemaine (Vic.) lives on the ground-sluicing
industry just now. It hasn’t three quartz mines worthy
the name, for the Spring Gully group isn’t panning out
up to hopes, and Francis Ormond is still groping about
for a payable shoot of stone. Then Cumberland and
Phoenix has closed down, and its plant sold. But divs.
flow in from the dredges, so the townspeople don’t look
so much down the sides of their noses as they should.
Balance-sheet of the Golden Point Dredging Co.
shows that, on Bs. called up, 19s. has been paid.
Russell’s Golden Point Co. has also started with a 2s. 6d.
div. on shares only called up to ss. These are first class
records, and explain the preference of Castlemaine folk
for this class of mining, as the risk isn’t great and profits
are almost certain if reasonable precautions have been
taken to prospect the ground.
Bright (Vic.) people are gradually opening their
eyes to the tact that Tewksbury wasn’t such a fool as they
took him to be when he pegged out the high land above
the river that every one had scorned as useless for bucket-
dredging. Tewksbury’s own dredge is reported to have
done record work, though the owner has kept a silent
tongue about results. Still, there must have been some-
thing out of the common or he would not have cleared so
fast to Maoriland to buy a couple more dredges. Nor
would there have been such ease in raising the funds to
put them on the ground. One or two Sj'dney people are
supposed to have joined with Tewksbury.
Melbourne newspaper men used so much paper
over compliments to Long Tunnel Dawson about his
analysis of the co.’s costs that they forgot to perform that
little duty for themselves. What has to be borne in mind
is that Long Tunnel stock has a market value of well on
the way to £5. Is there enough stone in sight to justify
that value? Development work during last half-year
resulted in 29,000 tons being proved. Putting this
down as worth 13dwt., the stone in sight carries
18,5000 z., which at £3 12s. 6d. per oz. is worth, say,
£68,000. Costs at £1 10s. per ton work out at £43,500,
or a profit in sight of about £25,000. On a share register
of 24,000 shares this is equal to a little over 20s. per share.
Co. has not thought about the capital for a new battery,
and it has to open up its north-west ground. Here there
are about 80,000 tons worth 9dwt., and if it can be worked
at a cost of 7dwt., then the backbone of the co. will be
found there. With the Extended working at 2500 ft., the
ground ought to be partly drained. Then the co. has
stone at 2050 ft. south to develop, but that cannot he
classed as proved until the trend of the shoot is known.
* * * ♦
Walhalla (Vic.) people, it was said last week,
had backed the Long Tunnel against the Extended, and
must be scowling over late developments. Whereupon a
Walhalla man writes: “ True, Long Tunnel has been
bumped lately, and those who got in at high-water mark
have had nightmare a couple of times. The Gladstone
tin collapse has not helped to steady things ; but, all the
same, Grayson’s incline shaft won’t turn on him. It will
be there when the last Pearson draws the ancestral div.
The mine will give the bear crowd a shake before long.
Present yields are more than bread-and-butter, and
another ’weight or two will leave the hand-to-mouth days
behind. Workings look a lump better just now, and
stampers are not telling a bad tale. To put the shaft
down another lift is only a small matter the way they do
things these times.”
* * * »
Re Coronation, Walhalla (Vic.), held up the
other day as a model for the Collins-street-run Donnelly’s
Creek shows to copy. The claim, on the outskirts of the
Gippsland township, started in a small way, and is now
going strong. It has a register of 2400 shares, same as
old Long Tunnel. Co. is putting through 100 tons a
fortnight of ounce dirt. There is a 6-head mill, but it is
shortly to be added to. The board is a practical crowd,
and the mine manager gets up early. Shareholders,
mostly miners, have handled regular divs. since Xmas,
and sit behind a decent reserve. Original shareholders
are on velvet, and are said not to have parted with a
scrip.
m * * *
Queensland coal-production makes very little
progress nowadays. From 1895 to 1901 the State tariff pro-
moted industries, and there were factories for the collieries
to feed, so the output moved along briskly from 323,068
tons to 539,472 tons. Then the Commonwealth Laborites
“sank the fiscal issue,” and Queensland made a further
mess of things and sent some Foreign-traders to the Aus-
tralian Parliament to help G. H. Reid knock out such fac-
tories as there were. Consequently, though the coal out-
put had moved from 323,068 tons in 1895 to 539,472 tons in
1901, it at once began to sag again, and last year it was
down to 512,015 tons. Moreover, in the struggle to keep
going, the collieries have been obliged to cut down their
selling rates; the average pricejn 1898 was 7s. 4|d. per
ton (Ipswich coal), and last year it was only ss. 11 jd. And
the hewing rates have come down to c rrespond. Pre-
vious to the Federal tariff there was an import duty ; but
the fall in the output of Queensland coal has not been
due to the removal of that duty. In fact, the imports of
coal, coke, and shale in the past five years have been
valued as follows
1900 .. ..£32,654 I 1903 .. ..£25,083
1901 .. .. 31,256 1904 .. .. 16,494
1902 .. .. 24,902 I
The development of Queensland coal mines, which was
going on steadily under the moderately high State tariff,
has thus been stopped, not by inter-State competition,
. but because the low Commonwealth tariff has knocked
out the Queensland coal-using industries that were
slowly g-etting' on their feet. The Queensland Laborites
sank the i seal issue and the hopes of Queensland indus-
tries and the Queensland coal trade at the same time.
Ipswich or Bundamba coal isn’t the best in the
world, but it is a long way from being the worst. It is
somewhat tender, and requires careful handling ; also it
needs screening. One or two cargoes have been sent
away, and haven’t turned out well ; but absolutely no
conclusive test has been made of it. And, anyhow, if
there were the home consumption that there should be,
there would be no need to look to foreign trade. The
known coal resources of the northern State are not any-
thing tremendous, and there’s no reason why Queensland
should break its neck in a struggle td feed the foreign
factory when it has none too much with which to feed the
factories it ought to have itself. The tests certainly
didn’t prove that the coal was not of excellent quality
for home use. An evil yarn was once told about N.S.W.
coals for coking purposes; nobody would buy N.S.W.
coke, because one or two tests of coke made out of coal
that hadn’t been properly screened and washed gave poor
results. Yet in 10 years, with proper handling, N.S.W.
coke has absolutely driven the foreign article out of the
Australian market. Queensland coal years ago was put to
another test in the Government steamer Otter, under
rigorous conditions, and came out satisfactorily. It then
took from 7’6831b. to BT7Blb. of Queensland coal to
evaporate water from the boiling point, against from
8‘043 to 8T221b. of N.S.W. coal. A coal that stands up as
well as that in comparison with the Bulli and Newcastle
product is good enough. It is only the tariff that isn’t
good enough.
* * * *
The output of Q. coal for the past couple of
years would not have shown up even as well as it has
done if it had not been for the opening up of three or
four new collieries in the Ipswich district, from which
large quantities of surface coal have been available at a
very low cost. The Aberdare colliery, the biggest in the
State, has held its own, but the other older ones haven’t ;
and the average time worked in the district has only been
about three days a week—which is a pretty heavy price to
pay for “ sinking the fiscal issue.” An association to more
or less handle the coal of the district—much in the same
way as the Lithgow association does on the western
N.S.W. field—might do some good. It would help, at
any rate, so far as shipping business is concerned ; at
present no colliery can promptly supply a large order.
But there would be a lot of waste even then ; an amalga-
mation of several interests is badly needed to get the
best working results. The present is an ineffective, cut-
throat system ; and if a colliery is hard pushed it often
isn’t as particular as it ought to be in “ filling,” and an
owner can’t be too particular in working banded seams
like those at Ipswich. But amalgamation or an associa-
tion wouldn’t increase the demand for coal; it might, in
fact, put up the price, and reduce the demand. Nothing
but a higher tariff can put up the demand and the price
at the same time.
» * * *
Mt. Morgan (Q.) is ahead of most
mining co.’s in two respects—it has adopted producer gas
for its power plant, and has steam shovels at work. Most
interest centres round producer gas because the co.
alleges a big economy is made in the use of coal. Some
of the coal is anthracite, mined at Wallaroo (Q.), and con-
taining over 80 per cent, of carbon. Producer gas is
formed by the mixture of steam with furnace gases, and
where big units of power are required at a fixed centre, it
is found more economical than the ordinary steam power.
Although roasting and chlorination is an elaborate pro-
cess, Mt. Morgan has reduced mining and treatment
costs below the figures of most co.’s which treat ore in
the crudest manner. The average is slightly under the
half-ounce ; and yet the 3d. monthly dividend tots up to
£150,000 per annum.

* * * *
North Queensland is having another little flutter,
in antimony mining. Market for the metal has topped
£6O, and buyers are offering from £2O to £25 per ton for
60 to 70 per cent, material. It is certain, from these
figures, that the N.Q. stuff is free from such impurities as
lead and arsenic. Nevertheless the buyers’ price leaves
£l5 to £2O per ton to cover transport to Europe, and
smelters’ costs and profit. Although the metal is
extremely volatile, metallurgical losses are not heavy. In
fact, antimony smelting and starring is anything but
difficult, and when Australia has rubbed its eyes
sufficiently and introduced a reasonable protective tariff,
the bulk of that £l5 to £2O per ton will remain in Aus-
tralia. We will ship the starred metal and receive the
foreigners’ cash for it.
* » * «
Officially reported that the surveyors of the
Etheridge-Almaden (N.Q.) route have discovered an easy
means of crossing the Newcastle Range. The survey is
being conducted at the joint expense of the Queensland
Government and the Chillagoe Co. Surveyor Fraser, who
is making a name for himself in N.Q., has charge of the
field work. The Government, however, controls the sur-
vey. An easy route over the Newcastle Range is cer-
tainly something accomplished. Fraser has gone back to
Einasleigh, whence he will begin his trial survey. Detail
survey from Almaden to Einasleigh was made three years
ago, but, despite that fact, and despite the solution of
the Newcastle Range difficulty, it looks as if the present
session will not see the Etheridge Bill.
* * * *
Aldershot works (Maryborough, Q.) have just
handled two rich parcels of New Guinea stone. First, 46
tons 18cwt., from the Woodlark King, owned by
McLellan, Hicks, and McLean, gave £BlO, or 4oz. 18dwt.
per ton (this gold is only worth £3 10s. 6d. per oz.); the
other, from the Illawarra claim, on Woodlark Island,
belonging to Hicks and McLean,"gave 4oz. 2dwt. per ton
from 23 tons. Latter gold was worth £3 13s. lid.,
making the parcel worth £348. A small quantity of con-
centrates is still to be dealt with. These owners have
another parcel coming down.
•* * « *
Reconstruction of Hannan’s Star (W.A.) and
proposal to double the treatment plant, seem more
unjustifiable as time proceeds. During September co.
treated 2450 tons worth 15s. 9d. at a cost of 235. 10d., and
made a loss of Bs. Id. per ton, or £lOOO. During previous
three months the loss was £l4OO. Further, the estimates
of ore reserves are unreliable, as the co.’s history has
proved. Last official estimate gave the value of reserves
as 345. a ton ; and, as shown just now, the ore treated in
September was worth only 15s. 9d. The 4s. assessment
will give directors £42,400, but if they double the plant,
and double the loss, the money won’t last long. Mine
can provide barely 25,000 tons monthly of the grade now
being treated. What will happen when double the quantity
has to be gouged out ? Probably the loss will be more
than doubled.
* * « •
Lady Shenton, Menzies (W.A.), which was
supposed to be utterly depleted, has some pay ore still.
During September tributors gouged out 523 tons, giving
4110 z., which isn’t bad for a dead’un. Mine was always
very patchy, and the shoots difficult to locate and follow ;
but tributors have a keen scent for patches. Many mines
in W.A. would be working more extensively if tributes
could be obtained. For instance, tributors have long
been trying to get into Hannan’s Brown Hill, and are
willing to start from the surface, but management won’t.
This dog-in-the-manager policy will be stopped some day.
* * * *
The latest race of mining men seen in Tassy are
of the sanguine order. The one notable exception is
Ryan, of the Gladstone. Grayson and Paul Northey vie
with one another in whooping up their respective shows.
Now another Northey has joined the chorus of these
greater prophets. This is J. W., brother of Paul; and he
tootles in a nice strain over a tin show up at the half-
worked-out Weldborough field. The claim is the Weld-
brook, a float of Langford’s, and recently discussed by
“ East Tasmanians” Nos. 1 and 2in The Bulletin. “The
flats are wide,” says the newcomer. They are—and many,
as anyone who has visited Weldborough knows. They
are also wet, he opines. That is not unusual
on the part of flats, especially in winter.
J. W. Northey found the Weldbrook flats too wet, in
fact, to bottom, so he tried the dumps of old shafts. He
sampled the claim in other places with “equally good
results.” Of the overburden, he writes “ The whole of
the overburden carries tin. In places the surface soil is
rich in tin. I have seen lib. weight of tin washed from a
small dish of dirt, but I do not wish to impress on your
minds that the whole of the surface soil is like this.”
This note of warning comes just in time to save the situa-
tion. It inspires the hope that in the summer some of
the flow of enthusiasm may evaporate, and that J. W.
Northey will then do a systematic sampling of the claim,
Weigh the property up in the balance that Paul used at
Arltunga, and then let the public know if his later results
coincide with surface prospects. Till then the public had
better wait. It is just as unsafe to buy a pig in a bog as
one in a bag.
* * * *
J. P. Lonergan, who has passed out in London,
was James Crotty’s nephew, and shared the latter’s for-
tunes on the Lyell field. Lonergan, prior to Crotty’s
strike of good fortune, was a railway-man who had worked
his way forward with Celtic push and good humor to the
post of station-master at a Melb. suburban station. It
was a change from selling - tickets to becoming the holder
of mining stocks worth a hundred thousand. Crotty
and Lonergan made big rises out of North Lyell. But how
much bigger would it have been had they never gone to
London to bring in Bull directors ! Lonergan was also
up to his neck in the fateful South Lyell, in Lyell Con-
sols, and a dozen other shows of the field which once had
a value of pounds, but now are almost forgotten. In his
halcyon days in Melbourne Lonergan was a marvel of
generosity, and more than one man ought to pay a
tribute to his memory. Also he owned the Mt. Lyell
paper, which came to so untimely an end. Of late years
he lived in London, but his surroundings there were
never so congenial as in Melbourne amid the dare-
devil set in which he moved.
* * * •
“Vigilans,” from Yam Creek (N.T.) : —“The
Northern Territory Mines of Australia, Ltd., is an English
co. It holds an immense area in the North ; much of the
country from the Howley to Pine Creek is in its hands.
Not a mine is really being worked in the 50 miles. Men
have tried to get tributes, but no business. Something is
wrong in the Mining- Act, when a great slice of country
can be held like this. For years nothing of consequence
has been done, and the place is merely a wilderness inha-
bited by a few Chows. Is it any wonder the country
makes no headway when this and other co.’s hold mining
areas just for speculation ?
“The Howley Mine, Brock’s Creek, has been shut
down for many months. Europeans have vainly applied for
tributes. Why they hold on to this mine is a mystery.
Tons of machinery are lying rotting - all over the place. A
40-stamp battery is falling into decay, while white ants
have destroyed the woodwork. •
“At Yam Creek the co. is ‘developing’ the
Ironblow and Mount Ellison mines. Some huge sum (they
say £170,000) has been spent (largely wasted) in batteries,
buildings, &c. A 20-stamp battery has hardly ever been
used. Two Wilfley tables are used by goats as a camping
place. A smelter and two reverb, furnaces lie idle and
sadly need repairs. Tons of machinery are stored away
or lying about promiscuously. Electric light is laid on
everywhere, and there is a hundred miles or so of telephone.
A main line cost £28,000 for 11£ miles. A general mana-
ger, assayer, assistant assayer, three clerks, underground
manager, engineer, shift-boss, and foreman carpenter
look after 12 European miners and 35 Chows at the Iron-
blow. At Mt. Ellison two whites look after 28 Ah Pongs.
The Ironblow (silver-gold) is down to the 200 ft.
level. Enough ore is lying- at grass to last the smelters
for eight months. Some of this ore runs up to 670 z.
silver and 12dwt. gold. There is no difficulty in smelting ;
as yet they have not met any trouble from zinc.
A large quantity of iron ore from the blow
is lying on the hill, and carries enough gold to pay an
economical co. Last smelting was six months ago. In
six weeks the value of the ore smelted amounted' to
£ll,OOO. There’s a veil of mystery hanging over this
mine. Unlike most N.T. mines, the workings on the
100 ft. and 200 ft. levels are good. It should be a good
gamble to buy shares at Is. 6d. (present quotation). After
next smelting more should be heard of the Ironblow.
But in connection with the N.T. you never know. At
Mount Ellison (copper) developing work is going on, on
the same lines as at the Ironblow. Everyone is very
reticent. All the work at Yam Creek was done by
Chinese, and therefore there is no European settlement
to-day ; only Chow hovels are in evidence.
‘ ‘ Efforts are being made by the S. Australian
Government to boom the ‘ white elephant.’ 11. Y. L.
Brown, the Govt. Geologist, is here, there, and every-
where. A diamond drill is down 330 ft. at the Union, at
an angle of 45deg. Wells and party are off to the Victoria
River. Another party has left recently for the Rop r.
Yet mining in the N.T. is almost dead. Zapopan is let to
Chow tributors, who pay 10 per cent, and 10s. for crush-
ing. Up to date (Sept. 30) they have got 5700 z. in 36
days. The Northern Territory Mining and Smelting Co.
is a mystery. Daly River Govt, smelter is shut-down.
Chows are scratching at Bynoe Harbor and Pine Creek.
The Mining Act wants remodelling, and dummying for
Chows needs to be stopped before you can improve
matters in the N.T.”

The annual report of the Maori land Mines
Department shows the results of the work of 50 dredges
for four years:—
Gold. Capital
oz. Invested. Dividends.
1801 .. 44,914 £472,076 £45,359
1902 .. 71,315 348,801 107,506
1903 .. 54,162 395,429 81,177
1904 .. 59,613 356,784 85,226
Last year’s return thus averaged about 24 per cent, on
capital invested. But the return was very irregular.
Only 21 of the 50 dredges paid anything at all ; while the
Electric, on a capital of £26,000, paid £39,650, or over 150
per cent. After that the most profitable was the Rox-
burgh Jubilee, which paid £6750 on a capital of £7500.
Both these co.’s have continued exceedingly profitable
throughout the present year. The average profit to the
shareholders of all dredges furnishing particulars to the
department works out at about 28s. per oz. won.

Aboriginalities.
“ and only man IS VILE.”
[For Tub Bulletin.]
I’ve seen a bloke hauled up fer drivin’ ’orses what had
sores
On their shoulders, or their withers, or their backs,
And a sehool-marrn get a wiggin’ fer leath’rin’ cranky kids,
And a butcher fined fer over-ridin’ hacks ;
And I’ve heard the parsons preachin’ (not very often,
mind !)
’Bout the cruelty that’s rampant in this isle,
“ Where every prospect pleases,” an’ Nature’s always kind ;
Where everything’s rybuck “ but man is vile."
Now, you take them sore-backed horses an’ let Nature
have her way ;
Turn the prads out where v'.e man can’t make ’em work,
Nature traps ’em in a bog-hoie, and the sun shines just as
gay,
While they s' irves to d ;ath—my word ! she’ll never shirk
From playin' of a lo,/-down trick. Now blokes will
sometimes try
To do a little kindness for a mate,
But as far as I can gather, if you take things full an’ by,
You’ll find that Nature mostly deals in hate.
If a man ain’t drowned he’s earthquaked, an’ Provi-
dence ’ll grin
Oftentimes, and send a tidal wave along
To show how kind is Nature, and that man is full o’ sin,
An’ to prove that she is right and sin is wrong.
Heres some silly blokes,” says Nature, “under trees
out of the rain ;
I’ll stiffen ’em with lightnin’.” Then she laughs
An’ finds some kids in swimmin’; drowns ’em all, and
grins again
At a bloke who tries to save ’em, while she chaffs.
■*
P’raps a bush bloke’s doin’ decent, an’ has crops an’ kids
an’ stock,
An’ he’s kind to ’em, an’ they are fond of him ;
Nature reckons he shall prosper, an’ helps to fill his sock,
While she gets her teeth an’ claws in fightin’ trim.
Then she kills the kids with fever, rusts the crops an’
sends a drought ;
Dries the country so that she can help the fire,
And as she licks the land up and burns the cocky out,
She somehow makes the hymn-book out a liar.
Nature likes to see a kitten slowly paw a mouse to death
(If she didn’t she’d ha’ never taught it how).
An’ to watch a crow pick eyes out while a jumbuck’s still
got breath,
An’ a snake to bite a cocky’s only cow.
If these is “ pleasant prospects ’’—well, I’ve got no more
to say;
But you study Nature’s note-book for awhile
And you’ll find, if you are nimble with yer eyes from day
to day,
That other things besides a bloke is vile.
Claude Jewell.

BLACK BILL: “I say, young feller, me an' Mike
is just goin’ to settle a little difference of
opinion atween us, Markis o’ Queensbery
rules, an' we wants yer to tear us apart when
we gets inter holts, see!"
“A.D.”: A writer in a recent Bulletin
asks how it is that young fruit trees, growing in
the bush, are always free from pests. Plum,
peach, and citous seedlings will thrive in the
bush only up to a certain point. They perish
from want of sun and root-moisture. The far-
reaching gum and stringy-bark roots suck up the
available nourishment. The seedling thrives in
the loos« sub-soil, but when its roots develop, and
it starts to suck the hard, untilled clay, it meets
trouble. The constant dripping of moisture from
the eucalyptus probably frees the bush seedling
from pests; also it blights with the strength of
sulphuric acid the fruit when it starts to appear.
“Kaira” : I’ve been reading a lot about
wild-bee chasing, and thought I’d have a go
myself. I chose a biv bumble-bee for the object
of my chase, because 1 could see him further than
the smaller species, and because, if I lost sight of
him, I might still hear him buzz. After waiting
for a few minutes for him to drink his fill, I found
he could hold more than my patience would
permit, so I shooed him off the premises. He
made it easy going for me across my paddock.
Then I had to trespass on other people’s pad-
docks, and disturb other people’s dogs and cattle.
I had him well in hand till we came to the closer
settlement area, where the fences blocked me
considerably. We next came to a Chow’s garden,
and over the spring onions I flew like a scalded
cat, while Bung Lung, shouting “muck a hi,”
was a bad third with the pitchfork. When we
came to the garden plots the pursued stopped to
take in more cargo, but by the way he was puffing
I saw it was only a ruse for a spell, so I shook
him up again. We next came to a decline, and I
had him at the end of my nose; in fact, if he had
stopped to scratch himself we must have tele-
scoped. He put on a spurt, and was getting
away from me now, and I had to keep my eye
straight on him. He took a half-left turn ; I did
likewise. He went straight on, and I tripped
over a dog with my feet and struck a box with my
head. The box was a beehive. I took refuge in
a waterhole, and a thick, indignant blanket of
bees settled over it looking for me. But I tracked
my insect all right.

“H.S.R.” writes:—
Ths Bullktin does not appear to have received the
prospectus of the Rockmelons’ Standard Soul Trust,
which is bound to cause a revolution in the sky-pilot
business. The idea is to build automata resembling
parsons after the manner of marionettes, and with a
newly-invented tape talking-machine, which will run for
half an hour, do the preaching now so expensively done
by parsons of all breeds of religious thought. It is
expected that any congregation can be run by this
machine for about £25 per annum, including the royalty
to the Standard Soul Trust on the invention. For a
sn all extra charge the Trust will fit up one of its patent
automatic collection-plate outfits. You can order any
breed of parson, from hard-shell Baptist to coon re-
vivalist, or Torrey and Alexander style. New tapes can
he had at so much per yard, and the sermons, prayers and
hymns can be turned on by quite inexperienced persons.

“Two Seas,” on a much-debated beast:—
I lived in dingo-haunted country for some years, and
my experience says that dingoes do bark—to a limited
extent. They give three or four sharp barks, followed
by a blood-curdling howl unlike the cry of any other
animal on earth. It was the only sound in the bush that
disturbed my sleep and brought me out of my bunk look-
ing for my gun, and I can still hear ilsometimes in my
dreams. I have seen some mention of a “ tame ” dingo.
Nonsense ! You can’t tame a dingo—not even a half-
bred dingo. I’ve tried often, and know it, and have fed
pups myself—allowing no one else to do so—and they
would never cease to snap at my hand if I came too close.
I tied a slut up one night as a decoy when “laying” for
a dingo. Went to sleep—result, a fine litter of dingo
pups. Trained the quietest of them to work sheep, and
took her on a droving trip. But she was never tame.
When travelling to the station where I was to take
delivery of the sheep, she followed myself and mates, but
we rarely got a glimpse of her on the road. Keeping
about one hundred yards behind us, she would stop and
slink behind a tree or a log if one of us pulled up and
looked back. When we took delivery of the sheep she
worked well all day on her own—watching one wing of
the mob —trotting up and down and turning °the
stragglers in quite noiselessly ; but if I spoke to her, or
took any notice at all of her work, she would slink out of
sight.
Don’t know what she lived upon. At night she never
came up to the camp like the other dogs at supper-
time, looking for her share of the bones ; but she was
always ready to do her silent work in the morning when
the sheep moved off camp—and she always did it alone.
If one of the other dogs came round her side she curled
up her top lip, which was a hint seldom ignored. She
was never friendly, although I had her for many years.

“C.X.Gr.”: Queensland keeps an expen-
sive vermin fence going on the 8. A. border. Why,
Heaven only knows. Riding along it a short time
ago I saw more rabbits on the Queensland
side than in S.A. As for the dingoes, where the
fence is a bit low they jump it in their stride. One
of the boundary-riders showed me a pad coming
ud to the fence on the one side and continuing on
the other, made entirely by dogs. He also told me
that whenever the netting had the least bit of a
sag on it the rabbits would climb it and drop on the
other, side. I believed him, for I saw one or two
dead ones suspended in the netting.

“Krex”; “C.X.G.” (B. 7/9/’O5) is
going “on the blind,” or does not know what he
is talking about, when he says that it was a rare
thing to see a dingo on Strzlecki Creek, a few
years back, when the S.A. Government was
paying for scalps. In 1900, when the bounty was
3s. a scalp, they could be seen just as frequently
as at present; and a mail-horse-minder at Del-
linghri, 20 miles from Innamincka on the Strzlecki,
by poisoning a dead horse half-a-mile from his
camp, scalped 35 in two nights. The dingoes
even used to eat their poisoned mates, turning the
skin inside out, just as a hawk does a rabbit.
“ C.X.G.’s” sarcastic reference to Queenslanders
as a painfully accurate people would be funny if
the drivers of the Strzlecki mail for the past 18
months were not South Australians. I know
them personally. The yarn about dogs chewing
hobble straps on horses is an old camp-fire lie to
illustrate the temerity of the animals, but it is
getting rather grey now. Hope this truth flies up
and hits “C.X.G.” in the right place, which in
his case seems to be almost any place.

“ Kodak ” : The tall, mild, quiet man
with the trustful brown eyes cleared his throat:
“Once, when I kept fowls, a little incident hap-
pened which revealed to me the hitherto”—
“Five to one Hitherto!” muttered someone at
the bar—“ unexpected cunning of the ordinary
every-day variety of fowl,” concluded the quiet
man, looking round the room. “One day I
noticed a hen limping rather badly, so after a
while I fixed it up with a bread poultice. The
hen seemed to like it and it did her a lot of good
—until she pecked the bandage off and tasted the
poultice. Then she and her friends made a meal
of it. Next morning when I went out I found
every hen in the place limping!" There was a
pause. Then a faint voice asked for beer.

“Pilio”: Par (14/9/’O5) headed “What
you see at Australia’s back-door step” isn’t
accurate. First, Cavanagh-street is not the main
street of Port Darwin, and, in addition to the white
saddler mentioned, it has a white pub run by a
white Australian and his white family, a white
painter, also a white sub-collector of customs and
white family, a white railway super and his white
ditto, also a white school-mistress with a some-
what speckled class-list. And there are others.
The municipal laborers are white, but contracts
are open to Chinese. The jetty cost £BO,OOO, not
£lO,OOO, and took two years over the estimate to
build—a good deal of colored labor was employed.
There’s not a Chinese clerk in the Territory. The
cable station does not employ a Chinese engineer.
In fact, the par referred to is so grossly inaccurate
and exaggerative as to be beyond criticism. Any
white man who cares to work can find a living
here. The mongrel Asiatic may be a curse, but
an additional curse is the white man who arrives
here penniless, and with a disinclination tc earn
a penny. We’ve had several of these lately.
They were treated well too ; but one, at least, has
evidently expended part of his borrowings in
stationery on which to libel this unhappy land.
Yes, the Chow is a curse here —some white men
are a curse anywhere. Send the right sort, and
we’ll keep his end up.

“L.L.8.”: There were two brothers,
neighboring cookies. One kept pigs, the other
didn’t. The swine of the brother who did got into
the crop of the brother who didn’t, and did for it.
The brother who didn’t sought liquidated damages
therefor. The brother who owned the pigs that
did told him he had no hope, and that it wasn’t
the fault of the swine but of the fences, and that if
the fences, &c., &c. Each told his brother “other
things ”as well. The other day I met the pigless
brother and listened attentively to his tale.
“Nonsense, Jim,” said I, “what do you intend to
do ?” “Do ! ” said he, “ nothing now. But just
you wait a while —wait till mother’s dead.”

“ C.X.G.” : Afghans have some quaint
ideas about animal diet. Tumbled across one the
other day. He had 101 b. or 121 b. of butter melted
into a gallon jar. Asked him what he was going
to do with it, and he explained it was for the stud
bull camel. Said it made him very strong.
On another occasion I hit on one doctoring
a sick camel with mutton broth, which, he
explained, was one of the best things
going for sick camels. Judging by the ex-
pression of contempt on that camel’s face
and the noise he made, I don’t think he
quite fell in with Rusty Khan’s ideas on
the subject.
Manifesto recently posted up by a N.S.W.
farmer :
Take notice, I warn all persons, especially Protestants,
off my land ; and caution everyone not to call at my
house upon any excuse.
I want no person’s acquaintance, and request everyone
not to speak to me, to prevent false charges being faked
up.
The last canard is colorable escobarderie. Owners of
stock on my land must remove them within seven days
from date.
H. Blank Smith.

The female inspectorofVic. school-teachers’
back-blocks residences, lately appointed by Bent,
is telling some interesting things. The other day
she recommended that a way-back school should
have a male teacher instead of the girl at present
stationed there, “as there is in the whole district
no suitable accommodation for a young girl, there
being a total absence, of sanitary provision, and
the residents failing generally to recognise that
any improvement is needed. But,” the report
closes, “ these dirty people are well off for horses
and buggies.” Which is a charming little piece
of criticism of the “poor farmer” when you look
at it.

not one of the convicts. The old nigger-driver had
much coarse hair on her expansive chest, and
sneezed like a man ; but she was, they said, a very
highly-connected lady all the same.”

“ F.H.R.” : There is a collection of
humpies on the Coonamble line called Balla-
doran. The principal industry is sleeper-chop-
ping, which is carried on for about nine months
every year for the Railway Department by con-
tract. Now, why can’t the Department simply
open the depot and let the men cut independently
at a fair price all the year round ? By insisting on
well-cut sleepers they would get the best of cutters,
and instead of a lot of humpies, which have to be
abandoned part of the year under the present
arrangement, a flourishing little town would spring
up. Rolling stones gather no shekels for them-
selves or the Railway Department. What we
want more than anything in this country is con-
stant work, so that people can settle down. I
haven’t been long enough in one place for the last
seven years to get my hair cut twice in it.

“Nulla”: Lately I bought some fowls.
J like poultry about the place. My fowls are of
the tame, Paddy’s Market type, and they do
their duty on the six working days, with an occa-
sional double-yolked egg to make up for Sunday,
Yet whenever I sing their virtues to members of
the fraternity I am met by the supercilious re-
joinder : “ Ah, yes ! not too bad. But you should
have some o’ mine” (procured at enormous expense
from England, Ireland, or elsewhere). _ “ Then
you’d have something to talk about.” I lie enthu-
siastically on behalf of my beloved barndoors, but
alas ! to no purpose, and it really seems that
unless I can get a fowl from behind the Hima-
layas or some place equally remote, I can never be
a full-blown member of the Most Powerful
Order of Perjured Poulterers.

28/9/’O5). Trucking some valuable mares, one got
her fore-feet caught in the door of the waggon. In
her struggles she fell between the loading stage
and truck, the weight of her body literally tearing
both hoofs off. Smearing the feet plentifully with
Stockholm tar, we sewed them up m pieces ot oat
sack. After being turned out for some months
new- hoofs formed and she was used for bringing
up the cows and working horses on the station tor
years, rearing several good foals before her cleatn.
Drover John O’Sullivan started from the
Gulf last April to overland 884 bullocks to Bunda-
berg, 1316 miles, dropping 200 of his charge at
Richmond en route. He only lost three beasts,
although the party encountered stages of 84 and 75
miles absolutely devoid of herbage, and one of 44
miles without water.

“Pot Jostler, Jun.” : It is not generally
known that in Australia we have a unique speci-
men of the seal tribe (0. lobala). It is a hair seal,
but on the young cub is a distinct growth of under
fur, which disappears as the seal grows old. They
are becoming extinct, which is a pity, as they
form the connecting link between the hair and
fur seals. A few specimens are still to be seen in
King George’s Sound, but I do not know of any
other place in Australia where they congregate.
In years gone by numbers could be found on
Seal Rock, off Port Stephens (N.S.W.), but they
no longer frequent that spot.

It is alleged that turkeys do exceptionally
well on pricldy-pear. They will thrive on it with
very little additional food. This is the experience
of farmers in the pear-cursed country in Queens-
land. It is good news, but all the turkeys in the
universe could hardly keep down the prickly-pear
of one State. It has become a case of eating
forests from the root up.

“The Still- Walker ” : A good way to get
rid of a jumper or bulldog-ants’nest that is a little
too close to the humpy or tent to be pleasant, is to
get a piece of fresh raw meat, dip it in sugar, and
put it on the nest to be disposed of. In about two
minutes little black meat and sugar ants swarm all
over the meat and all over the nest. As soon as
a bull ant gets to windward of a sugar ant, and
gets a whiff, he’s off to tell his mates to shift camp
as a Lands Commission or a Butter Inquiry is
coming near. Next day there is not a bull or
jumper within miles of that nest. Did ever you
smell one of those big sugar ants? Never do if
you haven’t; because—phew !

“ Colm ” : Brown was off-sider
for the wood-hauler at a Queensland
sugar-mill. He came to my hut early
one morning breathing religion and
last night’s rum. Said he: “Sam’l,
it’s all right what the parson bloke
says about them miracles.” I gently
indicated my desire that he should
start at once for Sheol, but he per-
sisted thusly: “I bought a tin of
fresh ’errin’s at the store for tea, and
when I got to camp and opened it I
knew the parson bloke was right. I’d
never been able to swaller that yarn
about feedin’ the multitude with a few
small fishes, but, Sam’l, that tin of
’errins would have been too much for
fifty thousand multitudes. After they’d
had all they wanted there’d ’ave been
a whole tinful left.”

“ Wilbea u : Even in British Papua the
Ghinaman is at a discount. In 1858 the St. Paul,
from Hongkong to Sydney, was wrecked off Rus-
sell Island with 327 yellow men aboard. The de-
generate Papuan couldn’t see the force of letting
so much fresh meat go to waste, and used up the
aliens at the rate of three or four a day. The
islanders didn’t have to kill any pigs for over 80
days.
The patient camel, being a pleasant yam
from Westralia :
At Deep Well, Murchison (W. A.), on the afternoon of
6th Oct., Constable Dillon discovered in the bush the
mutilated body of the Afghan, Ameer Jan, who had been
missing for more than a week. Ameer Jan left his camp
on September 22 to round up a mob of milking camels,
and had not boon seen again. As a mad bull camel was
running loose near Deep Well in a frenzied state of
“must” and had taken charge of the milkers, other
Afghans considered it just as well to leave the mild-eyed
stock alone. Also in the circumstances they considered it
necessary to arm themselves with rifles and search for
Ameer Jan on horseback. They found the “ must ” old
bull, and as he was flecked with gouts of dried blood
about the chest and legs they were quite satisfied that
Ameer Jan was in Paradise. So they reported die matter
to the police at Cue, and from their praying mats in
the evening breathed a promise that they would
level up affairs with the bull when he got over his
tantrums. Dillon reported that the top of the
man’s head had been bitten off, the face was torn
away, and the left hand was missing. The body was
trodden into shapelessness, and the ground around it was
torn up with the violence of the brute’s fury. It isn’t
often a “must” bull-camel gets such a chance as this to
show what he really can do when he tries. The man who
potters around among camels in the cool months without
a loaded rifle courts his fate, and even so armed he wants
to be ready to fire promptly, for bulls on season arc
frequently cunning enough to lie in wait and show every
sign of amiability till the opportunity for murder arrives.
Cue is or was the most camel-cursed town in W.A.
Once before a mad bull that was blowing horrible red
blue-veined bladders out of his mouth in Austin-street,
knocked off his obscene caper long enough to bite the top
off the head of a passing Afghan, and then continued his
lewd pastime, while some other Afghans pulled the corpse
out of range. Some time afterwards that camel was shot
by a white camel-man while in the act of trying to
destroy a bullock-camel, and incidentally the white man
himself. About a dozen Afghans swore in court that
the deceased was a nice, kind, quiet camel that wouldn’t
hurt a worm. He was so tender-hearted that once when
a hen was killed he went and sat on the eggs himself.
The white man got mulct in damages, but an awful lot of
“ backsheesh ” changed hands over the case.
* * * *
“ Nellie G.” : Can anyone tell me why, in
the bush, “cow ” as an epithet is applied with such
contempt? If a ntan quarrels with his mate, if his
horse is shying, jibbing or misbehaving itself gene-
rally, in each case “cow” as a term of abuse is
equally applicable and insulting. To the feminine
mind this seems rather ridiculous, for, as a matter
of fact, the cow, of all animals, is the most deserv-
ing of approbation, the most dutiful and well-be-
haved. Perhaps, however, that is the reason for
the contumely with which man uses the term. Of
course the cow isn’t exactly a gay and dashing
beast, and that is, of all things, what the average
man desires to be. If you want to make him your
enemy for life, you have only to insinuate that you
think him harmless and tractable. The other day
I was watching a bullocky admonish a stubborn
leader. He used the whip freely and employed
the most forcible words in his comprehensive
and expressive vocabulary, all to no avail. At
last he called the animal a “cow”—a simple, un-
adjectived cow. It turned and regarded him re-
proachfully, with tears in its eyes, and then pro-
ceeded with a chastened spirit. A horse has been
known to commit suicide and a dog to run amok
after being called a cow, and if you try the asper-
sion on the house cat it is ten to one she will put
dead rats in the water-tank.
THE BILLYCANNIA ROAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From port and port the seaboard round
To arid central plain ;
From hut and homestead all the way
To There and Back Again ;
From Everywhere to Nowhere runs
The Billyeannia Road—
The weird pleasaunce of travellers
Who own no fixed abode.
A thirsty crew from Pint-o’-Beer ;
A crowd from Two-to-One ;
A sleepy lot from Lazy-Bones ;
A rich man’s only son.
A drifting host from Got-no-Push ;
A few from Sheer-bad-Luck ;
With odds and ends from God-knows-Where,
Make up the shiftless ruck.
Year in, year out, they saunter through
The land of Let-it-Slide,
And kindly all their wants allow
The Lord-knows-Whom provide.
Misfortune’s frown is half a smile
Unto that careless brood,
The very ills of life they grow
To look upon as good.
No man is king among them all,
But each man equals each ;
That which the new chum has to learn
The old hand loves to teach.
True Socialists, they share the luck
From day to day bestowed ;
A happy free republic is
The Billyeannia Road.
N.S.W.
W.L.D.
