The Lion sat on the white chalk cliffs, looked o’er the
foaming blue,
And sucked the tip of a thoughtful tail with a kind of
doubtful chew.
As the Jappy ships came steaming on in their cocky,
uppish way,
He swallowed a lump with a kind of gulp and went to say
“ Good day ! ”
The Lion said to the little Jap he hadn’t done too bad,
The tune he’d played to the dancing Bear was one that
made him glad ;
But the Monkey got some rooster quills, and fixed them
in a row
To the freer end of his long backbone, and then began to
crew.


“The cook was a great tyrant at
Government House. No one except
Lady Jersey dared make suggestions to
him. When she was once away, the
cook served midday dinner for a week
for Lord Ancram, Mr. Goschen, the
English governess, the German gover-
ness, and the four children. A cutlet
fpr each person, and one over, was the
fare.—S./W. Herald.
Her son, then a boy of seven,
was coached in French, Latin, Ger-
man, and English. He found it
difficult to spell in all languges
correctly. The boy looked a dignified
little mite in tall hat and Eton suit.”
S. M. Herald.
“ Lady Jersey’s actions in her home
life were well-planned and systemati-
cally carried out. History she thought
the best study for girls.
Her young daughters,
when in Sydney, learned
English history con-
tinuously.”— S.M, <

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Peter Close, it is said, vacates his position
in Sydney and goes to London shortly.
Someone said of the late Edward Langton
(Vic.), in his mid-way political career, that “he
rose from a position of precarious importance to
one ol assured insignificance. ”
Pauli, the mining director who won the
Charters Towers seat as an Independent, went to
the Opposition cross-benches when he entered Q.
Assembly.
Justice Heydon, of N.S.W., is a bitter
man. The other day, when a barrister wanted
time, his honor refused, telling him, if he hadn’t
read up the case, to fall back upon his natural
intelligence.
In 1877 William Overend, of Terang (Vic.),
passed out leaving an estate valued for probate at
£324. On Monday, 23rd inst., Justiee Holroyd
was applied to for leave to deal with a sum of over
£4OOO realised from the sale of a portion of the real
estate.
One of the proprietors of a daily newspaper
which condemns betting on horse races, but pub-
lishes the betting lists, defended the position, the
other day at Randwick, by asking, “Should a
newspaper which condemns a Government refuse
to publish the speeches of its members ? ”
The death of John Curtain, member of the
Vic. Leg. Assembly from ’7O to ’77, recalls that he
once electrified the House by saying, “ Of course,
Mr. Speaker, we are all tending towards the same
jail.” The House guessed what he meant, but
some members wished he hadn’t said it.
Thomas Walker, journalist, spiritualist,
and many other things, who for some time repre-
sented Northumberland after a hazy and scram-
bling fashion in the N.S. Wales Assembly, has
been elected on the Labor ticket as Parliamentary
blatherer for Kanowna by a more than two to one
vote.
Australia has had some shady politicians,
but when was an Australian member or ex-
member charged, like Watt, with conspiracy to
murder? Also it is difficult to remember when
one got 14 years, like Jabez Balfour. At his
worst the Australian M.L.A. has been mostly a
short sentence man.
Ages of Victorian Supreme Court Judges
with date of appointment:—John Madden, aged
61; appointed 1893. Edward Dundas Holroyd
will be 78 in January ; appointed 1881. Thomas
A’Beckett, 68; appointed 1886. Henry Edward
Agincourt Hodges, birth uncertain, called to the
Bar 1873, became a Judge in 1890. John Henry
Hood, aged 60 in June; became a Judge in 1891.
Captain Crowther, recently imprisoned by
the Moors, and whose life was for a few days
worth anything from twopenn’orth of gin to a
principality, is a brother of Mr. S. H. Crowther,
of Dungog (N.S. W.). Another brother is colonel
of a regiment in India, and a fourth of the family
(Major Crowther) died recently in Maoriland.
They are grand-nephews of Nelson.
The smallest member of the S. A. Assembly
is lawyer Mitchell. He was speaking of the evils
of cigarette smoking and narrating his own
experience when Archibald sardonically inter-
jected : “ They stunted your growth all right.”
Some people allege that Mitchell’s hatred of
the evil thing dates from the time when some-
one said that he and his cigarette made two sides
of a square.
PUGILISM DISCUSSED IN N.S.W.
PARLIAMENT.
“ It's a dirty, low subject, and I'm sur-
prised that it should be brought up in such
company. No decent man in his...senses,”
&c., &c.
A few November birthdays : Admiral
Rawson, 620 n the 4th ; President Stirling, M.L.C.
(S.A.), 56 on the sth ; John See, 54, and W.
M'Millan, 55 on the 14th ; Senator Guthrie, 47 on
the 17th ; Lord Northcote, 59 on the 18th ; Arch-
bishop O’Reily (S.A.), 59 on the 19th; Senator
Dobson, 64 on the 24th ; Senator Playford, 68 on
the 25th ; Mark Twain, 70 on the 30th.
Even that pushing, brainy, little woman’s
advocate, Vida Goldstein, seems to have other
yearnings at times. The other day she told the
National Council of Women that she hoped a cer-
tain committee would get through its business
speedily. “Life,” she pleaded, “seems to me
one long succession of committee meetings.”
“ H.” : It’s as good as a feast to see Vic.
Premier Bent at one. He goes through all the
items on the menu as if they were clauses in a
favorite Bill, and the onlookers can hear him
muttering to himself. “Clause one, Horse
Duffers. That Horse Duffers stand as printed;
carried. Clause two, Soup; sub-clause one,
Turtle. That sub-clause Turtle stand as printed ;
carried. Sub-clause two, Con-som. That sub-
clause Con-som stand as printed ; carried.” And
so on. True to Parliamentary procedure, Bent
leaves the preamble till the last, and then empties
the dish of olives on his plate, until there is a
regular Mount of Olives before him. It is one of
the most interesting things in practical politics to
see Bent reduce a hostile majority of olives to
submission.
Premier Carruthers stated in N.S.W.
Assembly last week that it was Captain Cook
who discovered Australia. Even Thomas Bent
knows more than that at times. If there is going'
to be a race between the two over the barren
wastes of ignorance, The Bulletin will be almost
inclined to back Thomas, provided he promises
not to sing by the way.
Possibly the oldest Australian native is
Willia-m Byrne, of Princes-street, Sydney, who was
born in 1808, the year of the rum revolution, or
Bligh rebellion, and celebrated his 97th birthday
last week. He was in business as a bootmaker on
Brickfield Hill in the thirties, when the old toll-
bar stood near the intersection of George and
Goulburn streets. The Byrnes are a long-lived
family ; three of the six brothers and sisters died
respectively at 79, 87, and 94, and a fourth within
five months of the century.
You can’t kill some men. Shannon, the
watchman at the Queen Cross battery, Charters
Towers, whom the recent explosion didn’t blow
up, used to be a Gympie pugilist, and the punch-
lngs he got then were baby-taps to what he has had
since. At the notorious Tarangaba mine, near
Yeppoon (Q.), a shot accidentally exploded, tear-
ing his arm. In 1900, timber fell on him in the
Kelly’s Queen Block mine, ripping the other arm
and crushing his ribs. A year later, in the same
mine, a shot drove a GJoz. stone into his chest.
And he is still moving round looking for trouble.
Journalist Thos. Corrigan, who recently
died, aged 79, at Subiaco (W.A.), began his
Australian career on the Mercantile Adver-
tiser, a Sydney commercial print owned by
J. F. Burns. Next he joined the Cumberland
Argus, with John Ferguson and the late Garnet
Walch as colleagues, and later on helped to crack
the Stockwhip. In the early 70’s he went to
Newcastle, where he stood by the Pilot for
awhile, and afterwards became part proprietor,
with Hugh M ‘Dicken, of the Chronicle. In
April, 1876, the Newcastle Herald was born,
and Corrigan became its editor. The Chronicle
died the same year.
Miss Hordern and Mr. Anthony Hordern
were, atlatest advices, with other Australians visit-
ing the lakes of Killarney. This is Anthony
Hordern the fourth. It is just 83 years since the
first Anthony Hordern appeared in Sydney. His
wife started a millinery shop in King-street,
between Pitt and Castlereagh streets, Anthony
the first doing business as a coach-builder
next door. The second Anthony became a
draper in George-street. The third followed up
the business, adding to it large railway specula-
tions in Westralia, in the midst of which fie died.
It is his son who has lately been considering
the Irish scenery.
Editor Philip Mennell, of the British
Australasian, recently dead in London, was a
native of Newcastle-on-Tyne. He was admitted
as an English solicitor in 1875, but abandoned the
law and came to Australia, where he trod the
Inky Way. He founded the Bairnsdale (Vic.)
Advertiser, and in 1882 became sub-editor of
Melbourne Age. Later he returned to London
and for six years was connected with the Age’s
cable agency there. He made three further visits
to Australia—in 1891 as London Daily Chronicle
correspondent; in 1895 as Times ditto; and in
1900 for the Morning Post. His best known
literary work is his “ Dictionary of Australasian
Biography,” besides which he published “The
Coming Colony,” a series of Westralian articles
reprinted from the Daily Chronicle ; a bio-
graphy of Lord John Manners, and a volume en-
titled “In Australian Wilds.”
There is an elderly business lady in a
Victorian country town who has never been a
biscuit-toss from her front door for over 30 years.
They erected a clock and band rotunda in the
town with much noise five years ago, and
she is the one person in the district who
hasn’t seen it, though it is only in the next
block. The other day, while making
out her accounts, a bright glare
attracted her eye, and looking
through the window she saw a
house in flames. Calling a boy
in the shop to go and ring the
firebell, she returned placidly
to her ledgers. She didn’t
even go out to look at the fire.
“Gr.” : K.C.M.Gr. -eese
are_ not the only people who
delight in looting the alphabet.
A girl I know signs all her
correspondence, private and
otherwise, Glorianna Maggs,
A.T.C.L., said letters inform-
ing all and sundry that Miss
Maggs is the proud possessor of some small musi-
cal “degree.”
The Right Rev. A. F. Winnington-
Ingram, Bishop of London, who has been de-
nouncing the fall in the British birth-rate and
“ the miserable doctrine of comfort” as the cause
of it, is an unmarried and childless person aged
47. His address is at Fulham Palace, and he
belongs to two clubs, and his favorite recreations
are golf, cycling and fives. He tries to dodge the
more aggravated forms of discomfort on £lO,OOO a
year. In his more frivolous moments he signs
himself “A. F. London.”
“ H.” : Premier Bent of Victoria has two
tags to his speeches that never fail him. Since
coming into office, he has never yet addressed a
public gathering without remarking, “ And now,
with good seasons and good rains and the blessings
of divine Providence, we can look forward to
great prosperity for this great State,” finishing up
with, “May you all live long and prosper.”
Lately, Tommy is starting to turn on the loyal
tap, and, for the last 34 speeches, has introduced
after “this great State,” the sentence, “and it is
a great State —with all these millions of acres
given to us by our Good King without a blister.
I say without a blister. By our Good King and
his mother, the Good Queen. May you all live
long and prosper.”
Ex-Judge Boucaufc, of S.A., has brought,
out his long-planned book on “The Arab, the
Horse of the Future,” with a preface by Walter
Gilbey, to show that Australia is about the best
country in the world for breeding purposes. The
old Judge has not lost his trick of vigorous
language :
I emphatically disclaim any desire in this book to
interfere with betting-, or in any way to denounce betting
or betting-men. They may bet till they are blind as far
as I am here concerned. They may risk a thousand
pounds to a penny as to which sparrow will first hop oil a
post. My object is to show on the authority of racing-
men themselves that the present style of betting, or the
present style of racing, is fast ruining, if it has not
already actually ruined, the English horse. . . . The
sprinter is a huge mistake, except for sprinting, and the
pure Arab of the desert is unequalled—also except for
sprinting—in any station of life in which it may please
God to call him, ... It will of course be said that I am
interested in the subject as an owner of Arabs, and there-
fore that I am not a reliable authority. That is right
enough. As I said, lam interested, and do not claim to
be an authority.
INSTRUCTIONS TO A DEPUTY-OPPOSI-
TION LEADER.
A favorite dodge of Deputy Opposition Leader Joe Cook
is to ask for a declaration of Government policy on any-
thing and everything.
REID: “ Get into this chair, and when you see
those two fellows trying to do the business of
the country just jibber and chatter like an
idiot. Any drivel will do. I'll be back anon.”
Premier Bent blows his own trumpet
with the assurance of a whole German band. In
the course of one short recent speech, the report
of which occupies about six inches in the Argus,
Tommy claimed that he “had read everything of
every clime,” that he could give a display of
calisthenics quite as good as that of the pretty
girls who had just performed, that he “knew ail
about Thunderbolt,” that he “ could recite well,”
that he was a good speller, that he once made a
lot of money for charities by lecturing on India,
and that he was a judge of paintings because he
was “an artist himself.” By this time Bent had
not nearly exhausted Bent, but he had exhausted
everybody else.
Elijah Bunn is an unusual kind of peti-
tioner at the door-mat of Maoriland Parliament.
Some time ago one Sewell, defendant in a small
civil case, blew himself up with dynamite in the
door-way of the courthouse at Murchison. Bunn’s
case is that Sewell was going to blow himself up
inside the court and slay everybody, that Bunn
got him outside so that the damage was miti-
gated, that Bunn was consequently close to
Sewell when Sewell blew up, and by reason of
that proximity he lost an eye in the disaster, and
that his services in saving his Worship and the
audience should be recognised. Which seems
very fair logic—even though petitioner’s name is
Bunn.
Mr. Walter Trafford, whose grievance
against the N.S.W. Pharmacy Board for refusing
to register him as a chemist is one of the matters
which occupy the attention of a Parliamentary Select
Committee, objects to The Bulletin’s inclusion of
that matter with others as “petty.” He points out
that the refusal of the Pharmacy Board to register
him tends to deprive him of his living ; that in a
court of law lie has successfully upheld the
right of his claim; that the Board refuses
to be bound by that verdict, and that his only
remedy now is a Select Committee. Certainly,
Mr. Trafford’s grievance (from an examination of
the papers) is a big one. The Pharmacy Board’s
attitude to him is singularly unjust, and there
should be a remedy. But that doesn’t vitiate the
argument, which was that Parliament should have
a small standing specially.-paid committee or
officer to inquire into such matters. Further,
Parliament shouldn’t be so foolish as to create a
board which is so much above the law that
special Parliamentary interference is needed to
correct its injustices.
Andrew Seton Chisholm, pastoralist, who
died at Goulburn (N.S.W.) lately, aged 72, was
the son of a Sydney native born in the city (or
town then) in 1796. His grandfather, James
Chisholm, is understood to have been connected
with the 102nd Regiment, and on retiring kept
the Thistle Inn, in George-street, where now
stand Bradley’s auction rooms. The house, which
had a garden in front, as most George-street houses
had in those days, afterwards became the head-
quarters of the Bank of New South Wales. The
first Chisholm is said to have secured the George-
street property as a matter of barter, one authority
giving 4h acres as the area. The price was 12doz.
port wine, six gallons gin, two pieces of broad-
cloth, 251bs. of American tobacco, one chest of tea,
two bags of sugar, one set of harness for gig, one
saddle and bridle, one single-barrelled fowling-
piece, two canisters of gunpowder and four bags
of shot. The military officer who sold his interest
in the land was considered to have had the best
of the bargain. The family was also much in-
terested in the Eveleigh estate, which Henry
Parkes purchased at a big figure for railway pur-
poses. The unearned increment did a good deal
for the Chisholm family.
Henry Weedon is the gaud-Mayor elect
of Melbourne city. He possesses a certain amount
of sense which he has shown in municipal politics
by a discreet silence when another man would
havebrayed. He was once upon a time a decorator
and perpetrated much more or less calamitous
adornment on the interior of Melbourne
Town Hall, the interior of the National
Museum, and on Government House when the pale
aristocratic Hopetoun came out to fete the Yorks.
Then Weedon got into some excellent mining
gambles and accumulated several sackfuls of
sovereigns. _ With them he bought a big photo-
graphic business and accumulated more sackfuls.
He was one of the syndicate that bought
the wrecked P. and O. liner Australia for
a song and made—and is still making—ridiculous
profits out of the deal. He is most noble grand
buckjumper of the mysterious order of Free-
masons, and a something or other of the Man-
chester Unity Oddfellows. These things all
help him to the. Mayoral chair. Besides,
Pleasance had found the game too expensive, and
couldn’t be induced to stand again. This left three
possibles—Burston, who makes money, and is a
colonel; Hennessey, who makes bread, and is a
husk; and Weedon. Burston was favorite, but
he scratched for half-a-dozen reasons, chief of
which is that he owns a jolly pretty daughter, and
wants to take her to the Cold Country. A care-
ful canvass of the council yielded only five votes
in Hennessey’s favor. So Hennessey retired,
and on the 9th November Henry Weedon will be
invested with the honor of spending a couple of
thousand sovereigns for the glory of the citv of
Melbourne.
Archpriest Therry,
whom the Balmain
(N.S.W.) Council has re-
fused to honor with a
local monument, was a
picturesque figure of the
old times, and did a lot
of pioneering work in the
attempt to civilise Bal-
main and other savage
dependencies of the Set-
tlement. He was the
first Government-accre-
dited R. C. priest to Aus-
tralia. Several had pre-
ceded him, some as con-
victs and others with par-
tial authorisation, but
Father Therry, as a con-
sequence of a debate in
the House of Commons,
carried with him the
despatch of the Earl of
Bathurst, entitling him to the full exercise
of his functions and the Imperial salary of
£lOO a year. He first attacked the savages, the
ancestors of the Balmaniacs, in a wooden
hut in Pitt street, Sydney, and, according to
accounts, he took sackfuls of hair off them and
applied plentiful soap to their exposed parts.
Some people never forgive. Government House
of the day called Tlierry, in effect, a socialist and
member of the Labor party, and stopped his
salary ; but the Imperial Government, on receipt
of the news, ordered the salary to be restored and
back payments to be made good. Father Therry
was spiritual boss, so far as the R.C. interest
went, of all Australia, and consequently had quite
a commodious parish. He was a bit literary, but
some of his manuscripts show that his spellng was
eccentric, and as he was an Irishman (born in
County Cork), of course he wote verses, most of
which are deservedly forgotten.
The motor car which carries Charlie Angas,
of S. A., round at a fiendish pace cost £lOOO. If
that car rushed past the grave of old John
Howard Angas, and he knew who was in it, he
would rise and throw clods after his son.
The name of the late Pat. M'Caughan,
remembered as Melbourne’s most decorative citi-
zen, cropped up t’other day at a sitting of the
Police Commission :
Robt. M'Hugh, ex-inspector of police, who retired in
May, 1903, after 32 years’ service, said that he had been
engaged in licensing work, and had been greatly inter-
fered with in his work. The late Patrick M'Caughan,
who owned an hotel in Flinders-street, came to witness
and asked him to help him to put the licensee, a man
named Anis, out of the hotel. Witness turned up the
register and found that there was nothing there to
justify his interference. Mr. M'Caughan demanded that
witness should interfere, and witness replied, “ I won’t
do it. If he has committed a breach of the Act we will
take action, but I will not do it on mere hear-
say.” Mr. M'Caughan became quite offended, and
left. Apparently he saw Mr. O’Callaghan, as shortly
afterwards witness was called into "the Chief Com-
missioner’s office. Mr. O’Callaghan complained that he
had been offensive to Mr. M‘Caughan. Witness detailed
the conversation that had taken place, and Mr. O’Callaghan
said, “Yes, it practically agrees with what he complains
of, but I don’t think it is wise for you to run counter to
Mr. M’Caughan, who is a very wealthy and influential
man.” The end was that witness got a lecture, and was
told that he should be more courteous, more polite and
more conciliatory, that he should not offend Mr.
M'Caughan in particular, as he was a man of very consider-
able influence.

A. R. Addison, M.L.C., of S. Australia,
used to fill the Anglican pulpit in a country town
every Sunday till he heard that a lady parishioner
had openly stated she would rather stay at home
for the term of her natural life than hear him
preach. The following Sunday he frankly told
his congregation that thereafter they would have
to get the local cash draper or the chemist to don
the night-gown on alternate Sundays, he having
decided to hold forth but once a fortnight so that
the lady parishioner might have a chance to
dodge him.

ASSURANCE, SOCIETY LIMITED.
REPORT BY THE DIRECTORS in connection with the
SIXTH ACTUARIAL INVESTIGATION of the Affairs of the Society.
THE Directors have the pleasure to present to the Members their Report in connection with the Actuarial Investigation for the Quinquen-
nium ended 31st December, 1904, together with the Report of the Actuary of the Society.
In the previous Quinquennial Report the Directors stated that they deemed it desirable in the interests of the Society, in view
of the fall in the rate of interest throughout the world, to make provision for valuing on a per cent, basis, and the present valuation has
accordingly been made strictly on the basis of that rate. Since the last Investigation a new Table of Mortality has been compiled, designn ted
the Om, which is reckoned to be the most reliable experience of mortality among assured lives. The effect of its employment in the valuation, as
compared with the former standard used, is to increase the reserve liability. They are gratified to be able to announce that notwithstanding
the decrease in the assumed rate of interest, and the employment of a new Table of Mortality involving increased reserves, the surplus dis-
closed by the Actuary’s investigation at the end of the Quinquennium, amounts to the substantial sum of £261,541 6S 6d.
After a careful and complete investigation of all the securities on the books of the Society they decided to recommend that out of
the disclosed surplus a sum of £50,000 be set aside for addition to the Investment Fluctuation Fund, bringing it up to £123,858 3s. 9d.
An amount of £211,541 6s. 6d. is thus left for appropriation, and they recommend that this sum be divided amongst policies
entitled to participate.
During the period under review the Directors have continued the policy previously adopted of consolidating the business and
strengthening the position of the Society, the increase to the number of policies existing being over 3000, and the increase to the premium
income over £20,000.
They have to record with deep regret the death, on the 9th September, 1905, of Mr. Robert Thomson, F.1.A., who was connected
with the Society since its inception, originally as Actuary, and afterwards as Consulting Actuary. They desire to place on record their appre-
ciation of the services he rendered to the Society.
They congratulate the Members on the strong position of the Society in respect to its reserves, and look forward with hopeful antici-
pation to a considerable improvement in the Bonus results in the future.
HENRY HENTY, Chairman.
LESLIE J. PARK, General Manager.
Melbourne, 18th October , 1905.

A WOMAN'S LETTR.
Sydney October 31, 1905.
My Dear Arini,—
Henceforth, if you are one who “considers
herself a lady,” and if you are about to marry,
you will decide upon a quiet wedding. All the
best people in England who are marrying, and
the majority of the wise virgins here, are foregoing
as much as possible the old-fashioned and rather
vulgar “ splash ” of an ostentatious wedding cere-
mony. There is also an increasing tendency to do
away with even the reception, and it is becoming
more and more common to read notices of
weddings with only the immediate relatives
of the contracting parties present. Even
the wedding dress is often dispensed with,
the travelling dress being substituted. The pair
that have been Breathed upon don’t want to spend
a tithe of their honeymoon cutting awful cake and
listening to speeches when they’ve got a walking
tour, or motor journey, or fishing expedition on
hand.
Yesterday the first petal of the trefoil of naval
weddings was pulled off. Miss Moseley, of
Neutral Bay, married a Euryalus minor officer
named Townsend, one of whose glories is that of
being son of an admiral. Miss Gollin’s wedding
to another man-o’-war, one Blackwood, follows
to-day. He is a sprout of the Dufferin
family. And Miss Reading, to Surgeon
Mummery, occurs to-morrow. The Flagship
carries these days a chronic wreath slung
high above its funnel. Miss Jennie Brennan and
her assistants, who went on board with the morn-
ing milk to put the hundred yards of wreathing
round the hoop-iron that skeletons that wreath,
were afterwards entertained by the presently-to-
be bridegrooms. Wondering trippers on the ferry
boats—“persons not in society”—think it is a
new form of signalling, especially as it is lighted
up at night. There is also, I’m told (on unreliable
authority), a knot of orange blossom on the
anchor. One of the strongest reasons why we
should have a fleet of our own lies in the fact that
our girls might then marry the captain of the first-
class cruiser Boggabri or the first brass-bound
Australian lieutenant of the Thargomindah, and
so keep the good Australian money in its own
country.
The Flag-Lieutenant, on behalf of Lady Fan-
shawe, is requesting a large number of Somebodies
and Anybodies to R.S.V.P. to invitations to an
“At Home” to be given by the Lady-o’-war on
Thursday.
A reverend gentleman named Taylor has re-
lighted that ancient fire of controversy, the do-
mestic servant problem, and made it again a burn-
ing and a crackling institution. Controversy
between mistress and maid rages in the D.T.
more furiously than the heathen, who, I believe,
are often too lazy to rage. Servants’ complaints
are mainly of long hours—l 4 on an average—over-
work, insufficient time for meals, an almost abso-
lute lack of leisure, and they demand that the
eight-hours system be made to apply. The
missuses who reply are solid in their assertions
of considerate treatment of their employees.
Maids taking part in the discussion con-
tradict one another. Tne efficient general
housemaid or cook need not stay in a situation
where she is treated with such injustice, say some
of the less hostile Mary Janes. A hundred
considerate employers yearn for her services.
Not a doubt but that in many second-rate hotels
and lodging housbs the servants work long and
hard. In private houses they won’t and don’t. I
know personally a cheerful intelligent woman of
thirty, a capital all-round worker who is “general ”
in a boarding house where there are eight boarders.
From six in the morning to nine or ten at night she
runs at her work. She daren’t walk, she says, or she
would “get behind.” Her employer is almost
as keen a worker. The general was offered a
situation at the same wage—l2s.—in a household
of two ladies, and she replied, “I’d die of
quietness. Here it’s always exciting, opening the
door to the boarders, and the different gents
coming and going and chaffing me a bit. I always
did like a bit of life. And, of course, there’s a tip
now and then.” The girl’s knee joints are in
such a state from incessant galloping that she
must presently break down and go to a hospital.
Where there are children in the household and
but one maid kept, the latter has no doubt to
work fairly hard, but so has the mistress. And
the latter has not the option of quitting her
billet. The Australian housewife is a great toiler.
She has need to be if she would keep a grip of her
home. But eight o’clock breakfast and seven
o’clock dinner or supper imply 14 hours, through
which the work of the day is done smoothly and
consistently, or crushed through with ominous
creaking of household wheels, according to cir-
cumstances. Mr. E. W. O’Sdllivan wants legis-
lation on behalf of the domestic, but even Miss
Rose Scott and other forefront sympathisers with
the working woman acknowledge that, as yet, the
law cannot intervene. The very quality of the
service in the average house precludes the
exact time schedule that obtains in office,
shop, or factory. Even if the eight-hours
system could be applied to the girls’ case,
where would the maids who went off duty at two
in the afternoon sleep and have their evening
meals ? The girls who worked the second eight-
hours shift would most likely have to sleep on the
premises. Could the first lot afford to pay for
sleeping and eating outside ? For that matter,
could the average employer afford the second
relay of servants ?
Raise the social standard of domestic work and
the problem will solve itself. Inefficiency on the
part of the employee is mainly the cause of friction
between herandheremployer. Training schools and
diplomas will alter matters in favor of the domes-
tic worker as they have altered them for the nurse
and the teacher. The latter professions were once
more harshly treated than the domestic service
of to-day, and as little considered socially. Recall
the experiences of Charlotte Bronte and her sis-
ters as governesses. Meantime I’d like to suggest
to Rev. Mr. Taylor that he form a registry office,
get the names of all those evidently capable but
overloaded girls who write to him the pathetic
letters of protest, and—well, he can then stand
back, so as not to be trodden underfoot by the
rush of considerate employers, as the unbeliever
was at the gate of Samaria. I would be sorry to
see a flattened clergyman at the door of a registry
office, with frenzied married ladies standing on
his remains and calling for domestic help.
Mr. Andrew Seton Chisholm, of one of our lead-
ing grazier and sporting clans, died last week at
his place at Goulburn. He married the then
beauty of this State —Miss Phoebe Raymond. My
Old Identifier says that there has been no one so
good-looking as she in the interval between then
and now. One of the Andrew Chisholm daughters
married one of the Camden Park Onslows, and
another became Mrs. Alston who, with her husband,
is a well known teacher of singing. As a girl she
studied in Leipzic, but her fine voice was injured
by overtraining. Leipzic seems an unfortunate
place. Napoleon, I believe, injured his voice
there also, about 90 years ago, through trying to
holler to 170,000 men simultaneously.
Mr. “Jack” Want’s health has not improved
sufficiently to allow him to be rocked in the cradle
of the deep per the Mongolia. So the family has
cancelled its intended passage by that liner, and
will await the K.C.’s recovery.
The Lazarus honeymoon pair, who had £4OO
worth of jewellery stolen from their rooms at a
Sydney hotel, are offering a reward of £lOO for in-
formation, &c. But jewel robberies are so care-
fully planned and the burgling firm keeps its head
so clear of the lees of drink that the precious stones
are rarely recovered. The steal must make the
Frank Gardners—who are at Petty’s—keep a lynx
eye round them for “Raffles” and such like Na-
poleons of crime, or else they keep Madame’s
jewels in a vault. She has two of the pink dia-
monds of the world, and the wide diamond collar
that lights up a box at Her Majesty’s on first
nights—well, there’s nothing like it in this office.
Talking of bijou things: Foregoing pair of
visitors have with them a rarely beautiful bit of
old Florentine workmanship—a small gold box
about four inches long, two wide, and one-and-a-
half in depth, carved, and enamelled in blue. At
the touch of a spring the lid flies back, and a tiny
bird with ruby eyes and emerald wings is shot up,
singing like a lark. It warbles for some two
minutes, then drops from sight as suddenly as if a
wild cat were in sight. The mechanism of its
being and doing is quite concealed, and for fear of
destroying the bird that sings the golden song
Mr. Gardner has never attempted serious scrutiny.
The curio was given him by the late Pope
Leo XIII., and because he is so often asked about
it by collectors in divers parts of the world (it is an
historic thing in bijouterie) he does the dis-
interested and carries it with him.
An undergraduate girl friend of mine (I don’t
think she’ll “get through” because she’s put a
mirror in the inside of the crown of her hat and
gazes hard into the hat during lectures) tells me
that she’s afraid there’s going to be a case of prof.-
napping at the University some moonless night.
One of the professors is unmarried ; he’s young,
and he’s got rather the aspect of a romantic Italian
tenor. Looks as if he might any moment fling a
corner of his coat over his shoulder and swagger off
the rostrum singing La Donne 6 Mobile, fie pro-
fesses one of the driest sciences going—the great
African Desert is a bog by comparison —and report
says he never in his life toyed with Maryphyllis
in the shade. But the girls who are cramming go
by looks. Meantime they ache to send him letters
and violets, and books for finger impresions, and
ties, and brilliantine for his moustache. Possibly
he gets, and stealthily throws such gifts into the
Uni. duck-pond, and the ducks gobble up the
white-hot letters and die in great quackony. I
hope some able-bodied Princess Ida will ’nap him
and stop his career as a heart-flutterer.
An Australian art student in England throws
ashes on the alleged glory of staying at great
houses. “Yes, I stayed five days down at the
Countess of Dot’s place, and in that time I saw
my hostess twice, at dinner. None of the crowd
in the house took any notice of me beyond a
‘How do?’ Not one cared for my singing or
pretty parlor tricks. They were all for outdoor
games and sport and bridge. On the morning of
the fifth day after breakfast I saw my luggage in
the hall, and a servant told me that my train went
at eleven. I felt for a minute as if I were being
turned out. But it is the custom here to keep
strictly to the time you are asked to stay. Your
room is wanted for a fresh guest.”
Miss Murray, who draws those ox-eyed, elfin-
faced young parties as illustrating the wearing of
the latest millinery and gownery in the “ Woman’s
Page ” of the S.M. llep.ald, is a Tasmanian
lassie, and a great-granddaughter of Admiral
Tarleton.
A correspondent tells me that the dolls’ stall at
Adelaide Government House Fete had on view a
doll’s bed —cost £26 ss. The sheets and counter-
pane were embroidered, and the pillow-shams
trimmed with real lace and insertion, all exqui-
sitely made ; but it was an absurd price, f rail
that. Yet it was sold. Fancy the poor kiddy
doomed to play with a 25-guinea bed ! Safe to
assert that the child won’t feel half the exhilara-
tion that the Ragged School youngsters experi-
enced when Mayoress Allen Taylor entertained
them at Sydney Town Hall. The costly toy is
part of the preparation for decadence. One
of the band of bored infants who dug up valuable
gifts in Miss Eadith Walker’s grounds, at her
recent children’s party, complained to her mother,
on returning from a schoolroom-dinner at a little
friend’s house, that “she was starved. The en-
trees were not at all rechcrehd.”
The side-saddle in M.L. is doomed —so a corre-
spondent tells me. Every second she-centaur
there now rides cavalier fashion, and one of the
items on the sports programme of a big agricul-
tural show was a riding competition for ladies
mounted Amazon-wise. But for once (or oftener)
the riders are not following a fashion set in London
or Paris, but in M.L. itself by the islands’ own
wahines. Mrs. Brownwoman habitually rides on
a man’s saddle, with the stirrup-iron between her
toes and a spur on her bare INo. 10, and she can
light her pipe in good bushman fashion while at
full gallop.
Mrs. Willie Mitchell, once the most admired
and talked-of woman in Sydney, is to be married
early in December at Lucknow, India, to a Colonel
Goodwin. They will live at Lucknow.
The Cup is filling up. Mrs. Philip Morton and
one or more of her sisters, the well-turned-out
Hay girls, and Mrs. Colin Stephen and Miss Mar-
jorie Knox are now among those who “ intend to
go.”
There was a great flap of local Somebody wings
last week when two engagements were announced
—Miss Linda Teece, a daughter of the A.M.P
potentate, to Mr. “ Bert ” Littlejohn ; and Miss
Maisie Chisholm, youngest daughter of the late
Andrew Seton Chisholm, to a Monaro squatter
named Baird. Latter pair met on the Vancouver
boat.
“Lo, this only have I found, that God hath
made man upright, but they have sought out
many inventions.” A fashionable Sydney dress-
maker tells me of the new walk to be assumed
when you adopt your next winter fashions. The
Princess robe and the long redingote that are
coming must be worn with a poise and gait quite
different from those that went with the short
“trotter” skirt. Chief points of difference
are the bend forward from the waist,
and the angle at which your chin must
be carried. And—you must mark this speci-
fication with mental basting threads —in order to
cultivate the correct chin and body carriage you
must sleep on your face. Yes, you must sleep
face downwards with a small pillow stuffed under
your chin so that you shan’t suffocate. Two
redingotes, it may be remarked—one of tartan
cloth, the other of mauve and black silk —will
help to give the Flemington lawn its usual crazy-
quilt aspect.
Always a-going to and fro on the face of the
earth (like Satan before he stopped to boil
Job), Mrs. Burdekin and her linnet-like unmar-
ried daughter are writing out new recipes for
mat de mer and getting their deck shoes ready for
an England-bound liner. To balance things
several absentees intend returning. Dr. Taylor-
Young came back last week. His wife and infants
come later.
Mrs. Kearney, a lady from an up-country
centre, and the writer of a drama, “Tantalus
Talascus,” accepted and copyrighted by Mr. J. C.
Williamson, is in town to renew her acquaintance
with Lady Jersey.
Douglas Gerrard’s bad arm seemed worse on
Monday night, and he was evidently playing in
great pain. Even from the circle the swelling of
the hand was visible. Next time the actor sees a
black spider, he’ll run from it, not at it. Con-
cerning “ Merely Mary Ann.’’ The pathetic
figure of the over-worked lodging-house slavey
made some of the audience think that London
“generals” want the Law’s protection pretty
badly. Nearly midnight, yet the landlady, as she
goes upstairs, shrieks a fresh order at her up-to-
date “Marchioness” from every step. “And,
Mary Hann, remember that to-morrow is the
Lord’s day, and you must be hup to call the three
pair back for hearly chapel.” And Mary Ann
takes her apronful of boots up to her own room, to
clean them before she sleeps.
Dr. Kate Hogg has succeeded Dr. Agnes
Bennett in overseership of the female wing at
Oallan Park.
Mrs. Billyard-Leake, in mauve chine silk, with
rare old black lace, was the most artistically con-
spicuous guest at the Townsend-Moseley wedding.
She was the Tasmanian heiress whose husband
had, by will of the dispenser of the wealth, to
take her maiden name. The Billyard beau whom
she chose was the admired man about town, and
arranger of Union Club frisks of some dozen years
ago. When the 8.-L.’s are not absentees they
mostly live at Young.
Copies of the “declaration” given Miss Flor-
ence Taylor by her master, Leschitezky, are seen
in the long, musical fingers of many Sydney-siders.
The letter says “ Miss Taylor is capable by her
great gifts of becoming a first-rate pianiste if she
can give to the art the considerable time and
careful study necessary.”
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 30, 1905.
My Dear Yarrie,—
After all the booming, Henley-on-tlie-Drain was
little better this year than last. A few more
shrubs, an extra yard or two of art-muslin, more
frill on the part of the fancy kennel occupants,
made the only differences. Arrangements were
so bad that the Northcote dame got stuck midway
in the entrance, where two short posts left only
sufficient room for a mosquito to hop through.
The cheerful Ex., being built on a more consider-
able scale, and being, moreover, rigged with
capacious marine chiffons, was stranded, and the
Aides fore and aft gazed helplessly on. I hey
couldn’t hew the posts down with a, scarf-pin
or a gold pencil, and they daren’t put a
helpful shoulder to the situation. Finally
the dame extricated herself and smiled again.
The Gr. Gr. boot-box was festooned with arsenic-
green muslin. The only one to beat it was that
labelled “Melbourne Club.” A canopy and a
French cafe air of revelry prevailed over the Bull-
and-Bush brethren lodged thereon. The Mac-
kinnon-Fairbairn amalgams had a notice to the
effect that guests must not crowd the upper shelf
of the kennel. In the afternoon that kept lots of
the timid away. In the night, when the heavens
wept over Henley-on-the-Drain, all the notices
on earth wouldn’t have kept the bankers
off. They poured on till the boot-box rocked.
The billed races got on somehow. The swarms on
the reserved banks were too much taken up with
the hospitalities on the hulks and the peacocking
struts of the social folk to care about anything so
wildly exciting as a race. The fancy boat-loads
thickening the traffic on the Drain helped the
scene to look crowded and lively. But nothing
could make the Drain appear salubrious. Dead
dog and vegetable debris took the stream where
the boats left room, and the aroma could only be
got through with careful steering.
Miss Ethel Guthrie, who has been having a
good time in the Cold Country, reappeared at the
Drain festival. She wore black tailorings, with a
dash of chaste white in striking relief.
Yes-No Reid is becoming a nuisance to the
tram-conductors. I had heard many stories, but
regarded them as the usual funny fiction about a
fat party till I saw things for myself. The round
man wagged a fat finger at the tram. It stopped.
In rolled Georgie heavily and painfully. He took
some seconds to balance his bulk and some minutes
to recover respiration. Then a great oily
peace brooded over his countenance. His eyes
began to flutter like the tired wings of
a dove. Finally he settled to sleep. The
coin-collector yelled “Fare.” The fat figure
made no sign. “Fare” was bellowed. A
slight tremor passed over the huge accumu-
lation. Georgie opened his eves and smiled
guilelessly. “Fare,” he echoed in a shrill treble,
and sought in fifteen pockets. “ I have a
ticket somewhere,” he chirped. The man in
buttons jerked away with brutal impatience to
collect other fares. When he came back, Georgie
was holding out a church coin. That delivered,
he started to search again for the lost ticket.
After five minutes of slow fat exploring, he dug
out the missing ticket, and, calling the exas-
perated conductor along, displayed it with the
contented remark, “I knew I had it somewhere.”
Then he sank it again into a deep abyss of his
garments, and went once more to sleep.
Miss Florence Young arrives in this hub on
Derby morning, which means that the Comic
Opera charmer is to air her Yank-cut frillies
at the Cup Carnival. A younger sister of
Florence is coining to the front of the
chorus. She now figures in the silk team, where
her straight buxom form and rather scornful under-
lip strike the stall-brayers as something quite
unlike the giddier elements of that frolicsome
team. It’s evident that the management shows
no favors to the next of kin of its stars. This young
novice has had to lurk in the back ranks for a
whole year. It’s only recently she has been moved
forward.
Miss Connie Armytage, the fiancee of ex-
Private Secretary Fitzpatrick, is off to Colombo
this week. With her goes Miss Brodribb, and
the pair intend to be on the salaaming tracks of
the Rajahs and such as don the turban of high
festivity for the Royal visit. In India they will
spend some weeks as the guests of Victorians
domiciled there.
Toorak has seized on the fact that a very rusty
old Federalist, long averse to the marriage yoke,
is looking round these days for a companionable
Eve. There is a flutter in the catteries in conse-
quence. A title and a fat income go with the old
boy.
Miss Alison Fairbairn, just, I reckon, to show
she isn’t prejudiced, and has the same sort of
blood as ordinary humanity, took the common or
garden epidemic of measles to herself, and at
present is not without spot. The attack was
timed badly, just as dress-fittings were due and
the Henley revel demanded her energetic aid.
Now she will not be declared free from spot till
the eve of the Derby. Several other bright girls
are down with the same complaint, and have had
their radiance dimmed thereby.
That very righteous surgeon, M. 17. O’Sullivan,
whose name is identified with the patriotic prin-
ciple of encouraging the birth-rate all he knows,
is back from his jaunt to the Cold Country with
an expanded chest and the latest Piccadilly tailor-
ings. On Wednesday the Women’s Hospital staff
turned out in a body to drink a welcome-back
brew at the Vienna. The O’Sullivan's melli-
fluous voice rolled out in Hibernian thanks for
the tea and muffin compliment.
Mrs. Bowes Kelly’s bidding to a swagger aspara-
gus spree at the Northcotes’ last week happened at
an inopportune period. She was otherwise engaged
contributing to the Victorian birth rate. Mr.
Bowes Kelly went on his own, with a personal
apology for the Missus in his pocket.

The arrival of a daughter to the house of Rep.
H. W. Lee led to much brass and blow on the
part of Parliament. One would have thought the
race was just saved from extinction by this con-
tribution to the world of a little Lee. A muster
of the members in the Opposition room was held
to compliment the proud dad, and a presentation
of comforters, mugs, spoons, rattles, and an
address of rejoicing further flattered the parent.
Even the Deakin eloquence was let loose on the
subject.
The arrival of an infant to the George Verdons,
who are at present lodged in England, occurred
after many years of matrimony minus a cradle on
the premises. George Yerdon is a brother of the
Melb. Commissioner of Titles—the dandiest old
bachelor abroad in these parts.
Face-healing is the latest fad. The cream and
flower of society are sweeping down upon Miss
Rubenstein’s new beauty parlors in Collins street.
The Viennese of Valaze fame is just back from
Europe, weighted with valuable secrets of youth
and beauty, and has a charming suite of rooms,
where massage is administered according to the
most learned principles, and Melbourne rushes to
get its Cup face polished up.
The doctors have been doing remarkably good
biz. in the theatrical world. Two or three times
Miss Jeffries threatened to end her season abruptly
and retire to a hospital ward. The alarm kept the
co. in a state of hot unrest. In the comic opera
ranks Miss Margaret Thomas struggled against a
collapse. Twice she was out of the “Cingalee,”
and twice Miss Preston tripped from the rear to
shoulder the star part. Then Miss Preston fell
ill. At latest she was in'hospital. Likewise Miss
Thomas. Now Miss Clara Clifton slowly recovers
from an ’itis that effected the throat. Miss Rose
Musgrove makes tremendous jokes about her fever
crop and her transformation. She actually tells
the story of how she lost both hat and wig, the
other day, in Bourke-street.
Colonel Selwyn Campbell, whose light goes out
with the Waxcellency Madden’s, and glimmers
when the Wax assumes its brief but shining vice-
regal period, is getting some attention on charity
committees. The military bachelor is at present
foremost with suggestions for the dames’ epileptic
hop.
The eye is being camera-ed for medallion pur-
poses. Miss Amy Willard, of the ‘‘Sweet Nell”
co., was the first to flaunt one of the kind. A
Dunedin photographer “took ” her orb during the
recent tour of Maoriland, and she relates how the
snapshotter took care to select the better eye of
the two. The difference ’twixt eyes is now being
thoughtfully looked into, and most Misses of any
perspicuity are marking for future photographic
purposes the more pleasing, or sparklesome, or
serious (as tastes go) of the pair, when there is a
pair of ’em, which is usually the case.
Just at this stage the she-bowlers of the State
are dispensing with corsets and equipping them-
selves for the green. Vainly the straight-fronted
ones essayed to bob and. bend, and secure a free
arm-swing in the accoutrement of the fashionable
hour. Accidents were too frequent, and now the
she who is more of a sport than a friller, dispenses
altogether with ironmongery about her person,
and girds herself in simply with a leather belt.
At every other green game, with the exception
perhaps of croquet, the sexes may go straight-
fronted in comparative comfort. But one must
bend to bowl.
A Melbourne dame who was boasting a while
back about the marvellous merits of hei Jap.
house-boy, now sports a pained silence and a
white servant. It has crept out that startling
discoveries led to the Jap s dismissal. A search
for sundry missing socks revealed the fact that
Whathoshima fancied them as dish-cloths. Fur-
ther investigation showed that when he removed
the baby’s bath-tub he invariably washed the
dishes in the same water. The Jap appeared to
have no idea that there was anything seriously
wrong in his way of doing things, and fear of
further revelations led the staggered housewife to
hustle him off without saying much. The family
is now strongly White Australian, although
it does not care to go too deeply into the reasons
for the change.
R.O. Archbishop Carr is anxious to promote
church dances. As a means to matrimony he
prefers the hop to the bazaar any day. It grieves
Archbishop Carr to find the mixed marriage so
much in vogue. Periodically his curates warn the
congregation against the Protestant partner and
he himself enlarges on the theme—but to little
use. The fact is the R.C. girl is vastly in excess
of the R.C. boy, and moreover the up-country R.C.
boy is strangely bacheloric nowadays. Mary
Anne, too, is disposed to look down on Mike, and
all the sermons in the world, full of Gracetho’they
be, will not deter a husband huntress from seeing
virtues and beauty in the Protestant Robert when
he begins to talk business.
That tired feeling ! One of the sex afflicted
with apathy and poverty advertises plaintively in
the papers for a home and a small pittance in
return for light services, spring-dusting being
specially designated. Spring-dusting implies a
languid flicking of a dilapidated boa-tail over the
what-nots of Suburbia, or the desultory flutter of
a rag about the nude legs of the cottage piano.
The tired gentlewoman who publishes a request
for a little sewing, and will stitch for pa and ma
and seven little Australians when she’s inclined,
doesn’t compare for tired feeling with the dis-
tressed lady who would like to coquette with the
furniture in return for her bite, sup and bed in an
irreproachable house.
There are some terrible examples of “art” in
the Vic. National Gallery, but the latest aggra-
vation, just walled in a particularly good light (all
good canvasses are hung in a poor one), has for
subject “ Canute listening to the Monks of Ely.”
It is presented by the executors of the will of the
late Mrs. McCulloch. Canute is a wooden image
and his crew ditto. It is large and dull and drab,
and the Gallery czars are anathematised for giving
it a good place.
In a city of alleged sanctity, a learned and
erratic old scientist is being pestered by a reproach-
ful spinster, who charges him with past attentions
which she interpreted as being of a matrimonial
character, and she girds up her lawsuit and
prepares to sue him for breach of promise.
Aide Hood quits the staff of S. A. Governer Le
Hunte presently. The attache of the apathetic
air was no great favorite in Tortoise Town. His
heavy-duty air and lisp disqualified him from any
giddy flutterings thereabouts.
A smart Kew girl makes a marriage journey to
India’s coral mudbank this month (November).
Mahomet’s journey to the mount is made
because business anchored this particular mount
to Burmese soil.
Geelong feels grieved that its wealthiest couple,
the Philip Russells, are lodged in Britain for a
term of three years. They have nailed themselves
to a Perthshire roost for that time, and are giving
any rolling stones from their old haunts a good
time there.
Head-Master Blanche, of the Church of Eng-
land Grammar School, is off for an educative
jaunt. He purposes accumulating much collegiate
knowledge and training hints to apply to Tommy
and Bob. He also should acquire a more grown-
up air and the wrinkles of authority, for he is the
youngest-looking of the teaching tribe in the
State.
Mrs. Dyce Murphy has moved with her small
but important squad into the Sylvester Brownes’
handsome house at Heyington, which is over by
the sunny side of Tooralt. The “Nice Murphys”
the family is dubbed by an admiring set.
Miss Addie Hine and Miss Irene Dillon were
the star whirlers at “The Players’” show the
other night. The former is a girl with the
most pliable and revolvable legs ever displayed
by so small an Australian native. T’other takes
the cake as the Call Boy given to a sprightly pas
seul every now and then.
The new arrival at Stonington, Miss Stuart
Wortley, has had to set all her wardrobe aside
and get into impromptu mourning for her late
uncle. The misfortune of bereavement at a time
that was to have rung with revelry is tempered by
visits to hospitals and such institutions. That
would be, to many young people, a greater inflic-
tion than the garb of woe, but perhaps to one of
the Talbot race it is a pleasure.
A Maryborough squatteress—Mrs. James
Thompson—and her stylish flock trip to the Cold
Country shortly. The jolly dame is the mother
of the tallest twins that have happened here for
many a day. Their debut this season was a
humorous event.
A croquet tournament drew a frilly pack to the
Windsor lawns last week. Miss Birdie Robb
(Birdie is an unfortunate name to grow up with ;
it’s all right for the chicken, but not for the post-
chicken stage) presided at a tea. Mrs. William
Parbury won a hand-mirror as a result of the com-
petition. The curate game is much in vogue
around here.
Miss Evelyn Scott is brandishing a brigU ;w
engagement circlet before the eyes of the 'stall
Johnnies. The lucky He is said to be au. ieal
artist showing at Rickards’.
The sharp competition in tea and bun shops has
driven the proprietress of the Winter Gardens
and Chicago Arbor in the heart of the Block, to
make a big splash. The rosery is now furnished
as an Arabian garden, with a gorgeous Japanese
department in the near neighborhood. The two
classes of art decoration are designed to catch the
crowd. Tea-sippers with a prejudice against
Monkey-land can squat under Arab arches, and
vice versd. Three hundred can call for their tea
and muffin in this place simultaneously, and 300
tea-pots are on brew at the same time. ’Tis a
colossal bunnery.
The debutante daughter of Dr. £. £. Smith
has scored musically once more. The last pass for
a diploma of Licentiate of the Royal Academy
gives her the right to append a few letters to her
name. Miss Louise Smith is herself not the kind
to tag them on, but perhaps she’ll lend them to
popper, who has a weakness for the alphabet.
YOURS Affectionately, TRYPHENA.
HOW TO BE BEAUTIFUL.
Yalaze preparations are still on top. None better are to
be found in the world. Mdlle. H. Rubinstein travelled far
and wide for a skin food to beat Valaze ; she didn’t find it.
All the champion centres of skin culture in Europe were
searched through and through. Result—Valaze prepara-
tions triumphed. Valaze preparations are on top of the
beauty producers of the world ! Valaze preparations are
the sole creators of cream end roses in the face of woman.
Nothing to , eclipse them or come near them on earth.
Better news still! Study in the most famous laboratories
of the world’s skin specialists has resulted in the establish-
ing of Massage Parlors at ‘274 Collins-street, where fair
woman finds a new skin, a fresh bloom, a new beauty of
form and face. No need to go disfigured, to be ugly, unat-
tractive, with skin marred or blemished ; the scientific skin
treatment at 274 Collins-street will rectify all that. Miss
Rubinstein and her two Viennese assistants conjointly,
with a replete electrical apparatus, effect miracles. Mole's,
warts, superfluous hairs, wrinkles, double chins, disfigure-
ments of all kinds are removed. The face blooms afresh.
Nothing is impossible. Scientific Massage treatment, a
steady use of Valaze, the herbal soap will effect wonders.
The faded, withered skin disappears. The greatest
specialists in Europe have trained, taught, instructed Miss
Rubinstein and her assistants. Certificates bearing the
most eminent signature in the world’s list of skin specialists
testify to the fitness of these ladies. All Australia should
ring with the news of their coming—it is an epoch. Valaze
was a boon—now, in addition, is the Scientific Massage
treatment, and a host of new Polish preparations for re-
fractory disfigurements. The jaded society woman, the
tired theatrical, the weary singer, all have a chance of
fresh beauty and bracing up. No one need despair—there
is hope for the plainest, the most world-fagged. The age
of miracles is not past*—be ugly, be discontented, be cross,

“ Be Wise in time and Beautiful for ever.”*
According to news from South Africa this
country’s old friend Crickolas Willis has been re-
manded back to Sydney on one charge, but there
are two more charges to be heard. This appears
to mean that he is to be sent back once, but the
Natal court hasn’t yet decided whether it will
send him back twice or three times. Which
vaguely suggests the case of the man who had
eight wounds. Three of them were mortal, but
the doctors reckoned they could cure the other
five.
During the debate on the Liquor Bill in
N.S.W. Assembly, hon. members suddenly dis-
covered that they had closed up the bar at Sydney
Cricket Ground. As all the members are ex
officio members of the ground, the rush to get the
bar opened again before anyone knew it was
closed was humorous ; and then a caitiff arose and
challenged the vote that re-opened the bar, on the
point that members were pecuniarily and other-
wise interested. The Speaker, like a good thirsty
sport, came to the rescue and over-ruled the chal-
lenge.
Says the man from Mildura, “ What do I
see in the Cup? Well, not much until I have
absorbed my regulation allowance of D.C.L.—you
know the brand ! Then I get a fair notion as to
which horses I should back, or leave severely alone.
No whisky in the world can reveal the winner’s
name for certain, but my experience tells me that
the wisest selections are made under the influence
of D. C. L. I usually spot the winner in three tries
— i.e., three nips.”*
