POLITICAL
POINTS
There were Six Hatters and Six Potters,
and now six Parisian councillors have refused to
shake the paw of Edward VII., and the six
Commonwealth States want to know what it all
means.
Lord Rosebery has the blastiferous cheek
to assert that if Australia sympathises generally
with the Tory party’s proposals for Protection,
therefore it must sympathise with that party’s
Chinese labor doings in South Africa.
It is thought likely that the Australian
excise on “ black-grown ” sugar will be raised
from £3 to £4 per ton, whilst the excise on white-
grown sugar will be left at its present £1 per ton
rate. That would give the white grower an
advantage of £3 per ton, and hasten the passing
away of the Kanaka.
A protest was made in the N.S.W. Assem-
bly, last week, at Mr. Crick taking part in the
debates since he is defendant in a serious
case. Certainly no ordinary man would care
to face his fellow-members until he had cleared
himself of an accusation of such seriousness, but
Crick is no ordinary man.
“ J.F.” : It would help things along if the
Australian Parliament took all N.S.W. outside
the 100 miles limit from Sydney as Federal terri-
tory, and collected the revenue thereof straight-
away, and thereby gave J. H. Carruthers some-
thing more important than the driving of a peg to
go to law about. I want to see a really good fight.
“Woodki” : I never had a very exalted
opinion of our State Legislature, but when it
comes to this sort of thing, in N.S.W. Hansard,
I think it’s time to call a meeting of myself and
pass several resolutions “ viewing with alarm ”
and calling on the country to “promote such
broad-minded legislation as will tend to the best
interests of the community ” :
Mr. Arthur Griffith : .
“A.S.”: The defence and immigration
Sroblems can be settled in one act. Get Dr.
laclaurin to tell the invader, when he comes,
that “the land has been sold and paid for, and
any talk of invasion or annexation is conversation
of a most dishonest character.” Then the invader
will go away home, and the need for more people
will cease.
“ Priam’s ” theory :
I am sorry that the Booth settlement will not come to
Australia ; it promised interest in the future. A band of
emigrants, picked for their Hallelujah devotion ; a set of
weak, emotional minds huddled together on a block of
land in the inland loneliness ; hard, coarse conditions of
life and penury made continual by the exactions of the
cause ; and the hysterical religious atmosphere that
strengthens and is strengthened by sex cravings. Sooner
or later that settlement would have yielded results of sur-
prising interest to students of the human mind.
Vic. Political Labor Council is raising the
question whether the Village Settlements Act of
1893 has not produced better results than the
Bent Government cares to admit. In short, was
it advisable to throw the Village Settlements Act
aside to make room for Bent’s Closer Settlement
Act, whereby big landholders are trading off their
more or less barren territory upon an obliging
Government ?
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers has offered to
submit his books for examination to the Lands
Commission. The offer was accepted, the Com-
missioner remarking that this “had been done
already with one Minister. ” With what Minister ?
It would be more satisfactory to the public if
evidence were tendered in open court of the agency
transactions of Ministers. It is presumed that
they are perfectly correct; if so, there is no reason
for concealment.
A Maoriland elector rose lately, gave a
long disquisition on the dangers attending Rugby
football, and then asked the candidate if he was
in favor of giving each of the M.L. Rugby team
now in England a grant of £2OO, seeing that they
might come back maimed for life. The candidate
declined flatly to encourage with a subsidy of
public money the pursuit of a bit of leather
covered with mud. “ Well, I won’t vote for you,”
said the disgusted elector.
Yes-No Reid cut a sorry figure, as usual,
in the House of Representatives, when discussing
the military Estimates. He vigorously denounced
the policy of the Government, which he described
as inadequate and ludicrously incomplete. After
half-an-hour’s tirade, someone mildly mentioned
that the Government, through lack of time to re-
organise things, had been forced to accept the
Reid Cabinet’s Estimates, and that the fat man was
braying at his own handiwork.
“ Two Seas ” on railway freights :
As showing the “pull” that the hairy producer of
wheat has in railway freights over the ordinary producer
or citizen who is not engaged in that soil-stirring occupa-
tion, a case of books weighing 4cwt. cost £1 9s. 7d.
railage from Warialda to Sydney, 462 miles. For the
same distance the Railway Commissioners will haul their
pet’s wheat at 14s. per ton—less than one-tenth the rate.
The bullock puncher doesn’t fare so well, as a ton of
“stinkin’ ’ides” (wet) over the same distance costs £2
17s. 10d., probably on account of the grievous weight of
the smell, although the inoffensive sheepskin pays the
same rate. A ton of greasy wool costs £3 7s. 3d., scoured
wool £3 13s. 6d., and, as a ton of the former doesn’t weigh
more than 20ewt. of the latter, the inference is that the
extra charge is made to discourage country scourers in
favor of their brethren around Sydney. Taken by and
large, N.S.W. railway rates afford some humorous reading.
But the wheat-grower smiles most.
Spruce Myth, haranguing Waverley (Syd-
ney) Women’s Liberal League the other night:—
As a contrast, he referred to the United States, where
an invitation was extended to all who could bring energy,
skill, money, or inventive genius, with no distinction as to
creed, color or nationality. Unfortunately, the politics of
Australia precluded any such consummation.
Now, is it possible to believe that Spruce is merely
mistaken when he tells such fairy tales —especially
as he always seems to work them off on “ fashion-
able ” women, who can’t be expected to know any
better ?
‘ ‘ Snell ” writes :
While the Victorian Police Commission is sitting
wouldn’t it be worth while inquiring how it comes about
that Melbourne policemen are aide to become fairly large
property holders on the poor pay that is their legitimate
reward? The idea of Policeman Hex owning his little
suburban terraces was erstwhile a joke, but I submit that
it is no joke. It is a stubborn fact that many constables,
starting with nothing, are able, in the course of a few
years when their wages have mounted to, say, £3 a week,
to become landlords, while ordinary workers receiving the
same pay are only able to just about scrape along.
Couldn’t the Commission try and discover how it’s done,
if only to provide an object lesson in economy for the
benefit of the community at large ?
The Local Government Bill evoked a
pathetic remonstrance in N.S.W. Legislative
Council on the ground that it would mean
“double taxation”—taxation, that is, by the
shire and by the State. MacLaurin, Kater and
Pilcher thought this was “glaring.” Still the
Sydney or Newcastle resident has paid that same
double taxation for many years without any
especial glare. And the people of England, Scot-
land and Ireland pay it as a matter of course.
“Loyal” persons like MacLaurin, Kater and
Pilcher are always telling us to imitate England
till they find it may cost them and their class a
few coppers, and then they would see England
boiled at the stake first.
Q. Premier Morgan has given a humorous
and effective twist to Brisbane Art Gallery row.
The squabble at first was between Brisbane Coun-
cil and the Government, the former refusing to
give up the pictures until the latter satisfied a
claim for rent. Morgan and Co. were too busy
saving the country to waste time on sore-headed
aldermen, however, so they neatly passed the
matter on by ordering the trustees to get posses-
sion of the collection at once or be gazetted out of
office. The situation is harrowing ; the aldermen
and trustees rushed into conference on the matter,
but the only joint resolution e ine to was that
“the Morgan Government was the most con-
temptible that ever disgraced Q ;ensl and.” The
aldermen will now either have o back down or
sacrifice their friends, the trustees. It’s a business-
like Cabinet, is Morgan’s.
ONE OF OUR ASSETS.—THE HOLE THE
GOLD CAME FROM.
“ Hufiin Guffin ” from Victoria :
There may now be added to the usual brigade of
hangers-on and followers-up of agricultural shows, race
meetings, &c., the Deform League platitudinist and his
kind. The three-card trick man, the cheap-jack, the
Punch and Judy proprietor and the side showman of all
kinds is now hustled in his railway seat and crowded in his
efforts to earn a dishonest copper by the representatives of
the Farmers’, Produeers’and Property-Owners’ Association,
the Reform League lecturers, the National Ass. officials,
etc., etc. The men with the monkey and the lottery are
said to be considerably upset by having to associate with
the new crowd when travelling, and are seriously pro-
posing a strike.
The N.S.W. Assembly has modified its
Liquor Bill, by extending the maximum time
before a pub. can be extinguished from three years
to eight years or more. There was, apparently, a
majority for the original proposal, but it gave way
before the stonewalling of a minority, and majority
rule went overboard. This sort of thing is simply
legislation by threat or bribery. To threaten a
majority that, unless it votes a certain way, it
will be broken down by long hours and exhaus-
tion, is just as unconstitutional as to threaten that
it will be broken down with an axe. And to bribe
it into surrender by the offer of some much-needed
rest is no less immoral than to bribe it with coin
or large notes. Legislators don’t appear to fully
realise this yet, but some day they will.
British War Minister Arnold Foster is
pessimistic as to army reform. Speaking at Croy-
don recently, he
confessed that he sometimes feared that nothing would
bring the nation to a sensible state of mind regarding tne
army but a tremendous defeat.
This should encourage Australians to follow the
advice of their Foreign-trade press and refuse to
establish an effective local system of defence.
Then when the British army is having its valuable
lesson, Australia, not being able to strike a blow
for itself, will get a valuable lesson also.
S.M. Herald, in its “On the Land”
column, concerning the Harvester squabble :
It was freely stated that the increased valuation would
fall heavily on the producers, but instead of that the
price to the producer was almost immediately reduced
some £l2 10s. Another somewhat significant fact is, that
while imported harvesters are down to £7O here, the
machines are being sold in the Argentine, where there is
no local manufacturer, at over £lOO.
The Herald argued for many years that Protec-
tive duties inevitably raise prices, and that the
local manufacturers were avaricious persons who
must be suppressed in the interests of the pro-
ducers. But it sometimes threatens to be a better
and wiser Herald nowadays.
Sworn in evidence before the Tariff Com-
mission by W. W. Forwood (of Forwood, Down
and Co.): —
Nearly the whole of the agitation and percolation
cyanide plants now in use in gold-mining in Australia
were of Australian manufacture. The whole of the furnaces
imported from abroad for the purpose of roasting ores on
the Westralian mines had been, or were, in process of
being discarded, and those of Australian design and manu-
facture were being substituted. The Australian-made
grinding-pans, and pans for grinding and amalgamating,
were certainly holding their own against the grinding
mills and grinding-pans of foreign make. Far more
machinery and appliances of foreign make and design
for treating ores in Westralia and at Broken Hill had
been discarded through failure to do their work than of
Australian make and design.
The Improved Small Holdings Bill, now
before the Victorian Assembly, is a scheme-for
“settling people on the land.” Under it each
settler is to receive £2OO worth of land, and have
£2OO lent him to go on with, and it is expected
that, with a total expenditure of £225,000, some
560 people will be settled on the soil in three years.
What a hideous joke—the settlement at great ex-
pense of less than 200 people per annum, when
some 14,000 people are leaving the State annually
for want of land, and taking several times £225,000
with them, and when then are 14 fertile but
almost empty counties, c paole of holding some
three million of people, who would settle them-
selves if the right kind of a land-tax and land law
broke up the big estates !
Lord Jersey, when he first
touched N.S.W. soil, made himself
“solid” with Australians by the de-
fence of the country against its
“ mosquito critics.” Returning to the
Commonwealth on a visit, he shows
that he has preserved a capacity for
fair judgment. Interviewed by the
newspapers, Lord Jersey gave as his
views: Australia ought to have a
High Commissioner in London, fresh
from Australian life; the “Stinking
Fish ” party is mainly responsible
for the adverse criticisms of Australia
in England; a flood of Booth immi-
grants would not be desirable for
Australia; it is the persistence of
borr owing and not any fear of “ Labor
legislation ” which depresses Austra-
lian securities in London.
Owing to the great exodus of
population from Tasmania the Evans
Ministry is “earnestly considering
the question of closer settlement.”
But the old sea-captain’s Government
can’t do anything unless it gets back
some of the land from its big landlords
to settle the people on, and the land-
lords won’t sell voluntarily at prices
that people can atford to pay, and the
ancient marine person regards any
compulsion as an outrage on property.
So Evans is a dismasted character, and
prostrate in the lee scuppers, so to
speak, and has sprung a leak, to say
nothing of having hove the main deck
overboard, and having much salt water
in the forecastle.
The average human life in
Britain is seven years longer than it
was at the time when Freetrader John
Bright uttered his “ rounded periods ”
in protest agafhst factory legislation,
and defended the brutal system of putting child-
ren to hard unhealthy labor.
S.M. Herald prints this for the informa-
tion of the Bruce Smith people who rave about
our restrictions on the Japanese :
In connection with the present negotiations between
the Federal and Japanese Governments it should be
borne in mind that no Australian, or foreigner of any
kind for that matter, is allowed to own land in Japan, no
Australian or Australian company can own or work mines,
or become a member of the Stock Exchange, or own
shares in railways, semi-Government banks, or subsidised
shipping companies.
William M'Culloch, of Vic., having re-
turned from the Benighted Kingdom, proceeds to
set Australia in order (see Melb. Age, Oct. 24).
After eulogising the discredited Jack Taverner,
he instructs the Australian Government whom to
appoint High Commissioner, rebukes “a late
Federal Premier” and a State Premier “not a
thousand miles away from Melbourne” for talking
through their necks, condemns the Australian
Parliament for its Irish Home Rule resolutions,
and concludes : “ I stated twice on the public
platform that tne people of Australia were pre-
pared to give quite as much [fiscal preference] as
they expected to get.” Now that William has
come back, things will go nicely, thank you.
Where the treasure is there shall the heart
be also! “Home,” to the average Australian
politician, is naturally the locality from which all
blessings flow —titles, stars, ribbons, gold braid,
and white silk continuations. Consequently the
Government of Australia provides a careful
definition of the word in the contract with the
Orient Pacific Company for the conveyance of
Australia’s mails: —
The homeward voyage (that is, to the United King-
dom).
The outward voyage (that is, from the United King-
dom).
Australia may borrow John Bull’s money, and
hire John Bull’s ships, and even import John
Bull’s submerged tenth ; it may be in a bad way,
and may be feeling mean—but surely it might be
allowed just for once to think of itself as “Home”
in its own mail contract.
Australia’s affable, placid Prime Minister
the other evening made this admission to Crouch,
M.P.: —
Britain has failed to keep her part of the Naval Agree-
ment bargain of ’O3, in that she has provided only one in
place of the two second-class cruisers stipulated for, while
Australia has promptly paid its tribute and kept its word.
And then the affable Prime Minister proceeded to
say that, of course, the Government didn’t think
it possible for Britain to commit a breach of the
agreement—Britain had just overlooked things,
apparently, or hadn’t remembered, or was busy
thinking of something else, or would fulfil the
agreement by-and-bye when it had time. The
cold hard fact that it had committed a breach of
the agreement Deakin was far too affable to admit.
But Deakin can’t go on being affable to the hare
and the hounds indefinitely, and some day the
hare will have to ask him to make up his mind
which side he is going to run with.
Prime Minister Deakin half threatens to
do something with the tariff this session. What’s
the matter with—
(l) Passing a Standing Order providing for the closure
of debate in order to suppress stonewalling.
(2) Introducing a Tariff Bill of one clause: “ All cus-
toms duties are hereby doubled.”
Then the Government might introduce, at the
beginning of next session, a new and elaborate
Tariff Bill, providing for duties on many things
now on the free list. A complete Tariff Bill
would, of course, be stonewalled, argued and
debated down to the last comma by the crowd
which is hired to destroy Australian industry for
the benefit of the importers, and despite all
standing orders it might, coming at the fag-end of
the session, be talked to death. But with a
closure rule, a Bill doubling existing duties in one
short yelp could be put through in two days.
After all the pitiful maundering of B.
Smith and the other people who have been
deluging this country with questions about hats,
and wailing over the shameful treatment meted
out by Minister Lyne to the importers of
the 1000 Panama that were said not to be
Panamas at all, the Customs Department proved
its case up to the hilt. The hats were first entered
at £54 13s. Id. the lot; then the importer’s invoice
valued them at £5O 3s. Id. ; yet, though a ring of
the chief importers refrained from tendering, an
offer of £lOB 3s. 4d., plus the duty on that valua-
tion, was made and accepted. So the importer
gets 10 per cent, more than he said the hats were
worth, which is mighty good business for a cash
transaction ; while the Commonwealth has about
£5O more to pass on to the States than if the
£5O 3s. Is. invoice hadn’t met the Lyne in its
path.
From the press gallery :
The House of Representatives, with much frothy de-
clamation, gave £25,000 of the taxpayers’ money to
help erect a memorial to the lately dead British Queen.
King O’Malley pointed out that the lamented lady had in
her lifetime drawn some £38,000,000 from the British
exchequer, but his remarks were received with a pained
frown. M’Donakl suggested spending the money on a
hospital, but was lectured for his presumption by Wilks,
who stated, perspiring with loyalty : “I willingly vote
the money, leaving it to those in control to put it to the
best use.” The total cost of the scheme, designed to
glorify the approaches to Buckingham Palace, will
be £1,000,000. “Think how much of it will be
spent in wages!” Langdon Bonython piped. Joe
Cook remarked: “It is a unique occasion. There
has been only one Queen reigning for 60 years over the
Empire.” Joe Cook further alleged that the late Queen
was associated with the abolition of slavery—for which
an Act was passed four years before she began to reign—
and that she had repeatedly saved the Empire from being
“ plunged into fratricidal war.” Then Willis and G. B.
Edwards and Wilks and Cook used such expressions as
“great and good,” “wise and glorious,” and all the
traditional adjectives, and Storrer reached the lowest
depth by appealing to Parliament: “ Vote the money, or
we will have a much worse name in the Old Land than we
have now.” Parliament then voted it.
Some recently published correspondence
re the Federal capital reveals certain curious
doings on the part of the soured and puerile
Carruthers. Here are some of the points:—
(1) A N.S.W. official appointed for the purpose recom-
mended the Monaro district as the best place for a
capital.
(2) The N.S.W. Government reserved from further
alienation the State lands in certain sites, including
Bombala and Dalgety (both on the Monaro), pending a
selection.
(3) The Australian Parliament selected the Dalgety site
in the district recommended by the N.S.W. official who
was specially appointed to report on the matter.
(4) Carruthers denied that the Dalgety site had been
reserved, and was bowled out.
(5) He refused to surrender it (a) because it was too
valuable, and later (6) because it was a poor, miserable
spot, and quite unfit for a capital.
(6) He induced the N.S.W. Assembly to refuse to sur-
render the Dalgety site. . _
(7) Prime Minister Deakin communicated with Car-
ruthers, asking for the Dalgety site for a Federal capital.
(8) Four days later Carruthers wrote back that he was
going to cancel the reservation of Dalgety as a possible
site for the capital—this after his previous denial that
there had ever been any such reservation.
How is it possible to do business on any rational
Erinciples with such a soured and snarling small
oy as Joey Carruthers?

or something. It beats me to understand why an

and pants ” to wreck the next plate-glass windo
with exceptional thoroughness.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Unless the Derby race on Saturday results in a
surprise from Jas. Wilson’s or Pay-ten’s stable, there is
very little probab.lity of the winner springing to a short
price for the Melbourne Cup. In this scribe’s opinion
Lady Wallace will win the Derby from Charles Stuart and
Scotland, and if the blue ribbon goes to the filly, who
has no engagement in the Cup, the three-year-olds will
look more than ever like a neglectable quantity for the
big handicap on Tuesday. Or if Lady Wallace goes down
before Charles Stuart or Scotland, or both of them, her
defeat will not alter the fact that the rather leggy
Scotland is of doubtful stamina for the Cup. The 3-vear-
old most likely to come into Cup notice through its
Derby running is Circuit, a strongly-bred colt in
Wilson’s stable, but it will have to finish ahead
of Lady Wallace in a fast run race before it will
be reckoned dangerous for the Cup. All things
considered it is not advisable to think seriously about the
Derby horses for next Tuesday’s race. Last year Blinker
made a good bid for the Cup after running “ nowhere ”
in the Derby-, but there are no good grounds for suspect-
ing that another Blinker will be sprung upon the public.
The Hotham Handicap has sometimes foretold
a Cup winner. If Blue Spec, Aurate, and Scot' Free
are hacked for Hotham Handicap, the winner of that
always interesting event should be worth considering for
the Cup: Perhaps Scot Free will be good enough to take
the minor trick on Saturday.
And as to the Melbourne Gup. Take the follow-
ing as the pick of the lot without pausing to consider
whether the rest of the field have any chances among
them : Maniopoto, Tartan, Warroo, Gladsome, Emir,
Marvel Loch, Scot Free, Toran, Blue Spec, Overdale,
Circuit, Roller, Aurate, and Scotland. Gladsome,
although much fancied by Sol Green a while
ago, is open to the objection that she has never yet
proved herself a great mare over a longer distance than 10
furlongs. The handicapper treats her as being 11 lbs. below
Emir at two miles, and although this seems a liberal allow-
ance, it is probably-not too much. Emir occasionally shows
stamina, and he is expected to excel himself next Tuesday
when he will have tog-ive 121 b. to Maniopoto and 81b. to
Tartan, not to mention lighter-weighted horses. He is
not worth backing to do it—at the price. He may beat
Gladsome and Marvel Loch (another doubtful stayer) in
any case, but even should he run the kindest race of his
life, this splendid galloper would probably get beaten by
the weight. And the odds against Emir running gamely
are fully 8 to 1, whereas some of the others are always in
a good mood.
Tartan and Warroo, in the same stable, repre-
sent the most trustworthy “ form ” of all the Cup
favorites. Tartan ran second in the Sydney Metropolitan
last year, and he won the Sydney Cup six months later,
proving- himself a stayer. Warroo also ran second for a
Metrop. before filling that honorable position behind
Marvel Loch at Caulfield the other Saturday. Of the two
stable companions Warroo looks the more likely to shine
in wet weather, when the going is heavy, but Tartan has
higher pretensions, on appearance and achievements, to
win a Cup in ordinary Cup time. The market on the
morning of the race should indicate which of the pair
carries the stable’s fondest hopes. The writer prefers
Tartan on the ground that, in winning a great two-mile
race, he actually did what Warroo has not yet tried to do.
Nevertheless, it is hard to believe Tartan capable
of giving weight away to Maniopoto, the horse that
brilliantly won the Metrop. at a time when his com-
rade gees from Maoriland were doing as they pleased
with the Australian opposition. Maniopoto’s “form” in
this country has certainly been eccentric, but if an
animal’s “uncertainty” is to be urged against his Cup
chance, how about Emir ? How about a dozen others, not
one of whom has ever put up a performance fit to be men-
tioned in the same paragraph as Maniopoto’s recent vic-
tory at Randwick ?
* ■ • •
Overdale is pretty certain to be running on
When many of them are slowing down. Scot Free, who
unluckily fell in the Caulfield Cup, has a chance over the
longer course, likewise Roller and Aurate, and any Derby
winner other than Lady Wallace. Indeed, the bold show
made by Aurate at the business end of the Moonee Valley
Cup last Saturday won considerable respect for the son of
Auraria. Torah seemed to tire in the Caulfield Cup,
nevertheless the natty little colt may prove a sticker next
Tuesday, and no devout student of the Racing Chronicle
can ignore him. Taking seven against the field, this scribe
names Maniopoto, Tartan, Emir, Warroo, Scot Free, Torah,
and Aurate, placing
Maniopoto 1
Aurate 2
Scot Free 3
Tartan 4
* * * *
Now is the time of portents. Last week a man
a-few doors from this office brought home a peacock, and
the noise that bird makes is deafening and horrible.
Several deputations waited on the owner, and offered
him drinks to suppress the nuisance. He was obdurate.
Then the staff noticed that every time the bird shrieked
it said “ War—roo ! ” A syndicate was formed to work
on the revelation of the oracle, and the bird has been put
in the cellar lest he should spoil the market by giving too
many people the tip. # ,
There seems to be a popular delusion that the
second horse in the Caulfield Cup has often succeeded in
winning the Melbourne Cup. As a matter of fact,
Acrasia last year was the rare exception to the rule as
regards Caulfield seconds. Within 21 years one winner of
the Caulfield has been the runner-up in the Flemington
race, and one has scored a first and third. Three horses
have been second in both Cups. Two of the Caulfield
thirds have won the Melbourne, and Acrasia, as aforesaid,
scored a second at Caulfield, and a win in the longer race.
Out of the 63 gees placed in the Caulfield Cup during
those 21 years only nine have been placed for the Mel-
bourne Cup. This year promises to add at least one more
to the list— but figures are more trustworthy than mere
fancies, anyhow.
* » * *
The attack on the V.R.0., caused by its refusal
of Wren’s horse nominations, has cleared the air of sundry
complaints that were laid against the racing club. From
time to time the V.R.C. was howled at on behalf of the
crowd that swarms to the Flemington Flat free of charge.
“Why didn’t the club consider the wants of the poor
Flatites ? ” was asked by people who wouldn’t have the
gall to ask why the Melbourne Cricket Club doesn’t open
its gates to the non-paying public when a test match is
being played. Both clubs hold their land under Govern-
ment grant, and both of them have three sorts of reserves
—for members, for grand-standers, and for the minimum-
priced public. The V.R.C. has now formally explained
to the Government that it differs from all the other big
racing clubs in not being privileged to charge for ad-
mission to the Flat, which at Caulfield is a two-shilling
reserve. Also, it does provide some accommodation for
the folks who get there gratis. The Flat has been levelled
and improved every year. A water T supply has been laid
on, and “ retiring ” places are available, and a tote would
be available if legalised. A free stand-building
is out of the question, for it would block the
view of the races for those who pay to see the
show ; moreover, it would kill the business by making
the Flat more attractive than the reserves, where the
view of the racing is more distant. Then there was an-
other complaint that the club had “ taken away’’from
the public the rising ground on the far side of the Salt-
water River, formerly the resort of picnickers on Cup Day.
Why should the autocratic V.R.C. seize the land that had
been so long utilised by joyous family parties for
Cup-seeing purposes? The committee explained firstly
that this background to the show had been disfigured by
a crop of advertisement boards proclaiming the virtues of
various beers and boots and things. Secondly, the
ground was a menace to the.interests' of the show, seeing
that the owner who let the advertisement spaces was at
liberty to sell it to a speculator, who might build a cheap
stand thereon and charge the public a shilling or two per
head for an entertainment which he didn’t provide. So
the club-bought that land, and having bought it, didn’t
choose to leave it open to deadheads. It is not expected
to be any more of a philanthropist than Williamson or
MusgrOve.
° • * * •
Membership of some of the Melbourne shops—
vaguely described as “certain betting clubs”—appears
to be charged with exciting possibilities just now. Mem-
bers suspected of being traitors in the camp are visited by
a.vigilance Gonunittee at their private residences, and are
either searched, there and then, for incriminating evi-
dence of their dangerousness, or are lured to the shop by
false pretences, locked in a room, and threatened with
various sorts of violence by the agents for the ruling
powers. One of these intimidation “ clubs” has obtained
a list of -names of candidates for the malodorous police
force, and any would-be myrmidons of the Law who
belong to the “ club ” are promptly black-listed.
The Cornstalk season will comprise three big
meetings at Sydney Cricket Ground under the « anage-
ment of the Summer Nights Syndicate. The tiust was due
last evening when this paper was weltering through the
machines, and there are still a couple of evenings to go
The second meeting- will take place in January, and its
biggest prize will be the Summer Wheel Race, ihe thud
will be the supreme effort, and be held in March, when
the great Svdney Thousand, the richest cycle handicap
in the world, will no doubt produce the usual crop of
scandals.
* * * *
To very many people the most interesting
feature of the Cycling Carnival that opened last night at
Sydney Cricket Ground will be the five-miles race with
which each evening'’s sport is to close. Maoriland s crack
road racers, the Arnst Brothers (J. and K.), will compete
in them, and it’s worth proing- a long way to see big* Jack
hurl himself through the atmosphere, while brother Dick
is not calculated to bore anybody either.
♦ * * 9
Sydney Telegraph recently remarked that
with the advent of improved motors in Australia we take
considerable interest in big- performances on similar
machines “at home.” Then it went on to describe what
happened at a race in Paris. The Telegraph is *at
home ” in any country but Australia.
* a *
It is about time cyclist Floyd MacFarland gave
up winning all the big handicap races with his mouth.
This paper is willing to admit right here that Floyd Mac-
Farland can outskite any three Australians, and some of
them are no duffers at the game either. If the elongated
American could only ride as well as he can brag', what a
sack of boodle he would take back to the States!
* ■* * *
Now understood that Don Walker, finding that
those parts of him which hold him off the ground are
“ better than ever,” will reconsider his decision to retire
from the cycling track. In his time, the Victorian has
scooped over £3OOO in prizes, and, having won 11 out of
14 scratch events lately, he begins to thinn the talk of
retirement w-as somewhat premature. But then there
was never any doubt about his legs—it’s his tail that’s
weak. Once the rear man has passed him, Don’s
tail has a tendency to fall right down and get tangled
among the spokes of his back wheel. If he can only
stiffen his caudal appendage, he will probably win a lot
more money before he is played out.
* * * *
There’s trouble over that swimming exhibition
in the Seine at Paris. The French people had arranged
an exhibition, hot a race, of the finest' swimmers in the
world, and with the exception of Kieran they all seem to
have been there, including our own Annette Kellermann,
of whom the gallant Gauls made a tremendous fuss. They
entered the water at different times, and though speed
records were noted, it was not a competition, and the
amateurs reckoned that they would be quite safe. Now
they find that they reckoned without the English National
Swimming Association, and that body has opened the ball
by declaring Billington, the English crack, a professional.
Bill, has lodged an appeal.
* * * *
The Ford-Towns sculling match at Raymond
Terrace on November 11th is attracting some attention.
Both men are training hard, and although young Towns
has so far impressed the spectators most, Ford and his
party are in no way dispirited. Towns dropped his boat
on the stones last week, and brother George having sent
up his own shell for training purposes, has set to work
vigorously to build a new boat in which Arthur will
endeavor to uphold the family bay leaves.
The closing day of the third round of Sydney
First Grade cricket was not remarkable for the batting.
With the exception of Mackay’s 147 not out for Burwood,
and L. Pye’s 122 for Central Cumberland, the scores were
small all round. Mackay’s 147 was a savage attack on
the bowling, and he lashed out at everything that came
within reach, lifting two right out of the ground. Pye,
too, swung the timber in great style, and eventually
met his fate through over-eagerness. Stepping out to
meet a leg-break he missed, and was promptly stumped.
* * * V
Joe Darling has been “denying” again—in
fact, Joe seems to do little else these times but go round
and deny things about the Australian XI. His latest
repudiation is the amount of the “make”; Joe is
emphatic that the rumored profit of £9OO per man is quite
incorrect. It would clear up a great litter of mystery,
and save Joe Darling a lot of wear and tear of jaw, if he
would publish a balance-sheet, or at least state exactly
what each man did make.
* * + »
Batch No. 1 of the last Australian XI. home-
returning stragglers reached Sydney last week, and
returned Captain Darling’s whitewashing with praise-
worthy promptness. Apparently, the bucket of white-
wash and the large brush form part of each kit-bag in
lieu of missing ashes. Denials of rumored dissensions are
also liberally strewn round. F'eminine accompaniments
have sown seeds of discord in more than one previous
cricketing invasion of England, and ’tis said that the
latest venture was not the luckiest in that regard. Some
day the articles of agreement will contain a clause that
members must travel in a state of grass bachelorhood.
* * * *
Said that cricket has seen the last of Syd.
Gregory. A few more of the last syndicate could be
spared with somewhat lees regret.
* * * *
Tyldesley and Hayes did not get any of the
consideration that seems to have been shown to
Armstrong- in the matter of making a season’s aggregate
of 2000 runs. In the last match of the year Rhodes got
them both out when they wanted 6 and 3 respectively of
the 20 centuries.
» » * *
The result of the Police Charities Carnival,
held at Melbourne Exhibition Building in April last, sug-
gests that the present Force should be converted into a
travelling circus, and a new Force should be created for
law’n’order purposes. The business management of
Melbourne police entertainments is beyond reproach.
This Charities Carnival yielded £lBOO, against total
expenses £384 (including only £9 for refreshments). The
hospitals got £l4OO out of the fund. At about the same
time some of the horsey toffs of Toorak held a gymkhana
at the Exhibition Building, and although they had the
advantage of the Easter Monday holiday and considerable
sectarian support, their profit is understood to have come
out at £6O. The public are not likely to bite at any more
gymkhana.
* * * *
The Australasian Amateur Athletic Champion-
ships meeting will be held in. Sydney on the 11th and 13th
of this month. Victoria has already selected half-a-dozen
of its representatives, and will choose the rest within the
week. Those already decided on are G. Blake End W. F.
Steele for the one and three miles; G. A. Wheatley for
the quarter, half and one mile. N. F. Burrows will also
compete in the half and one mile.- H. Duigan will look
after the high and long jumps and J. Pattinson the pole
jump and 120 yd. hurdles.
* * * *
Nigel Barker, the Sydney University flier who
beat Duffey, was born a few thousand years too late.
Had he lived in the days of the Olympiads he would have
been a bigger man than Edward VII., and been entitled
to walk round in the hot days dressed only in his bay-
wreath and sandals.
The late Golden Gate bruising hall is now a
running' track, and will be opened by a meeting of pro-
fessionals on the 6th of this month, when a big entry is
anticipated. A running track right in the heart of the
city should fill a long-felt want. Queensland seems to be
the happy home just now of the professional athlete. At
Mount Morgan, a meeting has been arranged for New
Year’s Day, when the “ Mt. Morgan Hundred ” will be the
chief attraction. At Bundaberg, prizes ranging from £4O
to the modest fiver will be scattered around. Charters
Towers recently had a “ Hundred,” and Townsville last
week had a £5O handicap and a youths’ handicap worth
£2o ’ . » *
The Sydney Yacht Squadron, which attempted
to open the season a fortnight previously, managed to
accomplish the feat on Saturday, in spite of a wind that
was downright horrible. The event of the day was an
All Yachts Handicap for prizes presented by the club
commodore, Mr. James Fairfax. Eight started. Culwulla
and Fairlie went off with a beautiful bound. Sunbeam,
Fleetwing and Oithona made a bad beginning. Off
Bradley’s Head a sudden squall struck the fleet, and the
struggle with the spinnakers jumbled things up beauti-
fullv, till Magic, with a top-sail shining like a fat cheque
on a sea of promissory notes, dashed out of the ruck and
led round the Head. Behind her came Culwulla,
Scotia, Sunbeam, Oithona and the tail. When the spin-
nakers re-appeared Oithona began to run past them, boat
after boat, till only Magic rocked away seven seconds in
front. Then Oithona got in front, and Culwulla ran past
Ma<’ ic. The end of the first round found Oithona first,
with Culwulla, Magic and Scotia in that order. Sunbeam
dropped out—it was no day for Sunbeams. On the second
run to Bradley’s Head the Magic came with a wonderful
burst of speed, passing Culwulla, but was unable to
overtake Oithona, which romped away from the whole
fleet, and finished nearly ten minutes ahead of the second
boat, Scotia. Culwulla and Magic dead-heated for third
P rize - ,
The Sydney sailing clubs want the Government
to resume the foreshores of Parsley Bay, Vaucluse, which
for years has been a favorite resort for sailing-men—on
Sundays particularly. The yachting clubs are holding
aloof, on the ground that the sailing club-members used
not to behave with decorum when they had the run of
the place. Though sometimes a rough “ talent ” would
spoil the pleasure of the majority, it is better that they
should be suppressed by the water police than the
decently-behaved should lose their playground. The
sailing crowd races on Saturdays and holidays, and con-
gregates at Parsley and other spots on the Sabbath, so
the parson crowd‘is against their petition. This will
probably settle its fate with Premier Carruthers.
That exceedingly fair and gentlemanly fighter
Peter Felix has arranged a bashing match with William
Squires. The matter at issue is the destination of a purse
worth £175, and the altercation will (D.V.) take place at
Sydney National Sporting Club on December 12th. This
writer, who usually avoids prophecy, ventures to predict
a very pretty scrap. # »
Yankee boxer Al. Neill, who broke his fist
fhditing with Willis (not the land agent—fighting our
redoubtable Nick would have broken the Yankee’s heart
and pocket too, in all probability) has gone to
California. He says he will come back again, with his
wife next time, to settle down.
* * * *
N.S.W. ex-Premier Twaddel received his knock-
out, as far as boxing was concerned, by the police report
on the Turner-Whitelaw upheaval. As soon as it became
evident that Turner had weakened so as to be unable to
properly defend himself, the referee gave the fight to
Whitelaw just as the police officer was about to stop
hostilities. Both the bruisers were medically examined
before and after the disturbance, and the badly-walloped
individual was declared to be unmarked, and as free
from injury as before the fight. His boiler gave out—
that was all.
* « * *
But if Australian fight promoters are-wise, they
will arrange for a set of Australian boxing rules in which
the kidney punch will be eliminated. Every other week
recently there have been exhibitions of kidney punching,
and the other day one man deliberately laid
himself out to bash an opponent, who is said to
suffer from kidney disease, round and over the
affected organs. Of course, the retort is that a man who
has kidney disease should not enter the ring ; but this
writer holds that the kidney region is one that should be
barred under the rules, just as the groin is. That it was
not barred was probably an oversight on the part of the
sturdy old Marquis, who never had back or any other
ache in his life. It is the only spot in which a chance
blow might inflict permanent injury that the thoughtful
old sport did overlook ; and the omission should be
remedied. If fight promoters don’t eliminate it, some
day the other side will start an agitation on the subject
which may kill the game. # t
Maoriland bruiser Griffin has been nicknamed
Maniopoto, probably on account of the lightning speed
with which he gets off his chair to the attack when the
gong sounds. In his return fight with Bert Clarke the
Maorilander kept himself fairly calm in the first three
rounds, and behaved with the polished indifference of a
Stoic. Then he got tired of pretending not to care, and
hurled himself through the atmosphere, whirling his fists
the meanwhile. The unfortunate Clarke, unable to get
out of the way, was struck about 17 times in as
many seconds, knocked down .several times, and beaten
all round the ring. Every time he 'rose to his feet a
whirlwind of meat fell on him, and by the sixth round he
was “ satisfied,” and so was his second, who handed in
the pale linen of defeat. What this writer wants .to see
is Griffin up against another whirler like himself. Then
a great wind will arise, and the dust-cloud will ascend to
heaven and obliterate the referee.
* * * *
The proverbially brief dog’s breakfast was a
lengthy function of eight courses interspersed with
music, compared to the return scrap between Martin and
Walcott, the latter being a genelman of color. The hos-
tilities took place at Sydney Gaiety Club on Saturday
night, and the first round terminated with Martin on the
floor, as the result of a right cross. The gong saved him.
When the signal for battle clanged once more Martin
rushed in like a wild elephant, but Walcott rushed also.
The trouble was that Walcott arrived at Martin before
Martin arrived at Walcott, consequently the former got
something hard and black in the middle of his counten-
ance, and was then banged hard and often round the
provision department. Martin reded against the ropes,
and Walcott came at him with whirling fists, but the Law
interfered, and the referee very promptly awarded Wal-
cott the money, for Martin was already beaten.’
**. * *
Melbourne’s weekly scrap at Queen’s Hall, on
the 23rd Oct., was between that busy feather-duster,
Paddy M'Mahon, out of the West, and Frank Silver, a
local product. Silver is probably one of the neatest ana
most gifted boxers in the Commonwealth, but, if people
who manage fights as public entertainments have tne
every-day sense of a mule, it will be a long time before he
gets "another opportunity to air his graces. Having boxea
brilliantly for a round and three-quarters, he carefully
took Mr. M’Mahon by the head and tossed him down.
The referee warned him, but he sought the earliest oppor-
tunity of repeating the act. He threw M’Mahon tour
times, and after so many bids for an adverse decision, ne
naturally got one. This is not the first fight in which
Silver has got an adverse decision in some such fasm ,
so his future displays will probably be in the absence oi
large and discriminating audiences.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a drunk going home in a cab to-night, and I know
that that drunk is me,
As the hansom rolls ’neath the stars so white, with its
fare in an ecstasy !
Each pub. sprints by like a beery ghost, for the feet of
the mare are fast;
Ho ! I’ve got my knife in the Hebrew host, and I’ve
potted the “ books ” at last!
For I staked my shirt on Marvel Loch, at a sweet old ten-
to-one—
May the angels fodder that noble crock when her last good
race is run !
May her trainer sit in the heavenly- stalls, with a special
hymn to sing—
May her owner stroll through the jasper halls, and be
hailed there as a king.
Long years have I poured my precious cash in the gaping
bags of Mo ;
Hooray for the jock in the light chrome sash who hath
laid my Ikey low !
Hooray for the chestnut six-year-old that has gravelled
the angry Yid,
Who hath freighted me with his gladsome gold to the
tune of a hundred quid !
I’ll pay my bills—there are twenty-nine—and I’ll buy the
wife new clothes,
In the d.c. row she shall far outshine the daughters of
Isaac Nose.
She’s a dam fine girl with a dam fine neck, and I’ll buy her
a bangle too ;
She shall have her share of the lovely cheque that I’ve
torn from the moaning Jew !
Ho ! I’ll buy that pup that I like so much, and I’ll pay my
long club score,
I’ll free my ticker from Uncle’s clutch (I would fain shed
Uncle’s gore).
I’ll dine the boys in glorious style, and we’ll drink at a
hundred bars ;
And I’ll treat myself—well, I should smile !—to a box of
the best cigars.
We’ll paint the city a bright, bright red (and business can
rip the while),
And we’ll hie us forth and awake the dead (and the dead
shall awake in style).
We’ll go to the play at night with girls who are not our
propertee,
And if the result should be a fight that fight will be great
to see.
There’s a drunk going home in — hie —cab to-night, and I
know that — hie—drunk is me,
As the hansom rolls ’neath the stars so white, with, its fare
in a hie-’stasy !
Each pub sprints by like a — hie—old ghost, tor the feet of
the mare are fast;
Ho, I’ve got my knife in the—hie —Brew host, and I've
potted the — hie—at last.
Ho! I bet my — hie—upon Marvel Loch, at a noble ten-to-
one —
May the angels fodder—hie —noble crock when her last
good race is icon !
May her trainer sit in — hie—heavenly stalls, with a
special hymn to sing-
May her owner stroll through — hie—jasper halls and be
hailed there as a king.
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.

A cigarette fiend changed the subject
somewhat at Golden Gully, Bendigo, a few Sun-
days ago, by gyrating before the early church-
goers, clothed in some loud shrieks and an open
razor. By the time the police were on his track,
he had tumbled down a 12ft. shaft, and was dis-
covered at the bottom digging up the mud and
devouring it. One of the constables beguiled the
lunatic to the surface by offering to fight him for
a fairly large sum. He arrived with great alacrity,
'and put up a willing ten seconds against over-
whelming odds. After that, the lunacy ward re-
ceived him, and there he remains up to the pre-
sent, talking mostly about cigarettes.
N.S.W. Full Court has again tried to
decide what a place is. A Sydney bookie was
charged with betting in a lane off King-street, and
when the case came on a policeman gazed hard at
a plan of the locality, and swore that a certain
dab marked A represented a spot in the lane on
which the bookie did habitually and continually
plant himself and trade. Whereupon the concen-
trated legal mind decided that the said bookie
was sufficiently long in one spot to make the
locality he occupied a “place,” and remitted the
case back to the magistrate who had ruled that he
wasn’t in a “place,” but"who did not say how he
managed it. Unless the High Court upsets this
decision, a bookmaker will not be safe in con-
ducting a betting business in a public lane unless
he prances up and down all the time, but if he
keeps on the move he will be all right.
The squeamish folk now squealing against
any “compulsory” system of military training
probably don’t know that by Federal law the
Governor-General has power in time of war to
call out every able-bodied male in the Common-
wealth. All that is proposed now is that
they shall be trained for that call.
“ Heroism of John Smith ” was one of the
headlines in the account of the grievous Maitland
(N.S.NV.) colliery disaster of last Sunday. He
was the man who carried away the dynamite in an
off-hand fashion under his arm while the mine was
exploding like a 4th of July oration. When a
man named John Smith is a hero (and quite a
number of John Smiths are) he is generally a
perfectly unobtrusive person who didn’t realise
that he had done anything remarkable till he saw
it in the papers. Compared with the hero of a
Blandolt melodrama he puts on no chest measure-
ment worth mentioning.
The manager of Mort’s Dock (Sydney) voiced
another grievance against the lamentable Aus-
tralian tariff last week, when the new Manly boat
was launched. Mort’s Dock built the new vessel,
but owing to the backward state of the iron indus-
try here, several important parts of the machinery
had to be imported. On these there is a duty
which the dock had to pay, whereas if the ship
had steamed into the harbor as a going concern
there would have been no duty whatever. The
inference is that we want a big iron industry of
our own, and a duty of at least 20 per cent, on im-
ported ships.

The Monkey’s objection to the Queens-
land Government including him under the general
term “ natives of Asia,” recalls to mind the famous
definition of a Chinaman with which Walter
James once delighted W. A. In 1894 he moved to
amend the Goldfields Act as follows :
No Chinese (in whieh term is included any person who
is apparently a native of any part of Asia, Africa, Japan,
or Java, or any one of the islands of the Indian or Pacific
Oceans, or of the Malayan Archipelago, or who is appar-
ently a child of any such person) shall hold any miner’s
richt.
lamf was in those days an enthusiastic young
man, with high ideals, and no paltry scruples
about ethnography.
“Melb.”: I have just seen a handsome
wooden shield, made out of the last native tree in
Melbourne city. This was the red gum which
stood on the Elizabeth-street front of St. Francis’s
Church. A much finer red gum stood on the
corner of Flinders and Swanston streets, where
the Cathedral is. A gouty antique hippopotamus
of a bent red gum is worshiDped by Anglican
identities, on the lawn of Bishopscourt. It goes
up a bit from the ground, then has a wonderfully
long horizontal stretch. Observe how the native
trees are beloved and cherished on Bendigo,
whereas Ballarat is all for the English greenery—
vide Sturt-street.

£50,000 SAVED!
...ACTION o. AIR...
PERFORMANCE v. PROMISES
On October 4th, 1905, a deputation of local manufacturers of
harvesters waited upon the Minister of Customs, and intimated that
if a prohibitive tariff were declared upon harvesters they would
reduce the prices then ruling by £5 the first year, and another £5
the second year. They were, however, so far as can be learned from
press reports, suspiciously silent about what the third, fourth, and
subsequent years were to bring forth re harvester prices, and those
who follow that good old rule—“judge the future by the past ”
will mistrust that should the manufacturers succeed in their designs
to prohibit harvester importations, they will find some way to get
back the £5 and £lO cut promised for a limited time only. It is
well known that they, like all other sellers have in the past, always
got ALL THEY COULD, hence these indefinite promises look
strangely like “ HOT AIR.”
On October 7th, the harvester buyers of Australia got ACTION,
—they got performance instead of promises—they got something
substantial NOW instead of vague illusory promises to be realised
on in the dim future; for on that date we made an open
quotation on our harvesters as follows:
£7O cash at starting.
£73 March Ist, 1906.
£77 half March Ist, 1906 and half March Ist, 1907.
£BO quarter cash and quarter each March Ist, 1906, 1907, 1908.
£B5 third each March Ist. 1906, 1907, 1908.
This is a cut of £l2 10 0 per harvester, averaging cash and
terms prices together. There will be about 4,000 harvesters sold
in Australia this season, and hence our action obviously saves the
farmers approximately £50,000.
We shall settle with the hundreds of purchasers whose orders
we now hold at the reduced prices quoted above, believing that
those who entrusted us with early orders should be loyally protected,
although we must lose thousands of pounds in so doing.

keep asking- questions, there is no Cutting or Blood Poisoning ; absolutely no

if distant one thousand miles, on the following day.
We treat thousands of cases annually. We understand such cases better
than those who treat only a few hundred. Practice makes perfect in the
treatment of disease. Do not despair. Order your treatment at once ; let us
add your name to our long list of cures.
is the greatest blessing - What does a man gain if he
wins a MELBOURNE CUP SWEEP, and is not healthy or
curable of a disease . (t A man who is truly healthy is
rich beyond all dreams of riches, "ingersoll.
Thus wrote America’s great prose poet, the man whose beautiful writings and exquisite similes
are quoted as household words throughout the American and English worlds of literature. Ingersoll
is a man who will never truly die, for in the spirit of his writings he is ever present in the world of
men.
The philosophy of the above quoted lines appeals to all. Ingersoll only voiced a common fact
in superior sentences. Every man knows the value of health, even the truly healthy man. The
trouble is he does not truly appreciate it. True appreciation of health is only given by the man who
does not possess it, who is sickly and envious of his healthy companions, and whose life threatens to
end early.
It is this man who appreciates health, and when told he is incurable would give all he
possesses to be cured.
What a tragedy is life to the man who is told by his Specialist that no cure exists on earth for
him, and, like a man doomed to death, he must surely die ! And perhaps, by Early Indiscretions,
Disobedience of Nature’s Laws, Neglect, Privation, Exposure, Delay in seeking Skilled Medical Aid
before it was too late, he has aided the generation and progress of a deadly disease within his
system, and has unknowingly written and signed his own death warrant.

M. Ular’s recently-published “ Russia
From Within” shows that during the war
Russia’s old nobility kept up the reputation of old
nobility in general. Asked where were the boots
of his barefooted troops, a general replied, “In
the pocket of the Grand Duke Vladimir.” Into
the.same pocket went immense sums which should
have been expended on a factory for the supply
of drugs to the army. Trains despatched by
Vladimir, supposed to contain ammunition and
clothing, were found to be laden with stones and
straw. The same benevolent person assassinated
a station-master who shunted his train to allow
urgently-needed war supplies to proceed. His
son Boris arrived in Manchuria with eight lady
friends recruited from different countries. He
gambled, caroused* lost enormous sums of money,
and refused to go to the front when ordered
by his superior officer. When remonstrated
with by Kuropatkin he attempted to assas-
sinate him. Boris’s brother Cyril was the
direct cause of more than 60 men from the Petro-
pavlovsk losing their lives. A first-rate swimmer,
and quite uninjured, Cyril was making his way,
after the blowing-up of the ironclad, to the shore,
which he could have reached in another five
minutes. Round him were hundreds of injured
sailors drowning. The rescue boats dashed for-
ward. Cyril bellowed without intermission, “It
is I, the Grand Duke ! ” Numbers of disabled
men were clinging to the boat. “ Knock them on
the head ! I am the Grand Duke ! Beat them
off !” he shouted. Yet in the face of such dis-
graceful proceedings the poorly-clothed, halt-
starved troops, with hardly any provision for the
wounded, put up a good fight, and gave indications
of wonderful capabilities if not hampered by the
interference of a rotten nobility.
London Speaker of Sept. 9 pays a big
compliment to an Australian writer :
We turn to Maxim Gorky, as we turn to the sketches
of life by the Australian Henry Lawson, to escape from
the middle-class atmosphere of compromise and make-
believe which prevails now in English fiction.

small frontal bones of the

through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want

our great remedy.

. “A-D.8.” witnesses a piratical proceed-
ing- in Sydney :
She was a little Briton, she was, and had the arts of a
Circe. The way she piloted him from a busy thorough-
’T,!’«tarne be^ h aQ he T re a co . nse l " t . in £ was admirable.
T’s true be was only a sandy-han-ed, pigeon-chested slip
of a galoot, whose legs wouldn’t be over envied as loco-
jnotn e members even when their owner was sober—but he
had his humors. She had manoeuvred him into a side
street ere his beery brain struggled into a sickly half-con-
sciousness of his captor s design, and then her engineer-
ing genius arose to its zenith. Twice he let out with his
fist, ana pitched forward on her ample bosom, and was
snatched up and carried with legs kicking. She would
skirmish ahead at street corners for “ D’s.”, and return
mg with jubilant haste, buckle on to him a-min
staggering about on door-steps, coaxing with a leer’
scolding- him like a naughty brat, her arms and
legs baulking his threatening collapses and foggy
outlets of escape. Holding feebly aloft a broken
cigarette in one hand, he thrashed helplessly at
the air with the other whilst he was being
pushed, tug-ged, and hypnotised along by a slatternly
whirlwind of draggled voluptuousness. The alley- corner
had just been reached and the goal was in sight when a
’tec. loomed in the distance. The loon’s legs and arms
were now whipping like tentacles and his foxy face was
wild with perplexity. She took it all in at a glance.
Ducking her head she rammed it into his back and sling-
ing her arms about his waist rushed up the alley and shot
him into a kennel and the door slammed behind them.
Right under the eye of the enemy the prize had been
carried off. A score of faces popped into sight from as
many cribs and instantly disappeared as a weasel-eyed
’tec. stalked gloomily through the deserted lane.

What Scientific Men Say
Dr. riunroe, M.S.E.E., Author of “Electricity and
its Uses,” says:
“As a Curative Agent, Electricity is becoming more and more useful
and established on a sound scientific basis. Its possibilities in this
direction are infinite, not only in the cure of Nervous Disorders, but in
the reduction of tumors, the dissipation of pain, the enticement of sleep,
the destruction of bacteria, etc. None can tell what further service the
Electric Current will render the arts of medicine and surgery. The
SEARCHING quality of its power is greatly in its favor. At present,
the cure of physical disease is the noblest of all the manifold uses of
Electricity.”
Drs. Beard and Rockwell say:
“ The effects of Medical Electricity may develop slowly, but slowness
of development marks a radical distinction between TONICS and STIM-
ULANTS. The agreeable stimulating effects which immediately follow
an application of general Electrisation, just as they follow the use of
gymnastics, walking, active games, etc., soon pass off, or merge into
the permanent or tonic effects that come more less slowly and after con-
tinuous treatment. The effects of Medical Electricity often continue to
develop long after the treatment has ceased. Weeks and months after a
patient has taken a course of Medical Electricity he will continue to
improve in his general condition, even though very little progress may
have been made while the applications were being received.”

“Squidge”: Walked into a tobacconist’s shop
in Melbourne one afternoon, and was kept waiting- 20
minutes while a man from the country bought a pipe.
Now I know why pipes are blamed for cancer and other
diseases. The hayseed examined about four dozen pipes.
His method was to put the stem in his mouth and blow
down it. The shopman told me everybody tried pipes
that way, and many of them only stopped blowing when
their wind gave out, or when they had blown through
all the stock.

Business, Robbery, etc.
A little learning very nearly proved a dangerous
thing to Fatteh Barack, an Afghan litigant, who has
been troubling the Perth courts. Fatteh. has been
losing some money in his legal amusements lately,
and, so his enemies said, to put the balance of
his swag' beyond the reach of the Law’s talons, he
invested it in the Savings Bank under assumed names.
Being a man of culture, nothing less, it is alleged,
than the names of three great Persian poets would satisfy
his taste ; and the bank clerk received deposits for Omar
Khayyam, Hafiz and Firdusi. But the legal nose is keen
for plunder, and scented what it suspected was Fatteh’s
plant. Lawyer Le Mesurier argued that the gentlemen
aforesaid were dead and so could not deposit money, and
then sought to attach the poets’ deposits for his costs.
Fortunately for the honor of learning, however, Justice
Parker held that there was no evidence to show that
Omar, Hafiz, and Firdusi were not living ornaments of
Perth society. Thus, in a manner of speaking, poetry
was vindicated.

“L.S.”: Re “ F.C.’s” par on chemists’ prices (B.
5/10/’O5). “ Common salt ”is not pure sodium chloride.
What “F.C.” got was probably the chemically pure
substance (Melbourne wholesale price, Is. 6d. lb.). His
remarks on “spirits of salts” are rubbish—wholesale
price 2d. to 3d. lb. (weight) ; retail (Melbourne shops, at
least) (id. a pint. But pure hydrochloric (mur'atic) acid
retails at 2d. oz. (measure). “F.C.” probably obtained
the latter. Paris green: wholesale price Is. 3d. lb. ;
retail 3d. to 6d. per oz. The book-signing at which
“ F.C.” cavils merely happens to be a check on the
casual lunatic, suicide, or murderer. As to “suspicious
regularity” of prescription charges, “F.C.” is surely
aware that a fixed scale depending on the dose is
adopted. He does not pay for the intrinsic value of the
drugs, any more than he pays his medical man solely for
the hire of his instruments when an operation is per-
formed. A chemist puts in three or more years of study
and examination, and four years apprenticeship (usually
working 15 hours a day for no pay) in the best years of
his life, to acquire proficiency in dispensing. His know-
ledge is surely worth the few coppers profit. And the
tame chemist who took the 2s. 6d. and didn’t smash the
asafmtida bottle on “F.C.’s” head for his schoolboy
performance was probably a cutter and an outsider and a
pirate. The non-unionist is parasitic on all businesses.
* * * »
Announced that Japan, “having purchased
some of the best blood stock in Australia,” will now go in
for breeding. Horse-breeding used to be one of the great
industries of N.S.W., and it was “natural” enough to
please the most rabid Foreign-trader, but somehow —pro-
bably because greed led to the export of all the best
breeding stock and left mostly weeds behind—it has lan-
guished of late. At one time the “ Waler ” went all over
the world carrying everywhere on his sure, swift feet the
reputation of Australia ; but now the export of horses is
comparatively trifling.
* * « 9
The Rockefeller Oil Trust has practically bought
up the Republic of Nicaragua—primarily with the idea of
exploiting its mines, and no doubt with an eye to the
ultimate importance of the place when the Panama Canal
is cut.
• * * *
“ Sussex-street ” has always borne a bad name
amongst butter-producers. The odor has now reached
fowl and egg dealing.. A co-operative society has been
formed to protect the interests of the producer against
“ Sussex-street.” One man says that his account sales
always recorded dead fowls and broken eggs, till the
monotony of the damage made him tired. Then he
instructed his son, on the quiet, to intercept a case of his
fowls, 16 in two compartments. At the sale-room; the 16
fowls were all alive; hut when the account sales were ■
rendered, as usual, so many were reported dead. The
salesman had the choice given him of a summons or pay-
ment in full. He paid. Another producer set a trap in
the matter of eggs. He was also tired ; so taking advan-
tage of the fact that he was personally unknown to the
agent, he attended and bought his own consignment of
the culmination of the hen. There were no breakages. ;
When he attended on the agent a week later, and put
before him the bill he had paid as buyer, and the account
sales he had received as seller, there was trouble for the
agent.
* * * *
“Two Seas ’’: One bears a lot these times about
cutting up big estates, and putting men on the land free,
with tools and tucker to keep them going until the
returns roll. in. Heaps of men are willing to take a turn
at “ struggling on the land ” on those terms; but not a
voice is raised on behalf of the poor little struggling
grocer. I’m one of them, and I venture to suggest that
the Government should resume Anthony Hordern’s new
emporium, for a start, arjd cut it up into small shops,
filled with a year’s supplies, and provide each tenant with
a delivery van and a man with an apron to do the heavy
work, and some small change in the till. I feel reasonably
sure that we would struggle with the tea and sugar busi-
ness just as satisfactorily to the State as the men who are
provided with free land and tools struggle with the soil.
We would be in a position to pay rates and taxes, any-
how, whereas the man on the land, with all his struggling,
is too poor to pay anyone but the land agent.
* » * *
At a recent meeting of a M.L. Star Bowkett
Building Society it was suggested that scrutineers be
appointed to examine the marbles previous to a drawing,
owing to suspicion of “fake.” Members were bashful
about accepting the position, one portly gentleman refus-
ing nomination point blank. “He doesn’t want the job,”
growled a voice from the back ; “ he knows his marble is
coming up. to-night.” Five minutes afterwards that
member’s marble did come up for a £5OO drawing. The
meeting then resolved itself into a society to debate the
laws of chance.

“Xela” gets his hair off : —“On a recent visit
to Sydney I went into a hairdressing establishment for a
shave. The tonsorial artist (I allow no common barber
to shave me) who operated told me that his shop con-
tained not one Australian-made article. ‘From the hair
restorer to the clothes brush, all imported, sir.’ * Poor
Australia ! ’ I murmured. 1 Your pardon, sir ; not quite so
close. Certainly, sir. I’d advise you to join our club ;
eighteenpence a week, and every attention. Your face
requires a little of our famous New York Pimple Ex-
terminator, sir.’ This rather annoyed me, as I’m
proud of my face, chiefly on account of its not being an
imported article, so I inquired if he’d paid duty on his
manners, but didn’t wait to hear.”
* . *. * *
Slave-buyers in China take stringent precautions
against the danger of purchasing lepers. One test is to
take the live stock under consideration into a dark room
and set a blue, light burning. If the slave’s face has a
greenish hue> in. this light he or she is thought to be
untainted ; a reddish appearance is held to indicate
danger of leprosy. Whether there is anything in the
Chow’s theory or not, his belief in the necessity of thus
inspecting the human property shows, how . large a pro-
portion of the population is suspected of the disease.
Still the cheap labor advocates sneer at all efforts to
guard against incidental importation of this Mongolian
loathsomeness, and talk vaguely about the “ hempire ”
when the subject crops up.
“ The Prooshau ” : The Indian hawker, know-
ing the bush youth’s weakness for scent, always carries a
good Supply, and the first bar of his overture, when
“bundarl” and “barsket” are displayed, is usually,
“ Buy bottle scent, young fellah ? verry cheap, only one
shilling.” Jim reckons he wants “ Poppin Axe,” where-
upon Mucka Singh declares, “ Better the scent this kind,
better the brand ; young gurrl mor-re like, I know.” So
the sale is made ; and when Jim goes dpwn to the “ kick”
at Mint “ Crick,” and expects to get a‘“ knock-down ” to
the “ noo. teacher,” he scents himself to the very boots,
and goeth forth like a rose leaf on the' breeze to make his
conquest. I saw;him enter, the. country shop one even-'
ing, just prior to a dance, intending to buy a hanfiker-
chief and a bottle of scent, but, only having' fid. over the
price of the ball-ticket, he was dismayed to find nothing
cheaper in the scent line than that amount. “Cripes,”
he said, “I wanted,a nose-rag too, but that’ll’ave to
wait, I s’pose ; must ’ave the bottle er sniff, if it’s a quid.”

“Edyson” : Mr. Carruthers will be pleased to
hear of my friend the Dago. He keeps a small fruit stall
in the city, and I knew him long ago as an enthusiastic
Federationist who expressed His sentiments in impassioned
but barbaric English. The other night he had changed
his tune completely, and was cursing Federation from cars
to crupper. I asked an explanation. He spluttered in
his anguish : “ They tell-a me the Federash she make-a
the business mooch good, so I do-a the big talk for the
Federash. Now the Federash come, what it is I see? No,
saie-a for the peanut—no sale-a for the peanut at all! ”

COHEN AT THE
DOOR
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ONCE UPON a midnight mournful, while
I wrote a leader scornful,
And the savage, sullen satire did exude
at every pore,
Whilst a distant dog was yapping, suddenly there
came a slapping
As if some pale spook was flapping—flapping at
my sanctum-door.
“’Tis some creditor,” I muttered, “with a bill I
owed of yore,
Only that, and nothing more.”
Yea, distinctly I remember, it was sometime
last November
That the bailiff did dismember all my household’s
precious store.
’Twas a time of sodden sorrow ; vainly I had
sought to borrow,
But they bade me call to-morrow, though my heart
was sad and sore.
“ ’Tis the bailiff,” I repeated, “ who has called on
me before.”
Here I opened wide the door.
On the mat there stood a Figger like an elephant,
but bigger,
He was piebald—partly nigger, partly specked
with yellow o’er.
“This,” I gasped, “ is Retribution—that remorse-
less institution
Which in endless resolution stalks the Night’s
insolvent shore.
This is Cohen —whom we’re owin’ forty billion
quid or more
It was Cohen at the door !
It was Cohen, Isaac Cohen, and a garment
strangely flowin’,
Made in arabesques of I O U’s and old P.N.’s he
wore.
‘Twas Australia’s great arch-bailiff; “Sir,” I
gasped, “ it’s no avail if
You insist that we shall settle on the spot our
debts of yore.
Shall we e’er be independent, and owe foreign
Jews no more ? ”
And he snuffled “Deverbore !”
“Bailiff,” said I, “thing of evil, bailiff still, if
Jew or devil,
Sent by Fate in ghastly judgment to this non-
finaneial shore—
To this bankrupt land Australian pawed by
every envious al'en—
This, our Home by paupers haunted—tell me,
Cohen, I implore—
Is there— is there balm in Gilead—must we serve
thee evermore ? ”
Said the Hebrew, “ Ever bore.”
“’Tis a lie,” I said, upstarting, for mixed shame
and anger darting
Permeated all my being to its hot, indignant
core ;
“ Take this half-quid as a token that thy cursed
spell is broken—
As a sign that we will pay you and be bled by
Jews no more.
We shall ship your bloated carcase with the D.T.
and the Akgus—
We shall sool you off Australia with a loud
emphatic roar ”
But he answered “ Deverbore ! ”
And that Cohen, never shifting, whilst our land is
doomward drifting,
Stands in bloated contemplation at Australia’s
bankrupt door.
And his eyes have all the seeming of a demon who
is scheming,
And the haggard slush-lamp streaming shows his
Nose upon the floor !
Shall that Nose loom in our portal like a vulture’s
beak immortal—
Shall the Nation be tormented by that spectre
ever more ?
By the grim, eternal spectre of I. Cohen at the
door?
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.
Working the Game.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BALAAM pulled out his pipe with a
grunt of satisfaction.
“Tell yer what,” he said ; “ there’s yer
boots. They ain’t fust-class ; but we cud git a
ikey lot o’ drinks fur them boots.”
The Weed spat over his bluey with con-
temptuous skill.
“ Tell yer what,” he said. “ I ain’t goin’ ter to
do no sech bloomin’ thing. Go an’ swap yer own
bloomin’ boots.”
Balaam stuck out his feet. They explained the
situation mutely.
“Fat lot I’d git fur them,” he remarked.
“ Don’t be a chouse. Let’s hev a suck-in, an’ go
ter Kooringa. It on’y a mile on grass, an’ they’ll
be bound to dredge out suthin’ ter wear. Go on.
We ain’t had a booze since sun-up.”
The Weed looked with sudden interest past the
little wayside pub., and across the darkening
paddock.
“Well, I ain’t goin’ ter chanst it,” he said.
“ S’pose they didn’t guv me no boots. Where’d 1
be then?”
Balaam sucked at his pipe. The heat and thirst
of a long day lay heavy on them, and the smell of
beer came down the road from the pub. It put
genius into Balaam.
“Gimme yer boots while yer goes an’ arsks
Then yer won’t hev lost nothin’ if they says ‘ no ’ ”
“I’ll have lost me boots,” said The Weed
darkly. “What’d you be doin’ while I’m askin’’
I’d like ter know?”
Balaam looked him up and down as the original
Balaam may have looked on the ass.
“ You orter be aimin’yer livin’ in Parlymint,”
he Well, .. plant em somewheres where
1 ean t fin em, an arsk.”
“ I bin there before,” said The Weed. “ They
says as they never has anythin’ ter give away but
dog-bites.”
He lay on his back, with his head on his bluey,
and blew smoke in the eyes of the stars. But the
smell of the beer dragged Balaam to his feet.
Yer don’t know the meanin’ o’ friendship,” he
said. “ I’m goin’ to Kooringa on me own ; an’—
they’ll guv me suthin’ as I kin swap.”
“ They won’t. . . ’nless yer brings the dog back
clamped on ter yer,” said The Weed. But Balaam
shuffled into the gloaming, unheeding.
There were five girls on the Kooringa verandah,
and several men. From the lighted rooms behind
sheafs of radiance sifted through the darkness of the
garden. Suddenly from the shadows came the
barking of dogs and the yell of someone in fear.
Two men in evening dress jumped down the
verandah steps; hauled a crumpled thing from
under a laurel bush, then hid it with haste and
irreverent laughter. For the crumpled thing was
Balaam in a dirty flannel shirt and a kilt made of
a tattered coat.
Take them brutes off of me,” he howled.
“ They're smellin’ me legs .. . they be bitin’ me:”
“They won't if they know what’s good for
them, said the fair-headed man. “ What are
you doing in this rig-out, eh ?”
Balaam explained very lucidly that he had been
bathing in the river beyond, when a tramp came
by and stole the part of his attire which he could
least spare. “So I hanged round till it was dark,
an’ then I corned along, thinkin’ as surely I’d drop
on someb’dy as ’ud do suthin’ fur me.”
The fair-headed man looked at the other, and
his eyelid flickered on his cheek.
“Come along round to the little office at the
end of the verandah,” he said. “ Keep behind
him, Bert. All right, old chap. We’ll fix you
somehow.”
“You’re a gennleman,” said Balaam, in deep
gratitude. .
I know it. Don’t apologise. Here you are.
Keep an eye on him, Bert, in case anyone comes
in.”
The fair-headed one returned inside of five
minutes with a pair of grey check trousers over
his arm. Balaam looked on them. Then he felt
very sick. For they held the shape of his legs
yet, and that darn on the knee had been put in by
his own hands yesterday.
“’Fraid this is the best we can do,” said
the fair-headed man, regretfully. “ They’re not
very clean, I think, and they’re a bit shiny from
too much sitting down. But they’re better than
nothing, eh ? I shall be very pleased if you’ll
accept them.”
Balaam took them, and climbed into them, mur-
muring gratitude in broken words.
“Don’t mention it,” said the fair-headed man,
cheerfully. “Always glad to help a fellow in a
mess, you know. Mind the step. I’ll see you
down to the gate. O.i, don t mention it. No
trouble at all. Good night.”
Balaam did not speak, even when the clang of
the gate shut him out in the silence of the pad dock,
He crawled back to The Weed, and sat down
under the hedge on his swag.
“Plow long did it take ’em ter find it?” de-
manded The Weed, without removing his eyes
from the stars.
“ Wot yer gittin’ at ?” asked Balaam, in sudden
wrath. *
“Nothin’ pertickler,” said The Weed. “On’y
I tried the boot dodge last time I was by, an’ they
bunted ’em up an’ guv ’em back ter me. The
blessed dog ran my tracks back to where my boots
was planted. I thought as you might’a’bin tryin’
on suthin’o’that sort.”
“ 111 knock the ’ead off of you some day soon,
Weed,” said Balaam, thoughtfully.
