GORDON & GOTCH.
James Vanvervecken and His
Moral.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE name of James Vanvervecken fco-day
recalls rather a fact than a personality.
The memory of his colossal failure, the
number of thousands whom he robbed, are of
greater interest than the man himself. But among
the men of his day, even in the first shock of that
crash which heralded the bursting “boom,” the
greatest exclamation was “ What a man !”
A short thick-set man, slightly corpulent, of
erect carriage and rather skulking walk; full
lipped, with heavily-pouched eyes of staring
habit, a taste for sombre clothes, and a detestation
of jewellery, given to speaking quickly with occa-
sional moments of hesitation —not a stammer, but
a pause as if to pick up the exact words he wanted
—such was James Vanvervecken. In hard drink-
ing days he was the possessor of a “capacity”
unequalled from Cairns to Fremantle. No
church-goer; a card-player and racing-man, in a
mild way; knowing everybody, yet without any
easily-designated close friends. These were his
principal characteristics up to a certain date.
Then the man underwent a sudden and delibe-
rate-transformation. He ceased to drink. Van-
vervecken for some twenty years had never gone
to bed drunk, but he had never gone to bed quite
sober. And, by merely willing it, he was able to
become and remain a total abstainer for ten weary
years. Quite what forced his determination can
never be known. But the men who examined his
affairs afterwards noted, curiously, that, just at
that date, Vanvervecken passed from the ranks of
commonplace thieves into the elevated condition
of a financial Sir Henry Morgan.
He did things, then and afterwards, which
amounted so nearly to the miraculous that one
auditor is said to have entertained the mad idea
that Vanvervecken was not altogether human.
The details are not incidental to this story; enough
to suggest that he mortgaged several large proper-
ties two or three times over, and that he devoured
the entire body of some half-a-dozen trust estates.
For ten years he walked, lived and slept on some
five or six hundred “live” shells, the smallest of
which could have blown him into Darlinghurst.
And in that ten years not one single grey hair
showed in his head.
Then the boom came, adding to the tortures of
the certain hell which he must have seen ahead of
him, a peep of the open gates of heaven. He be-
gin to make money almost as quickly as he had
previousij stolen it. To square things up he re-
quired an immense sum, but men made and lost
immense sums in those days.
S > ad led to the very considerable business of
watching his few hundred live shells, he went out
into the battle of the boom, and led the fight. At
this period one can easily imagine what the temp-
tation to have one. night’s good liquor-up meant
to the man—one night free from the spectre of
Nemesis—one night where his fiercely active brain
would not be eternally conning over the possibili-
ties of those shells. “Nightly,” so his valet said
afterwards, “ I used to hear Mr. Vanvervecken
jump out of bed and pull a chair across the room
to his writing desk. My room was underneath
his, and I could hear plainly.” One imagines him
just falling to sleep, when the memory of A
flashes across his mind. This Ais a very small
shell, but it is little things which great men
forget; so Vanvervecken, jumping out of bed,
sits at his desk in his pyjamas to indite the neces-
sary letter that will prevent the very slight
inquiry which would cause A to burst and ignite
B and all the other letters of the alphabet.
He was an extraordinary correspondent. The
exigencies of his position obliged him to write
scores of letters which otherwise would have been
clerks’ work.
With drink, he had dismissed, also, from his
life another little vice. A very charming, dainty
piece of vice, too—prettily framed at Vanver-
vecken’s expense in the cosiest of little houses.
“Vice” shed some very angry tears, and used
some surprisingly strong language, till finding
that, though the man went, the cottage remained,
she took comfort and another comforter.
At his club one night Vanvervecken remarked
that “a business man who talks in his sleep
should never marry.”
Perhaps he was thinking that it was hard
enough to carry a secret through the day without
blurting it out. For, mind you, before he became
a wholesale buccaneer he had been a social man,
though without near and dear friends. So the
boom went on, and Vanvervecken continued to
make money. Almost there was a reasonable
chance that he might be able to haul down “The
Jolly Roger,” and sail the commercial seas once
more an honest merchantman.
Then the bolt fell from a blue sky.
Without a preparatory tremor, the boom
opened beneath the country’s feet, engulfing a
leading bank. Vanvervecken was leaving his
office at luncheon time when a pale-faced, wild-
eyed man grabbed him by the arm.
“ Have you heard ? ” he gasped ; “ we’ve put up
the shutters ! ”
It was the Chairman of Directors, a man whom
Vanvervecken had known for thirty years, and
the news of his failure meant that Vanvervecken’s
Nemesis had arrived. He stared calmly at the
man’s white, frightened face, then, “Damn you !”
he said, and turning on his heel walked on down
the street.
During that afternoon rumors of a hundred
rocking firms filled the city ; and by night the
well-informed, in some occult way, had arrived at
a glimmer of the truth about Vanvervecken. He
dined at the club, and astonished the waiter by
ordering champagne. In the smoking-room after
dinner it was obvious that he had been drinking.
He talked and laughed as he had not done for ten
years. He spoke of the bank’s failure, frankly
acknowledging that it had hit him for “a few
thou.,” and he gave an inimitable description of
the efforts, during the afternoon, of several
acquaintances to pump him as to his losses.
Finally he made an explanation. “I took an
oath ten years ago not to drink while I was in
business, and I’ve kept it. To-day I made up my
mind to retire, and directly I made up my mind I
went into the nearest bar and had a small bottle.
I think I deserve it. Yes, damn it, we’d better
have another.”
Six of them kept it going. Out of the six the
only one able to walk unassisted to his cab was
Vanvervecken. Afterwards it was computed that
he owed the other five just an even hundred thou-
sand among them.
At his house, before going to his room, he gave
his valet a sealed envelope. It contained prac-
tically t'ne last of his and several others’ fortunes
—a hundred pounds. It was the valet who found
him in the morning:, looking—dead—very much
as he had looked living.
For the moral of his life : The strength that can
break vices can also break banks; and virtue,
with an ulterior motive, is no more respectable
than vice, with an excuse.
SHIRLEY.
Uncle Willie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AS soon as Uncle Willie had gone out
of the front gate Elvira placed the baby
in the cradle, and turned severely to-
wards her mother.
Elvira was aged 20 years, and the baby was
seven weeks old. Between them were the ten
other children of Uncle Willie.
“Look here, mother,” she said, “I’m not a
child now, and I’m going to speak. If you are
going to have any more,” pointing disgustedly at
the baby, “you’ll look after them yourself. I’m
just about sick of babies. I wonder you aren’t
ashamed. Father’s got more than he can keep
now; What with you, and us twelve, and grannie,
and the three aunts, and now aunt Alma and her
brats.”
“ Elvira, how dare you ? ” screamed her mother,
her yellow face becoming mottled with rage.
“How can you understand. Wait until you are
married yourself.”
“Understand!” snorted Elvira. “ And—and
how likely am I to get married when I never
have any clothes fit to go out in ? ” And bursting
into tears, she went forth and banged the door.
Outside the gate Uncle Willie mounted his
squeaky bike and panted on his way, his shrivelled,
ginger-whiskered face purple with exertion. There
were three miles of hilly road from Uncle Willie’s
“farm” to his office in the little town. Every
morning, and every afternoon, his small shabby
figure would be seen pedalling painfully up the
steep hills, regardless of mid-day sun or scorching
wind.
“ A family man must keep in health, and there
is nothing like exercise,” he used to say.
Uncle Willie was a family man. Yes, certainly.
There were the twelve, mostly girls, his wife, his
mother, his three sisters, and recently “Aunt
Alma and her brats,” to whom Elvira had wrath-
fully referred.
When single Aunt Alma had earned a good
income as a dressmaker ; but, in order to live up
to the dizzy pinnacle of gentility to which the
had been raised, she now claimed not to know one
end of a needle from the other. When, after
ten years of shiftless married life, her husband
died, leaving her reduced to genteel penury,
she came to Uncle Willie. With his measureless
chivalry he would have considered it indelicate to
suggest that dressmaking was still a paying trade.
So Aunt Alma stayed, and her children went to
school with Uncle Willie’s. The other aunts
stayed too, and none of the girls were old enough
to marry, except Elvira who had no clothes.
So day by day Uncle Willie staggered on under
this crushing load of women, and day by day the
bike staggered under Uncle Willie as he battled
uphill to keep his health and win bread for twenty
mouths.
The crisis came with the failure of the potato
crop.
At the arrival of Aunt Alma, Uncle Willie had
gone to his office and made a calculation of the
too-well-known possibilities of his income. He
reckoned up liis salary as accountant to the local
gas-works, his earnings from his legitimate busi-
ness (a land-agency), his possible earnings from
his little farm, and he saw that the total did not
allow for Aunt Alma and her children. So he
planted more potatoes and trusted in the Lord.
But now the season had come, and potatoes had
failed, and as Uncle Willie panted uphill that
morning he realised that, for the first time in his
life, he was in debt.
But the courage of a lion was hidden under the
shrivelled ginger exterior of Uncle Willie. “As
long as I keep my health,” he said, putting on
extra speed and wincing at a sharper twinge of
his ever-present toothache.
On arriving at the township that day Uncle
Willie took himself to the dentist and explained
that he must have three teeth taken out.
“They interfere with my work,” he said, “and
I must work, and I must keep my health. I think
it is the toothache that is keeping me so poorly.”
“If you wish to take chloroform,” said the
dentist, “it will be necessary to have the doctor
to sound you and administer it.”
But with those twenty gaping mouths on his
conscience Uncle Willie dared not purchase com-
fort at the price of half-a-guinea, so he had them
out without an anaesthetic. Then, with the
scenery swimming round him, he went to his
office and tried to work. But it was no good.
“I’ll take the afternoon off, and the ride will
freshen me up,” murmured Uncle Willie; and,
getting on to his bike, he wobbled uncertainly
along the familiar road, which appeared to him
like a swirling stream beating him back.
“I’ll go home and rest,” he was going to
say ; but once more those twenty mouths gaped
in his mind’s eye, and he realised that there was
no rest there.
At the beginning of the Long Hill it appeared
to be getting very dark.
“ It’s getting late,” he said ; “I’ll go home and
—rest.”
Half-way up it was night, and something black
fell on Uncle Willie and suffocated him. He
stumbled off his bike and fainted.
* * * *
Grannie sat at the head of the table. On either
side of her were ranged, in long, tapering rows,
Alma and the three aunts, Alma’s girls, Elvira
and her ten sisters and brothers. Two large solid
pastry developments containing meat, smoked
before Grannie, and a pile of buttered toast swam
in grease at the other end of the table.
Elvira, poured out the strong tea impartially,
alike foi* Grannie and the dyspeptic babe of twelve
months which sat on her knee.
Suddenly Elvira screamed and dropped the
baby and the strong tea. Some men had come to
the door and they were carrying Uncle Willie on
a stretcher. He looked very small and pale, and
blood had trickled from his mouth and stained his
ginger beard.
The men turned to Elvira in explanation. “We
took ’im into the doctor and ’e sod ees ’eart ’ad
failed.”

LIVERPOOL. LONDON AND CHICAGO.
WILFLEY v. CARD CONCENTRATOR.
The judgment in the recent law suit does not apply to the original Card Concentrator, but only to two experimental
Table Tops that were made for the purpose of trial during the absence of Mr. Guthridge from Australia, and before
the working of the Table was properly understood. We are, therefore, continuing the manufacture and sale of these
machines, and the mining industry will not be deprived of this valuable invention. According to the sworn evidence,
the “ Card ” has 50 per cent. “ greater capacity and makes higher concentrates and poorer tailings than the ‘ Wilfley.’
With the ‘ Card ' there is practically no loss of lead (mineral), as there is with the ‘ Wilfley.’ ”
The “Card ” Table may be seen on application, when evidence of its great superiority will be furnished.
N. GUTHRIDGE, Ltd., Sole Agents, 17 BOND STREET, SYDNEY.
